Sanusi’s Doctrine on Karamat and Their Nearness to Prophetic Miracles
Sanusi affirms karamat and compares them to prophetic miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi’s affirmation of saintly miracles (karamat) and why he considers them nearly on par with prophetic miracles.

Core Idea
Karamat almost join the miracles of prophets; denial stems from lack of personal experience.

Sanusi begins by stating that his position on karamat aligns with the mainstream Sunni verifiers. He then quotes his own work, Sharh al-Aqida al-Wusta, where he makes a striking comparison: the miracles of saints are almost as manifest as those of prophets. He also explains that the denial of such miracles is expected from innovators, as they have never witnessed them.

Guided Path
1
Understand Sanusi's alignment with Ahl al-Sunna
Sanusi explicitly states that his view on karamat is that of the generality of verifiers from Ahl al-Sunna. This establishes orthodoxy.
2
Analyze the comparison between karamat and mu'jizat
Sanusi says karamat 'almost join' (takadu talhaq) prophetic miracles. Notice the careful language: not equal but nearly so.
This underscores the extraordinary nature of karamat.
3
Reflect on the reason for denial
Deniers are mainly innovators who lack personal experience; their denial is based on ignorance, not evidence.
Sanusi implies that witnessing leads to certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومذهب الإمام السنوسي في الكرامة ووقوعها مذهب عامة المحققين من أهل السنة والجماعة، وقد نثر الحديث عنها مختصراً تارة ومطولاً أخرى في سنوسياته المباركة، وله عبارة ذهبية نقدية لم تحجم قدرة الله تعالى ولم تقصر من شأن الكرامة في « شرح العقيدة الوسطى » إذ قال فيها: ( فكرامات الأولياء باعتبار ظهورها تكاد تلحق بمعجزات الأنبياء وإنكارها ليس بعجيب من أهل البدع والأهواء؛ إذ لم يشاهدوا ذلك من أنفسهم قطّ ( إلى أن قال: ( وإنما العجب من بعض فقهاء أهل السنة؛ حيث قال فيما روي عن إبراهيم بن أدهم أنهم رأوه بالبصرة يوم التروية وفي ذلك اليوم بمكة: إنَّ من اعتقد جواز ذلك يكفر !! )( ولك أن تتأمل كلمته المتواضعة المنكسرة حينما حكى شروط الولي التي ذكرها الإمام ابن دهاق؛ إذ قال: ( ونحن بالنسبة إلى هذا المقام مقامِ أولياء الله تعالى وخاصة حضرته .. على ساحل التمني، نغترف من بحر التوحيد والعرفان الذي خاضوا لججه، وغابوا فيه بقدر الإمكان، ونعترف لهم بأن ما هم فيه من درجة العيان أو ما يقرب منها . . فوق ما الكثير عليه من درجة البرهان )
And the doctrine of Imam al-Sanusi concerning karamat (miracles of saints) and their occurrence is the doctrine of the generality of the verifiers from Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, and he has scattered the discussion about it briefly at times and at length at other times in his blessed Sanusiyyat, and he has a golden, critical expression that does not diminish the power of Allah the Exalted nor belittle the matter of karamat in 'Sharh al-Aqida al-Wusta', where he said therein: 'Indeed, the karamat of the awliya (saints) in terms of their manifestation almost join the miracles of the prophets, and their denial is not strange from the people of innovation and desires, for they have not witnessed that from themselves at all' — until he said: 'And the wonder is only from some jurists of Ahl al-Sunna, where he said concerning what was narrated from Ibrahim ibn Adham that they saw him in Basra on the day of Tarwiyya and on that same day in Mecca: whoever believes that this is permissible becomes a disbeliever!!' And you may contemplate his humble, broken words when he recounted the conditions of the wali (saint) that Imam Ibn Dahaq mentioned, when he said: 'And we, in relation to this station — the station of the awliya of Allah the Exalted and the elite of His Presence — are on the shore of wish, we scoop from the sea of tawhid and gnosis whose depths they have plunged into and in which they have been lost to the extent possible, and we acknowledge that what they are in, of the degree of direct vision or what is close to it, is far above what most are upon of the degree of demonstration.'
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage establishes Imam Sanusi's position on karamat (miraculous events granted to saints). He aligns himself with the majority of Sunni scholars who affirm their reality. Sanusi notes that he has discussed this topic in his various works. He then quotes his own 'Sharh al-Aqida al-Wusta', where he makes two key points: first, the karamat of the awliya, in their outward manifestation, are nearly on par with the miracles of the prophets; second, denying karamat is to be expected from innovators (ahl al-bida') because they have no personal experience of such phenomena. He then turns his criticism toward certain jurists from Ahl al-Sunna who, based on a story about Ibrahim ibn Adham being in two places at once, declared that anyone who considers such an event possible becomes a disbeliever — which Sanusi finds astonishing. The passage then highlights Sanusi's humility by quoting his acknowledgment that in comparison to the station of the awliya, he and others are merely on the 'shore of wish', gathering knowledge from their ocean, and that the saints' level of direct vision (al-`ayan) is far above the level of rational proof (al-burhan) that most people possess. This shows Sanusi's deep respect for the awliya and his rejection of hasty takfir.

Key Terms
الكرامة
معجزات الأنبياء
أهل البدع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Sanusi equated karamat to prophetic miracles.
Correction: He said 'almost join', implying nearness but not equality.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is the denial of karamat not surprising from innovators?
A) Because they are ignorant of theology
B) Because they have not witnessed such events
C) Because they follow their desires
D) Because they reject the Quran

Key Takeaway
Sanusi affirms karamat as real and nearly matching prophetic miracles, and attributes their denial to lack of personal experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted a reported miracle from the unseen simply because you never experienced it? What does Sanusi's explanation reveal about the source of such doubts in your own heart?
?
If you have not witnessed a karamah, do you treat the possibility of their occurrence with faith or skepticism? How can you cultivate certainty in what you have not seen?
?
Sanusi says that denial of karamat comes from innovators because they never saw it in themselves. Is there any aspect of your belief that you hold only because you were taught it, not because you have internalized it through spiritual experience? How can you move from hearsay to conviction?

Sanusi’s Critique of Hasty Takfir Regarding Saintly Miracles
Sanusi criticizes the takfir of those who accept saintly bilocation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi criticizes a Sunni jurist who declared takfir on those who believe in the possibility of saintly miracles like Ibrahim ibn Adham's bilocation.

Core Idea
Declaring disbelief over the possibility of saintly miracles is an excessive and unwarranted act.

After affirming karamat, Sanusi turns to criticize a specific incident where some Sunni jurists declared that believing in the possibility of events like Ibrahim ibn Adham being in two places at once constitutes disbelief. Sanusi finds this astonishing, implying that such takfir is hasty and unbefitting of true scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Understand the incident
Sanusi references a story about Ibrahim ibn Adham being seen in Basra and Mecca on the same day. Some jurists said that believing this is possible is kufr.
This is a specific case of a saintly miracle (bilocation).
2
Analyze Sanusi’s reaction
Sanusi calls this 'the wonder' (al-ajab), expressing amazement that Sunni jurists would resort to takfir over a matter of possibility.
Sanusi himself holds that such events are possible and are karamat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومذهب الإمام السنوسي في الكرامة ووقوعها مذهب عامة المحققين من أهل السنة والجماعة، وقد نثر الحديث عنها مختصراً تارة ومطولاً أخرى في سنوسياته المباركة، وله عبارة ذهبية نقدية لم تحجم قدرة الله تعالى ولم تقصر من شأن الكرامة في « شرح العقيدة الوسطى » إذ قال فيها: ( فكرامات الأولياء باعتبار ظهورها تكاد تلحق بمعجزات الأنبياء وإنكارها ليس بعجيب من أهل البدع والأهواء؛ إذ لم يشاهدوا ذلك من أنفسهم قطّ ( إلى أن قال: ( وإنما العجب من بعض فقهاء أهل السنة؛ حيث قال فيما روي عن إبراهيم بن أدهم أنهم رأوه بالبصرة يوم التروية وفي ذلك اليوم بمكة: إنَّ من اعتقد جواز ذلك يكفر !! )( ولك أن تتأمل كلمته المتواضعة المنكسرة حينما حكى شروط الولي التي ذكرها الإمام ابن دهاق؛ إذ قال: ( ونحن بالنسبة إلى هذا المقام مقامِ أولياء الله تعالى وخاصة حضرته .. على ساحل التمني، نغترف من بحر التوحيد والعرفان الذي خاضوا لججه، وغابوا فيه بقدر الإمكان، ونعترف لهم بأن ما هم فيه من درجة العيان أو ما يقرب منها . . فوق ما الكثير عليه من درجة البرهان )
And the doctrine of Imam al-Sanusi concerning karamat (miracles of saints) and their occurrence is the doctrine of the generality of the verifiers from Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, and he has scattered the discussion about it briefly at times and at length at other times in his blessed Sanusiyyat, and he has a golden, critical expression that does not diminish the power of Allah the Exalted nor belittle the matter of karamat in 'Sharh al-Aqida al-Wusta', where he said therein: 'Indeed, the karamat of the awliya (saints) in terms of their manifestation almost join the miracles of the prophets, and their denial is not strange from the people of innovation and desires, for they have not witnessed that from themselves at all' — until he said: 'And the wonder is only from some jurists of Ahl al-Sunna, where he said concerning what was narrated from Ibrahim ibn Adham that they saw him in Basra on the day of Tarwiyya and on that same day in Mecca: whoever believes that this is permissible becomes a disbeliever!!' What does Sanusi express astonishment at?
A) The story of Ibrahim ibn Adham
B) The fact that some jurists declared takfir on those who believe in the possibility
C) The denial of karamat by innovators
D) The humility of saints

Key Takeaway
Sanusi warns against hasty takfir, especially regarding saintly miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever quickly dismissed someone's faith because they held a view you considered strange or extreme? How does Sanusi's warning against hasty takfir expose the arrogance in your heart, and will you commit to verifying before condemning?
?
When you hear a miraculous account like Ibrahim ibn Adham's bilocation, does your first reaction reveal more trust in Allah's absolute power or in your own limited understanding? What does your reaction say about your reliance on the unseen?
?
Think of a religious issue where you are certain you are right. If a respected scholar disagreed, would you be tempted to label their belief as disbelief? Sanusi's critique of the jurist shows how easily one can slide into takfir. Are you willing to hold your tongue and assume good faith?

Sanusi’s Humility Before the Station of the Awliya
Sanusi humbly acknowledges the saints' superior spiritual state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will appreciate Sanusi’s humility in acknowledging the superior spiritual station of the awliya and his own limitations.

Core Idea
The saints attain direct vision; we are only on the shore of wish, gathering from their ocean.

Sanusi concludes by quoting himself from a discussion of the conditions of sainthood. He humbly admits that compared to the awliya, he and most people are merely on the 'shore of wish', scooping from the ocean of tawhid. He recognizes that the saints' level of direct vision (al-ayan) is far above the level of rational proof (al-burhan) that most possess.

Guided Path
1
Understand the metaphor of the shore and the sea
Sanusi uses the imagery of being on the shore of wish (sahil al-tamanni) while the saints plunge into the depths of tawhid.
This illustrates his humility and respect.
2
Contrast between al-ayan and al-burhan
Sanusi contrasts the saints' direct vision (al-ayan) with the demonstrative knowledge (al-burhan) that most people have. He admits the saints are superior.
This shows Sanusi's epistemological humility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومذهب الإمام السنوسي في الكرامة ووقوعها مذهب عامة المحققين من أهل السنة والجماعة، وقد نثر الحديث عنها مختصراً تارة ومطولاً أخرى في سنوسياته المباركة، وله عبارة ذهبية نقدية لم تحجم قدرة الله تعالى ولم تقصر من شأن الكرامة في « شرح العقيدة الوسطى » إذ قال فيها: ( فكرامات الأولياء باعتبار ظهورها تكاد تلحق بمعجزات الأنبياء وإنكارها ليس بعجيب من أهل البدع والأهواء؛ إذ لم يشاهدوا ذلك من أنفسهم قطّ ( إلى أن قال: ( وإنما العجب من بعض فقهاء أهل السنة؛ حيث قال فيما روي عن إبراهيم بن أدهم أنهم رأوه بالبصرة يوم التروية وفي ذلك اليوم بمكة: إنَّ من اعتقد جواز ذلك يكفر !! )( ولك أن تتأمل كلمته المتواضعة المنكسرة حينما حكى شروط الولي التي ذكرها الإمام ابن دهاق؛ إذ قال: ( ونحن بالنسبة إلى هذا المقام مقامِ أولياء الله تعالى وخاصة حضرته .. على ساحل التمني، نغترف من بحر التوحيد والعرفان الذي خاضوا لججه، وغابوا فيه بقدر الإمكان، ونعترف لهم بأن ما هم فيه من درجة العيان أو ما يقرب منها . . فوق ما الكثير عليه من درجة البرهان )

What does Sanusi say the saints have that most people do not?
A) More knowledge

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi places himself on the shore of wish while others dive into the sea. Where do you truly stand—are you content with scooping from what others have dived into, or have you ever yearned to plunge into the depth yourself? What is holding you back?
?
When you reflect on your own faith, do you mistake intellectual certainty for direct vision? How would your worship change if you admitted that your current knowledge of Allah is merely demonstrative, not yet witnessed?
?
Sanusi calls his own words 'humble and broken.' When was the last time you felt genuinely broken before the station of the awliya? Does your heart ache with longing to reach their state, or have you become comfortable on the shore?

Classification of Rational Proof and Definition of Burhān
Sanusi classifies rational proofs into five types and defines burhān as composed of yaqīniyyāt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five types of rational proof and why only burhān yields certainty through its certain premises.

Core Idea
Burhān is the only rational proof that delivers certainty, built entirely from certain premises.

Before diving into theological arguments, Sanusi establishes a crucial logical framework: not all rational proofs are equal. He classifies proofs by their material (premises) and distinguishes burhān as the only demonstrative proof composed entirely of certain premises. This lesson introduces that classification and the beginning of the types of certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage cuts off mid-sentence; the full classification continues in the next lesson.
2
Identify the five types of rational proof
The passage lists five types: burhān, jadal, khaṭābah, shiʿr, and mughālaṭah. Note that only burhān is described as 'decisive' and is composed of certain premises.
3
Understand burhān's unique status
Burhān is defined as a proof whose premises are all certain (yaqīniyyāt). This is what sets it apart from the other four types, which may use probabilities, accepted opinions, or even false premises.
The certain premises themselves are six types; the passage begins listing them with awwaliyyāt (first principles).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الحجة العقلية
قوله: (من البراهين القاطعة) بيان لما وقعت عليه (ما)، والبراهين: جمع برهان، وهو أحد أقسام الحجة العقلية؛ لأن الحجة تنقسم أولا بحسب مادتها قسمين: عقلية ونقلية، والأولى خمسة أقسام: برهان، وجدل، وخطابة، وشعر، ومغالطة. فالبرهان: ما تركيب من مقدمات كلها يقينية، واليقينيات ستة أقسام: - أوليات: لأنها تدرك بأول توجه العقل، وتسمى أيضا: بديهيات؛ وهي ما يجزم به العقل بمجرد تصور طرفيه؛ كقولنا:
Divisions of the rational proof. His saying: 'from decisive proofs (al-barāhīn al-qāṭiʿah)' is an explanation of what the relative pronoun 'mā' attaches to. Al-barāhīn is the plural of burhān, and it is one of the divisions of rational proof; because proof (al-ḥujjah) is first divided according to its matter (māddah) into two types: rational and transmitted. The first is divided into five types: burhān (demonstration), jadal (dialectic), khaṭābah (rhetoric), shiʿr (poetry), and mughālaṭah (sophistry). Burhān is that which is composed of premises, all of which are certain. The certain premises (al-yaqīniyyāt) are six types: – First principles (awwaliyyāt); because they are perceived at the first direction of the intellect, and they are also called badīhiyyāt (self-evident truths); they are that which the intellect affirms categorically merely by conceiving its two terms, like our saying:
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the divisions of rational proof (aqsām al-ḥujjah al-ʿaqliyyah). Sanusi explains the phrase 'decisive proofs' (al-barāhīn al-qāṭiʿah) from his matn. He notes that al-barāhīn is the plural of burhān, which is one division of rational proof. Proof (al-ḥujjah) is first divided by its matter (māddah) into two: rational (ʿaqliyyah) and transmitted (naqliyyah). Rational proof then has five subtypes: burhān (demonstration), jadal (dialectic), khaṭābah (rhetoric), shiʿr (poetry), and mughālaṭah (sophistry). Burhān is defined as that which is composed of premises that are all certain (yaqīniyyah). The certain premises themselves are six types. He begins to list them, starting with awwaliyyāt (first principles), also called badīhiyyāt (self-evident truths), which are known immediately by the intellect upon merely conceiving the terms, such as 'the whole is greater than the part.' The text cuts off at the example, but the classification is clear.

Key Terms
برهان
جدل
خطابة
شعر
مغالطة
يقينيات
أوليات
بديهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All rational proofs (jadal, khaṭābah, etc.) produce certainty.
Correction: Only burhān produces certainty because its premises are all certain. The other types may yield probability, persuasion, or even falsehood.

Checkpoint
How many types of rational proof does Sanusi list?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
Rational proofs are of five types, but only burhān, composed of certain premises, yields certain knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you build your most important beliefs—especially about Allah—on certain proofs (burhān) or on arguments that might be merely rhetorical or dialectical? Identify one belief you hold and honestly assess the certainty of its foundation.
?
When you hear a religious argument, do you pause to examine whether its premises are self-evident or only plausible? Commit to testing your own reasoning against the standard of certainty taught in this lesson, starting with one issue you are currently unsure about.
?
The author lists self-evident truths (awwaliyyāt) as the first type of certain premises. What self-evident truth about your own accountability to Allah have you been avoiding? How will you act on it today before you meet Him?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
5 min
Classification of Rational Proof and the Definition of Demonstration
Sanusi classifies rational proof and defines demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five types of rational proof and the defining characteristic of demonstration (burhān).

Core Idea
Rational proofs are classified by their premises; only demonstration uses fully certain premises.

In this passage, Sanusi begins by explaining the term 'decisive proofs' from the main text. He classifies proof into rational and transmitted, then divides rational proof into five types. He then defines demonstration as that composed entirely of certain premises, setting the stage for the next lesson on the six types of certain premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of proof
Read how Sanusi first divides proof into rational and transmitted, then lists five rational types: demonstration, dialectic, rhetoric, poetry, and sophistry.
The classification is based on the material (mādda) of the premises.
2
Identify the definition of demonstration
Find the definition: demonstration is composed entirely of certain premises. Note that this is what makes it decisive.
3
Reflect on the hierarchy
Consider why only demonstration is suitable for certainty. The other types use less certain premises and are not decisive proofs.
This highlights the importance of yaqīniyyāt (certain premises).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( مِنَ البراهين القاطعةِ ( بيانُ لِمَا وقعَتْ عليه ( ما ) ، والبراهين : جمعُ برهان ، وهو أحد أقسامِ الحُجَّةِ العقليَّةِ ؛ لأنَّ الحُجَّةَ تنقسم أولاً بحسب مادَّتِها قسمين (۳) : عقليَّةٌ ونقليَّةٌ ، والأولى خمسة أقسام : برهان ، وجدل ، وخطابةٌ ، وشعر ، ومغالطة. فالبرهان : ما تَركَّبَ مِنْ مُقدِّماتٍ كُلُّها يقينية ، واليقينيات ستة أقسام : - أوليات : لأنَّها تُدرَكُ بأوَّلِ توجُهِ العقل (٤) ، وتُسمَّى أيضاً : بَدِيهِيَّاتٍ ؛ وهي ما يجزمُ بهِ العقلُ بمُجرَّدِ تصورِ طرفيهِ ؛ كقولنا : الواحد نصفُ الاثنين ، والكل أعظمُ مِنْ جزئه . - ومشاهدات : وتُسمَّى أيضاً : حِسَّيَّاتٍ ؛ وهي ما يجزمُ بهِ العقل بواسطة حسّ ؛ كقولنا : الشمس مشرقة ، والنار محرقة. - وقضايا قياساتها معها : وهي ما يجزمُ بهِ العقلُ بوسط يَتصوَّرُهُ معها (۱) ؛ كقولنا : الأربعةُ زوج ؛ فإنَّه بسبب وسط حاضر في الذهن ؛ وهو الانقسام بمتساويين (۲) . - وتجريبيَّات : وهي ما يجزمُ بهِ العقل بواسطة ترتبه مراراً كثيرة (۳) ؛ بحيثُ يجزمُ العقلُ بأنَّهُ ليسَ على سبيل الاتفاق ؛ كقولنا : السَّقَمُونِيا تُسهل الصفراء . - وحدسيات : وهي ما يجزم به العقل لترتب دون ترتب التجريبيَّاتِ معَ مصاحبةِ القرائنِ ؛ كقولنا : نور القمرِ مستفاد مِنْ نورِ الشمس . - ومتواتراتٌ : وهي ما يجزمُ بهِ العقلُ بواسطة حِسّ السمع ووسط حاضر في الذهنِ ؛ وذلكَ أَنْ يُخبِرَ عن محسوس يمكنُ وقوعه جمع كثير يجزم العقلُ بامتناع تواطئهم على الكذب ؛ كقولنا : محمد
His saying: 'from decisive proofs' is an explanation of what the 'ma' refers to. 'Proofs' (barāhīn) is the plural of 'proof' (burhān), and it is one of the divisions of rational proof. Because the proof (ḥujja) is first divided according to its matter into two divisions (3): rational and transmitted. The first (rational) has five divisions: demonstration (burhān), dialectic (jadal), rhetoric (khiṭāba), poetry (shiʿr), and sophistry (mughālaṭa). Demonstration is that which is composed of premises all of which are certain (yaqīniyya). The certain premises are six divisions: - Primaries (awwaliyyāt): because they are perceived at the first turn of the mind (4), and are also called self-evidents (badīhiyyāt); they are what the mind judges by merely conceiving its two extremes; like our saying: 'one is half of two', and 'the whole is greater than its part'. - And observeds (mushāhadāt): also called sensibles (ḥissiyyāt); they are what the mind judges by means of a sense; like our saying: 'the sun is shining', and 'fire is burning'. - And propositions whose syllogisms are with them (qaḍāyā qiyāsātuhā maʿahā): they are what the mind judges by a middle term it conceives along with them (1); like our saying: 'four is even'; because of a middle term present in the mind, which is divisibility into two equal parts (2). - And empirics (tajribiyyāt): they are what the mind judges by means of its repeated occurrence many times (3), such that the mind judges that it is not by coincidence; like our saying: 'scammony purges bile'. - And intuitives (ḥadsiyyāt): they are what the mind judges by a succession less than that of empirics, with accompanying indications; like our saying: 'the light of the moon is derived from the light of the sun'. - And mutawātirāt (recurrent reports): they are what the mind judges by means of the sense of hearing and a middle term present in the mind; that is, a large group reports about a sensible that can occur, such that the mind judges the impossibility of their colluding on falsehood; like our saying: 'Muhammad...'
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage explains the phrase 'from decisive proofs' found in the main text. It first clarifies that 'proofs' (barāhīn) is the plural of 'proof' (burhān) and that it is a type of rational proof (al-ḥujja al-ʿaqliyya). The author then classifies proof (ḥujja) first according to its matter (mādda) into two: rational and transmitted. The rational type is further subdivided into five: demonstration, dialectic, rhetoric, poetry, and sophistry. Demonstration (burhān) is specifically defined as that which is composed entirely of certain premises (yaqīniyyāt). The author then enumerates six types of certain premises: 1. Primaries (awwaliyyāt) or self-evidents (badīhiyyāt): judgments the mind makes immediately upon understanding the terms, e.g., 'one is half of two'. 2. Observeds (mushāhadāt) or sensibles (ḥissiyyāt): judgments based on sensory perception, e.g., 'the sun is shining'. 3. Propositions whose syllogisms are with them (qaḍāyā qiyāsātuhā maʿahā): judgments requiring a middle term that is immediately present with the proposition, e.g., 'four is even' because it is divisible into two equal parts. 4. Empirics (tajribiyyāt): judgments derived from repeated observation that eliminate coincidence, e.g., 'scammony purges bile'. 5. Intuitives (ḥadsiyyāt): judgments based on a less regular but strongly indicated succession, e.g., 'the light of the moon is from the sun'. 6. Mutawātirāt (recurrent reports): judgments based on a large number of reporters whose agreement on a falsehood is impossible, e.g., the report that Muhammad (peace be upon him) claimed prophethood (the example is cut off). Each type is defined and exemplified. The passage is foundational for understanding the epistemology of kalam.

الحجة العقلية

خطابة
شعر
مغالطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All rational proofs are equally valid for establishing truth.
Correction: Only demonstration (burhān) is composed of certain premises and yields certainty; the other types do not.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, which of the following is NOT one of the five types of rational proof?
A) Burhān
B) Jadal
C) Khiṭāba
D) Qiyās

Key Takeaway
Demonstration is the only rational proof that relies entirely on certain premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your pursuit of religious knowledge, do you seek the certainty of demonstration, or do you often rely on arguments that are merely persuasive or rhetorical? What does that say about the foundation of your beliefs?
?
Sanusi teaches that only demonstration is built from certain premises. Examine the grounds of your most important conviction about God. Are those grounds certain? If not, will you seek a demonstration until you find certainty?
?
You have likely heard or used dialectical arguments to defend your faith. But after this lesson, are you content with such arguments, or will you strive to base your faith on premises that are undeniably certain? What would that require of you?

The Six Types of Certain Premises (al-Yaqiniyyat)
Sanusi enumerates and defines the six types of certain premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six types of certain premises that form the basis of demonstrative proof in kalam.

Core Idea
Certain premises are of six kinds, each defined by how the mind attains certainty.

In this passage, Sanusi enumerates the six types of certain premises that can be used in demonstration. He defines each type and provides examples. Understanding these categories is essential for evaluating the strength of arguments in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of six types
Read the entire enumeration to get an overview of the six categories: primaries, observeds, propositions with inherent syllogisms, empirics, intuitives, and recurrent reports.
2
Examine definitions and examples
For each type, note how the mind attains certainty: first turn of mind, sense, middle term, repetition, less repetition with indications, and widespread testimony.
The examples help clarify each type.
3
Distinguish similar types
Compare empirics and intuitives: both involve observation, but empirics require many repetitions, intuitives fewer with strong indications.
4
Reflect on theological significance
Consider why these categories are important for kalam: they show what counts as certain and how arguments can be built on reliable foundations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Empirical and intuitive premises are the same.
Correction: Empirical premises are based on many repeated observations that rule out coincidence, while intuitive premises are based on fewer repetitions with strong accompanying indications.

Checkpoint

A) Primary
B) Observational
C) Propositions whose syllogisms are with them
D) Empirical

Key Takeaway
The six types of certain premises—primaries, observeds, propositions with inherent syllogisms, empirics, intuitives, and recurrent reports—provide the foundation for demonstrative arguments in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these six types of certainty do you most rely on when affirming your belief in Allah? Is that reliance justified, or is it merely inherited assumption?
?

?
You just learned that empirics require repeated observation to eliminate coincidence. Where in your daily life do you assume certainty without sufficient repetition? What would change if you demanded more rigorous evidence for your assumptions?

The Composition and Purpose of Demonstration
Sanusi states the composition and purpose of demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that demonstration (burhan) is composed of six divisions and aims to produce certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Demonstration's purpose is certain knowledge, built from six divisions.

In this concluding passage, Imam al-Sanusi summarizes the structure of demonstration by stating that it is composed of six divisions. He also clarifies its ultimate goal: the attainment of certain knowledge (al-ilm al-yaqini). This short but dense statement ties together the previous discussion and sets the stage for subsequent topics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of the example of the Prophet's claim and miracles.
2
Identify the two key claims
The passage makes two main claims: (1) the six divisions compose demonstration, and (2) the purpose is certain knowledge. Find these in the text.
3
Reflect on why certain knowledge matters
Ask yourself why the goal of demonstration is 'certain knowledge' rather than just persuasion. This connects to the high standards of Islamic theology.
Certain knowledge is the firmest level of belief.
4
Connect to the broader logic of kalam
Consider how this passage fits into the overall discussion of argumentation in kalam. Why is it important to define the composition and goal of demonstration?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رسولُ اللهِ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ادَّعى النبوَّةَ ، وظهرَتِ المعجزات على يديه ، وهذا القسمُ مُركَّبٌ مِنَ القسم الثاني والثالث. فهذه الأقسام السنةُ منها يتركَّبُ البرهانُ . والغرض منه : حصول العلم اليقيني .
The Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, claimed prophethood, and miracles appeared at his hands, and this section is composed of the second and third divisions. So these six divisions constitute the demonstration. Its purpose is the attainment of certain knowledge.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion of demonstration (burhan) by stating its composition and purpose. The author notes that the example of the prophet's claim and miracles is a compound of two earlier divisions (likely the second and third of six types of premises). He then affirms that the six divisions together form the demonstration. The purpose of demonstration is to produce certain knowledge (al-ilm al-yaqini), the highest level of certainty. The passage is transitional, as the author next turns to other types of argument.

علم يقيني

Key Takeaway
The purpose of demonstration (burhan) is to produce certain knowledge (al-ilm al-yaqini).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever honestly examined the evidence for Muhammad’s prophethood with the intention of attaining certain knowledge, or have you simply accepted it because it was taught to you? If your faith rests on imitation, what would it take for you to make it certain through demonstration?
?
The purpose of demonstration is certain knowledge. Is your faith certain? If not, are you actively seeking that certainty through rational inquiry, or are you avoiding it because you fear the outcome?
?
Sanusi says demonstration is composed of six divisions of premises. Have you ever studied the logical structure of a single Islamic proof? If not, why do you neglect the very method that yields certainty in matters of the highest importance?

Definition and Purpose of Dialectic
Defines dialectic as composed of accepted premises and states its purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of dialectic (jadal) as composed of widely accepted premises and its twofold purpose.

Core Idea
Dialectic uses widely accepted premises to persuade or compel.

In this passage, Imam al-Sanusi introduces the concept of dialectic (jadal), a type of argument that relies on premises accepted by the public. Unlike strict demonstration, dialectic serves a practical role in debate and persuasion. Let's break down its definition, examples, and purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall meaning of the definition and purpose.
2
Understand 'widely accepted premises'
Notice that these premises are what the public agrees on for reasons like general benefit, decency, or zeal.
These are not demonstrative certainties but socially accepted truths.
3
Examine the examples
The author gives four examples. See how each follows a pattern: a particular case, a general principle, then a conclusion.
Examples include injustice, indecency, charity, and retaliation.
4
Learn the purpose
Dialectic is for persuading the incapable of demonstration or for compelling an opponent in debate.
This contrasts with demonstration which aims at certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجدلُ : فهو ما تأَلَّفَ مِنْ مُقدِّمات مشهورة ، والمُقدِّماتُ المشهورةُ : ما اعترف به الجمهور لمصلحة عامة أو لسبب رفة أو حميَّة ؛ كقولنا : هذا ظلم ، وكلُّ ظلم قبيح ؛ فهذا قبيح. وهذا كاشف العورتِهِ ، وكلُّ كاشف لعورته فهو مذموم ؛ فهذا مذموم . وهذا فقير ، وكلُّ فقير تُحمَدُ مواساته ؛ فهذا تُحمَدُ مواساته وهذا قُتِلَ أخوه ، وكلُّ مَنْ قُتِلَ أخوهُ حَسُنَ أَنْ يقتل قاتلَهُ ؛ فهذا حسُنَ أن يقتل قاتله. والغرضُ مِنَ الجدلِ : إمَّا إقناع قاصر عن البرهان ، أو إلزام الخصم ودفعه .
As for dialectic (jadal): it is that which is composed of widely accepted premises. The widely accepted premises are what the public acknowledges for a general benefit, or due to a reason of chastity/refinement or zeal; such as our saying: This is injustice, and every injustice is evil; so this is evil. And this is one who exposes his private parts, and everyone who exposes his private parts is blameworthy; so this is blameworthy. And this is poor, and every poor person is praised for sharing with him; so sharing with him is praised. And this one’s brother was killed, and everyone whose brother was killed, it is good that he kills his killer; so it is good that he kills his killer. The purpose of dialectic is either to persuade one who is incapable of demonstration, or to compel an opponent and repulse him.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage defines dialectic (jadal) as an argument composed of 'famous premises' (muqaddimat mashhura). These are premises that are commonly accepted by the public, based on considerations of general welfare, moral decency, or tribal zeal. The author gives four examples: (1) Injustice is evil because everyone agrees injustice is evil. (2) Exposing one’s private parts is blameworthy. (3) The poor deserve assistance. (4) It is good for a man to kill his brother’s murderer (referring to the right of retaliation). The purpose of dialectic is twofold: either to persuade someone who cannot grasp a full demonstration, or to silence and refute an opponent in debate. Dialectic is thus a practical tool for argumentation, not aimed at certainty like demonstration.

Key Terms
جدل
مقدّمات مشهورة
إقناع
إلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dialectic is inferior to demonstration and has no real value.
Correction: Dialectic serves a different purpose: it persuades those who cannot follow strict demonstrations and silences opponents. It is a practical tool, not a failed form of demonstration.

Checkpoint
What are the two possible purposes of dialectic (jadal) according to the author?
A) To attain certainty and to entertain
B) To persuade the incapable and to compel the opponent
C) To praise and to blame
D) To demonstrate truth and to deceive

Key Takeaway
Dialectic is a form of argument built on widely accepted premises, used to persuade or compel rather than to demonstrate certain truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage gives an example that killing the murderer of one's brother is 'good' because it is widely accepted. If you believe that, is it because you have thought it through, or because society says so? If you don't believe it, how do you argue with those who do without rejecting them?
?
When you argue with someone, are you more interested in persuading them or in compelling them to submit? Which approach does your heart prefer, and what does that reveal about your attachment to truth?

Rhetoric: Accepted and Probable Premises
Defines rhetoric and its two premise types with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of rhetoric in Islamic logic, the two types of premises it uses, and its purpose of persuasion.

Core Idea
Rhetoric persuades using accepted or probable premises, not certain ones.

In this passage, Imam Sanusi defines rhetoric (khataba) as a form of argument distinct from demonstration and dialectic. He explains that rhetorical arguments are built on two kinds of premises: those accepted from a trustworthy person and those that are merely probable. This lesson unpacks that definition and shows how rhetoric aims to motivate an audience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the example given for probable premises.
2
Identify the two types of premises
The passage lists two categories of premises used in rhetoric: (1) premises accepted from a person believed to be truthful, due to a secret or a beautiful quality; (2) probable premises like the example of the night wanderer. Understand each type.
The first type relies on trust in authority; the second on common likelihood.
3
Understand the purpose of rhetoric
Sanusi states the purpose is 'targhib al-sami'in' — to motivate the listeners. Reflect on how rhetoric differs from demonstration (which aims at certainty).
Rhetoric is persuasive but not conclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخطابةُ : فهي ما تأَلَّفَ مِنْ مُقدِّماتٍ مقبولةٍ مِنْ شخص معتقد فيهِ الصدقُ لسرّ لا يُطَّلع عليه ، أو لصفة جميلة ؛ كزيادة علم أو زهد ونحوه ، أو مِنْ مُقدِّماتٍ مظنونةٍ ؛ مثلُ : هذا يدور في الليل بالسلاح ، وكلُّ مَنْ يدورُ في الليل بالسلاح فهو لص ؛ فهذا لص . والغرضُ مِنَ الخطابة : ترغيب السامعين
As for rhetoric (khataba): it is that which is composed of premises accepted from a person believed to be truthful, due to a secret not known, or for a beautiful quality; such as increased knowledge, asceticism, and the like; or of probable premises; such as: This man walks at night with a weapon, and everyone who walks at night with a weapon is a thief; so this is a thief. The purpose of rhetoric is to motivate the listeners.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage defines rhetoric (khataba) as an argument built on two types of premises: (1) Premises accepted from a trustworthy authority, either for an unknown reason (like trust in a saint) or for a praiseworthy quality (like knowledge or piety). (2) Probable premises (muqaddimat maznuna) based on common likelihood, such as the example: a person walking armed at night is probably a thief. The purpose of rhetoric is to persuade and motivate the audience (targhib al-sami'in), not to establish certainty. Rhetoric is a step down from dialectic in epistemic strength, but effective for public persuasion.

Key Terms
خطابة
مقدّمات مقبولة
مقدّمات مظنونة
ترغيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rhetoric is merely emotional speech without structure.
Correction: Rhetoric in Islamic logic is a structured argument using accepted or probable premises, not just emotional appeal.

Checkpoint

A) Certain and doubtful
B) Widely accepted and demonstrative
C) Accepted from a trusted person and probable
D) Universal and particular

Key Takeaway
Rhetoric is a persuasive argument built on accepted or probable premises, aiming to motivate rather than prove certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Understanding Poetry as Imaginative Argument in Islamic Logic
Sanusi defines poetry and illustrates its two types with examples, stating its emotional purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical definition of poetry as a form of argument based on imaginative premises, its two types (attraction and repulsion), and its purpose of producing emotional response.

Core Idea
Poetry in logic is not about rhyme but about using fictional premises to stir the soul.

In Islamic logic, poetry is classified as a type of argument, but it is unique because its premises are not meant to be true. Instead, poetry uses imaginative descriptions to influence the listener's emotions. This lesson explores Sanusi's definition of poetry, its examples, and its ultimate purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic definition carefully: poetry is composed of imagined premises (مقدمات متخيلة) to attract or repel the soul (ترغيب أو تنفير). Note that the premises are not factual but imaginative.
The word 'مُتخيّلة' is key – it means imaginary, not real.
2
Examine the examples
Look at the two examples: one for attraction (wine described as a flowing ruby) and one for repulsion (honey described as vomited bile). Notice how the imagined premise affects the soul's reaction.
These are obviously false descriptions; they are meant to evoke emotion, not to state facts.
3
Grasp the purpose
Sanusi states that the purpose of poetry is the emotion of the soul (انفعال النفس). Realize that poetry is not intended to produce knowledge or compel belief, only to stir feelings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا الشعرُ : فهو ما تأَلَّفَ مِنْ مُقدِّماتٍ مُتخيَّلة لترغيب النفس في شيء أو تنفيرها عنه : فالأول : كقولنا : هذهِ خمرةٌ ، وكلُّ خمرة ياقوتة سيَّالةٌ ؛ فهذهِ ياقوتة سيالة. والثاني : كقولنا : هذا عسل ، وكلُّ عسلٍ مِرَّةٌ مُتَهَوَّعَةٌ (١) ، فهذه مِرَّةٌ مُتهوعةٌ . والغرضُ مِنَ الشعرِ : انفعالُ النفس .
As for poetry (shi‘r): it is that which is composed of imagined premises to induce the soul to desire something or to repel it from something. The first: such as our saying: This is wine, and every wine is a flowing ruby; so this is a flowing ruby. The second: such as our saying: This is honey, and every honey is a vomited bile; so this is a vomited bile. The purpose of poetry is the emotion of the soul.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage defines poetry (shi‘r) as an argument constructed from imaginary premises (muqaddimat mutakhayyila). These premises are not meant to be taken as true; they are fictional and designed to affect the listener's emotions. The author gives two examples: one to attract (wine described as a flowing ruby) and one to repel (honey described as vomited bile). The purpose of poetry is solely to produce an emotional response (infi‘al al-nafs) in the soul, not to convince or prove. Poetry is thus the weakest form of argument in terms of epistemic value, but it has rhetorical and aesthetic uses.

Checkpoint

B) To compel an opponent
C) To produce emotional response in the soul

Key Takeaway
Poetry is a logical argument using fictional premises to evoke emotional response, not to establish truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson teaches that poetry's purpose is to stir emotion, not to convey truth. How often do you let your emotions be governed by fictional narratives without checking their foundation? Will you commit to examining the arguments that move you?
?
Consider the example of wine being called a 'flowing ruby' to make it desirable. This is an illusion. In your daily life, what 'flowing rubies'—things made to seem attractive through false imagery—capture your desire? Are you willing to strip away the poetic language and see the reality, even if it is unpleasant?
?
The purpose of poetry is 'the emotion of the soul.' Your soul is being constantly acted upon by words and images. Are you the passive recipient, or do you actively choose what to let affect you? This lesson calls you to be vigilant: your emotional responses are not neutral; they shape your actions. Will you take responsibility for what you allow to move your soul?

Definition and Types of Fallacy (Mughalata)
Defines fallacy and enumerates its three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of fallacy (mughalata) and be able to distinguish its three types: sophistry, contentiousness, and illusion.

Core Idea
Fallacies are arguments that mimic truth but are false, and they come in three deceptive forms.

Fallacies are deceptive arguments that appear true but are not. The great scholar Sanusi defines fallacy and breaks it down into three categories based on the kind of false premises they use. Understanding these types helps us avoid being misled in reasoning. Let's explore his precise definition and examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Sanusi defines fallacy before listing its types.
The passage is concise; each type includes an example.
2
Identify the three types
Find each type in the passage: sophistry (safsata), contentiousness (mushaghaba), and illusion (wahmiyya). Note the kind of premises each uses.
The first uses premises that look like truth; the second uses premises resembling widely accepted ones; the third uses false illusory premises.
3
Analyze the examples
For each example, determine why the argument fails. Which premise is false? How does it resemble a valid argument?
The horse picture example: the minor premise 'this is a horse' is false. The scholar example: the major premise is not universally accepted. The dead body example: the premise 'every dead thing is inanimate' is false.
4
Reflect on the power of illusion
Sanusi notes that illusions can cause the soul to reject a correct argument. Consider how false premises can trick the mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا المغالطةُ : فهي ما تأَلَّفَ مِنْ مُقدِّماتٍ شبيهة بالحقِّ وليسَتْ بهِ ، وتُسمَّى سفسطةً ؛ كقولنا في صورة فرس في حائط : هذا فرس ، وكلُّ فرسٍ صَهَّالٌ ، فهذا صَهَّال. أو شبيهة بالمُقدِّماتِ المشهورة ، وتُسمَّى مُشاغَبَةً ؛ كقولنا في شخص يخبط في البحثِ : هذا يُكلِّم العلماء بألفاظ العلم حتى يسكتوا ، وكلُّ مَنْ يُكلَّمُ العلماء بألفاظ العلم فهو عالم ، فهذا عالم . أو مِنْ مُقدِّمات وهميَّةٍ كاذبةٍ ؛ كأن تقول : هذا ميّت ، وكلُّ ميّتٍ جماد ، فهذا جمادٌ ، أو تقول : هذا الميتُ جمادٌ ، وكلُّ جمادٍ لا يُفزِعُ ، فهذا لا يُفزِعُ ، فإنَّ النفس قد لا تقبل هذا الدليل الصحيح
As for fallacy (mughalata): it is that which is composed of premises that resemble truth but are not truth, and it is called sophistry (safsata); like our saying about a picture of a horse on a wall: This is a horse, and every horse is a neighing animal; so this is a neighing animal. Or premises that resemble widely accepted premises, and it is called contentiousness (mushaghaba); like our saying about a person who blunders in discussion: This one speaks to scholars with the words of knowledge to silence them, and everyone who speaks to scholars with the words of knowledge is a scholar; so this is a scholar. Or from false illusory premises; such as saying: This is dead, and every dead thing is an inanimate object; so this is an inanimate object; or saying: This dead thing is an inanimate object, and every inanimate object does not cause fright; so this does not cause fright. For the soul may not accept this correct argument.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage defines fallacy (mughalata) and distinguishes three types. The first type is sophistry (safsata), which uses premises that look like truth but are false; the example is a picture of a horse on a wall – the premise 'every horse neighs' is true for real horses, but the major premise is true and the conclusion is false because the picture is not a real horse. The second type is contentiousness (mushaghaba), which uses premises that resemble widely accepted premises; the example is a blundering person who uses academic jargon to silence scholars, then concludes he is a scholar – the premise that 'anyone who speaks to scholars with scholarly words is a scholar' is not actually accepted, but it looks like it could be. The third type uses false illusory premises (muqaddimat wahmiyya kadhiba), as in the example: 'this is dead, and every dead thing is inanimate, so this is inanimate' – this is false because a dead body is not inanimate (it decays, etc.). The author notes that the soul may even reject a correct argument (الدليل الصحيح) if it contradicts such illusions, showing the power of false premises.

Key Terms
مغالطة
سفسطة
مشاغبة
مقدّمات وهميّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A fallacy is simply any mistake in reasoning.
Correction: Fallacy specifically refers to arguments that mimic truth but are false, as defined by Sanusi.

Checkpoint
Which type of fallacy uses premises that resemble widely accepted premises?
A) Sophistry (safsata)
B) Contentiousness (mushaghaba)
C) Illusion (wahmiyya)
D) Demonstration (burhan)

Key Takeaway
Fallacies are arguments that appear true but are built on false premises; Sanusi classifies them into sophistry, contentiousness, and illusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you were convinced by an argument that later turned out to be false. Which of the three types of fallacy did it resemble? How can you train yourself to spot such deceptions in daily conversations?
?
When you argue with others, do you ever use words or terms you don't fully understand just to sound credible? That is a form of mushaghaba. Will you commit to being honest about what you know and admit ignorance when needed?
?
The source says the soul may reject a correct argument due to false illusions. Is there a belief you hold so strongly that you reject evidence against it? What would it take for you to examine that belief with the same logical tools you've just learned?

Definition and Types of Sophistry
Sanusi defines sophistry and classifies it into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what sophistry is and how it is classified into three types based on the nature of its premises.

Core Idea
Sophistry is disguised deception: arguments that look true but aren't.

Sanusi begins by defining a key term in argumentation: sophistry. He explains that it involves premises that appear true but are actually false. He then distinguishes three varieties, each with its own deceptive mechanism. This lesson unpacks his classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of sophistry
Notice how Sanusi describes it as composed of premises that resemble truth but are not truth. This sets the foundation.
2
Learn the first type: pure sophism (safsata)
Examine the example of a painted horse on a wall to understand how a false analogy leads to a ridiculous conclusion.
This type uses premises that are obviously false to someone with discernment.
3
Learn the second type: contentious disputation (mushāghaba)
Understand how using scholarly jargon can falsely imply expertise, as in the case of a blunderer being called a scholar.
This type exploits premises that resemble commonly accepted beliefs.
4
Learn the third type: false imaginary premises
Examine the examples of the dead body and the rope-snake to see how imagination (wahm) overrides reason, causing rejection of correct proofs.
This is the most subtle type, as it plays on emotional or instinctive reactions.
5
Reflect on the role of wahm (imagination)
Consider why Sanusi emphasizes that imagination can overpower reason, leading to false conclusions despite logical validity.
This highlights the psychological dimension of sophistry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المغالطة: فهي ما تألف من مقدمات شبيهة بالحق وليست به، وتسمى سفسطة؛ كقولنا في صورة فرس في حائط: هذا فرس، وكل فرس صهال، فهذا صهال. أو شبيهة بالمقدمات المشهورة، وتسمى مشاغبة؛ كقولنا في شخص يخبط في البحث: هذا يكلم العلماء بألفاظ العلم حتى يسكتوا، وكل من يكلم العلماء بألفاظ العلم فهو عالم، فهذا عالم. أو من مقدمات وهمية كاذبة؛ كأن تقول: هذا ميت، وكل ميت جماد، فهذا جماد، أو تقول: هذا الميت جماد، وكل جماد لا يفرع، فهذا لا يفرع، فإن النفس قد لا تقبل هذا الدليل الصحيح لمقدمات تتوهمها كاذبة، فتقول: هذا إنسان يمكن قيامه وبطشه وكل من يمكن قيامه وبطشه فليس بجماد، أو: فهو مفزع؛ فهذا ليس بجماد، أو: فهو مفزع، وكما إذا رأيت حبلاً مصنوعاً على شكل حية فتعلم أنه حبل، فإذا ألقي عليك خفت منه؛ لأن الوهم يغلب كثيراً على العقل، تقول النفس: هذا يشبه الحية، أو هذا شكل حية، وكل ما يكون كذلك فهو مخوف، أو: فالحزم الفرار منه، فهذا مخوف، أو: الحزم الفرار منه.
As for sophistry: it is what is composed of premises that resemble truth but are not truth, and it is called sophism (safsata); like our saying about a picture of a horse on a wall: 'This is a horse, and every horse neighs; so this neighs.' Or premises resembling well-known premises, and it is called contentious disputation (mushāghaba); like our saying about a person who blunders in discussion: 'This person speaks to scholars with the jargon of knowledge to silence them, and everyone who speaks to scholars with the jargon of knowledge is a scholar; so this is a scholar.' Or from false imaginary premises; like when you say: 'This is dead, and every dead thing is inanimate, so this is inanimate,' or you say: 'This dead thing is inanimate, and every inanimate thing does not frighten, so this does not frighten'; for the soul may not accept this correct proof because of premises it imagines to be false, so it says: 'This is a human being capable of standing and striking, and everything capable of standing and striking is not inanimate, so this is not inanimate,' or: 'it is frightening; so this is not inanimate,' or: 'it is frightening.' And likewise if you see a rope made in the shape of a snake and you know it is a rope, then if it is thrown at you, you fear it; because imagination (wahm) often overcomes reason. The soul says: 'This resembles a snake, or this is the shape of a snake, and everything that is like that is fearful, or: caution is to flee from it; so this is fearful, or: caution is to flee from it.'
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage defines sophistry (مغالطة) as arguments composed of premises that resemble truth but are false. It is also called سفسطة (sophism). Three types are given:
1. Pure sophism (سفسطة): using an obviously false analogy, like saying a painted horse is a horse that neighs.
2. Contentious disputation (مشاغبة): using premises that seem well-known but are misapplied, like claiming someone is a scholar because they use scholarly jargon.
3. Deceptive imagination (مقدمات وهمية كاذبة): using false imaginary premises, like reasoning that a dead body is inanimate and therefore harmless, when in reality the body is still perceived as a human capable of action. The example of a rope shaped like a snake illustrates how imagination (وهم) can overpower reason, causing fear despite knowing it is just a rope. Sanusi notes that the soul may reject a correct proof because of such imagined false premises.

Key Terms
مغالطة
سفسطة
مشاغبة
مقدمات وهمية
وهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: Sanusi shows that sophistry includes subtle deceptions that resemble truth, often exploiting imagination (وهم).

Consequences of Faulty Imagination
Sanusi enumerates the consequences of faulty imagination and warns against shirk.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how faulty imagination about creation leads to heretical beliefs, including shirk and reliance on intermediaries, and how recognizing the true nature of contingent things protects faith.

Core Idea
Faulty imagination about creation leads directly to associating partners with Allah.

Sanusi warns that most people fall into error because they rely on imagination rather than intellect and revelation. This passage traces the steps from faulty imagination to outright shirk, showing how misperceiving benefit and harm leads to affirming intermediaries and relying on powerless beings. Understanding this chain helps us guard our faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the list of errors that Sanusi presents as consequences of faulty imagination.
The passage begins with a direct causal link: 'بمثل هذا الوهم وقع أكثر الناس...'
2
Identify the specific errors
List the errors Sanusi mentions: falling into innovations, being stuck in habits, preoccupation with creation, misattribution of benefit/harm, shirk, affirming intermediaries, ascribing effect to none, relying on powerless beings.
Each error builds upon the previous one, culminating in shirk.
3
Understand the chain to shirk
Notice how faulty imagination leads to stopping at customary things and then to associating partners with Allah. The core is misperceiving causality.
4
Reflect on the Quranic allusion
The passage ends with 'إنما نحن فتنة فلا تكفر,' alluding to Quran 2:102. Understand that contingent things are tests, not independent agents.
5
Connect to your own perceptions
Reflect on modern contexts where people attribute power to created things (e.g., possessions, status). How does this passage correct that?
This helps internalize the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبمثل هذا الوهم وقع أكثر الناس في أنواع البدع والضلالات، حتى وقفوا مع المعتادات، واشتغلوا بالأكوان عن مكونها، فاعتقدوا نافعاً ما ليس بنافع، وضاراً ما ليس بضار، فأشركوا مع الله غيره، وأثبتوا الوسائط بينه وبين خلقه، وأسندوا التأثير إلى ما ليس له تأثير وتوكلوا على من ليس له حول ولا قوة ولا تدبير ولا تقدير، ولم يعلموا أن الممكنات كلها خيالات تنادي بلسان الحال الذي هو أفصح من لسان المقال: المقصد أمامك إنما نحن فتنة فلا تكفر.
And by such imagination did most people fall into types of innovations and misguidances, until they stopped at customary things, and preoccupied themselves with created things (akwān) apart from their Creator, so they believed to be beneficial what is not beneficial, and harmful what is not harmful, and they associated partners with God other than Him, and affirmed intermediaries between Him and His creation, and ascribed effect to what has no effect, and relied on one who has no power, strength, planning, or decree, and they did not know that all contingent things are illusions that proclaim with the tongue of the state, which is more eloquent than the tongue of speech: 'The goal is before you; we are but a trial, so do not disbelieve.'
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage explains the theological consequences of relying on faulty imagination (وهم). Sanusi states that most people fall into innovation (بدع) and misguidance (ضلالات) because of such imagination. They become stuck in habitual perceptions (المعتادات) and occupy themselves with created things (الأكوان) instead of their Creator. Consequently, they mistakenly attribute benefit or harm to things that do not truly have such effects. This leads to shirk (associating partners with Allah), as they affirm intermediaries (وسائط) between God and creation, ascribe causal efficacy to what has no real effect, and place trust in beings that possess no power or control. Sanusi concludes that all contingent things (الممكنات) are mere illusions (خيالات) that — through their very state of dependence — call out: 'The goal is ahead; we are but a test, so do not disbelieve' (alluding to Quran 2:102).

Key Terms
وهم
بدع
ضلالات
إشراك
وسائط
ممكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imagination is harmless; only explicit disbelief matters.
Correction: Sanusi shows that faulty imagination directly leads to shirk and other serious errors, making it a dangerous source of corruption in faith.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the root cause of people falling into shirk?
A) Following their desires
B) Faulty imagination (وهم)
C) Ignorance of the Quran
D) Evil companions

Key Takeaway
Faulty imagination about the causal powers of created things leads to shirk by affirming intermediaries and denying Allah's sole lordship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficulty, do you instinctively turn to created means (people, things) as if they have independent power, or do you recognize that they are mere illusions and only Allah has true effect? What does this lesson reveal about your heart's reliance?
?
Sanusi says contingent things proclaim 'we are but a trial, so do not disbelieve.' How have you failed to see the trials in your life as opportunities to affirm tawhid, and instead let them lead you to attribute power to others?
?
Think of a 'customary thing' you hold onto for security (a job, a relationship, your health routines). Are you 'stopping at customary things' and 'preoccupied with them apart from the Creator'? What change does this lesson demand from you today?

Correct and Corrupt Belief
Sanusi divides belief into correct and corrupt, defining each with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's classification of belief into correct and corrupt, including the concept of compound ignorance.

Core Idea
Belief is judged by whether it matches reality, not by strength of conviction.

Sanusi begins his discussion of belief by providing a clear, binary classification. He distinguishes between belief that conforms to reality and belief that does not, labeling the latter as not just error but 'compound ignorance.' This foundational distinction sets the stage for later discussions of what constitutes sound doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a sense of its structure. Notice the two divisions and their names.
2
Understand the two categories
Identify the two types: correct belief (i'tiqād ṣaḥīḥ) and corrupt belief (i'tiqād fāsid). Note the condition for correctness: matching reality (muṭābiq limā fī nafs al-amr).
The phrase 'mā fī nafs al-amr' means 'what is in itself' or 'the actual state of affairs.'
3
Examine the examples
Sanusi gives the belief of the general Muslim masses who follow taqlīd as an example of correct belief, and the belief of disbelievers as an example of corrupt belief. Consider why taqlīd does not invalidate correctness as long as the belief matches reality.
4
Recognize 'compound ignorance'
Corrupt belief is also called compound ignorance (jahl murakkab). This means the person is not only wrong but also thinks they are right. Contrast this with simple ignorance (jahl basīṭ) where one knows they don't know.
This term is important in logic and epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - وهو الاعتقاد ـ فينقسم قسمين :
مطابق لِمَا في نفس الأمرِ ؛ ويُسمَّى : الاعتقاد الصحيح ؛ كاعتقادِ عامة المؤمنين المقلدين ، وغيرُ مطابق ؛ ويُسمى : الاعتقاد الفاسد ، والجهل المركَّبَ ؛ كاعتقادِ الكافرين
The second - and it is belief - divides into two divisions: matching what is in the reality; and it is called: correct belief; like the belief of the general believers who are muqallids; and non-matching; and it is called: corrupt belief, and compound ignorance; like the belief of the disbelievers.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage classifies belief (i'tiqād) into two types. The first is correct belief (al-i'tiqād al-ṣaḥīḥ), which is belief that matches reality (mā fī nafs al-amr). The example given is the belief of the general believers (ʿāmmat al-muʾminīn) who follow taqlīd. The second is corrupt belief (al-i'tiqād al-fāsid), also called compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab), which does not match reality, like the belief of disbelievers.

Key Terms
اعتقاد
اعتقاد صحيح
اعتقاد فاسد
جهل مركب
تقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that any false belief is simply an error or mistake, and that compound ignorance is just a fancy term for being wrong.
Correction: Sanusi defines corrupt belief as compound ignorance, which implies that the person holds a false conviction with the belief that it is true. This is a specific epistemological state, not mere error. Simple ignorance is knowing you don't know, but compound ignorance is not knowing and thinking you know.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of correct belief according to Sanusi?
A) Belief that is based on evidence
B) Belief that matches what is in reality
C) Belief that is held by the majority
D) Belief that is free from doubt

Key Takeaway
Belief is evaluated by its correspondence to reality; correct belief matches reality, while corrupt belief is equivalent to compound ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you hold any core beliefs about your faith simply because you were raised with them, without ever verifying whether they match reality as Sanusi demands? What would it take for you to check?
?
Sanusi shows that even a muqallid can have correct belief. But what if your taqlid leads you to something that does not match reality? How would you know, and what would you do?
?
Could you be suffering from compound ignorance about a religious matter you feel certain about? Are you willing to test that certainty against the sources, or are you afraid of what you might find?

Three Opinions on Taqlid in Belief
Sanusi enumerates three opinions on taqlid and defines it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three classical opinions on whether taqlid suffices for faith and the definition of taqlid.

Core Idea
Taqlid in belief is critically examined; salvation may require personal reflection.

What if someone accepts Islamic beliefs simply by following others without personal investigation? Sanusi presents three views from the scholarly tradition. This lesson explores these opinions and the precise definition of taqlid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three opinions
Find each of the three opinions about the muqallid. Note what consequences each opinion attaches to abandoning reflection.
Opinions range from no sin to disbelief.
3
Understand the definition of taqlid
Notice how Sanusi defines taqlid and what it excludes. This definition frames the entire discussion.
Taqlid excludes belief based on prophetic statement, consensus, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : أنَّه مؤمن غيرُ عاص بتركِ النظر ، الثاني : أنه مؤمن لكنه عاص إن ترك النظرَ معَ القدرةِ ، الثالثُ : أَنَّهُ كافر . نصُّهُ في «شاملِهِ» الذي حاذى به « طوالع البيضاوي » : التقليد : اعتقاد جازمٌ لقول غير معصوم ، فيخرجُ : اعتقاد قولِ الرسول ، والإجماع ، ومعرفة مدلول الشهادتين ، والمعاد والفتنة ؛ إما بدليل إجمالي معجوزٍ عن تقريرِهِ وحلَّ شُبَهِهِ ، أو تفصيلي مقدور عليهما فيه . ففي إيمانِ ذي التقليدِ فيهما لا مع عصيانِهِ بتركِ نظرِهِ إن قدر ، أو معه ، ثالثها : هو كافر ؛ لنقل المُقترح مع عز الدين والآمدي محتجين بأنَّ أكثرَ من دخل الإسلام على عهده صلى الله عليه وسلم لم يكونوا عارفين بالمسائل الأصولية ، وحكم صلى الله عليه وسلم بإسلامهم ، ونقل الآمدي عن بعض المتكلمين وأبي هاشم ، مع مقتضى قول الفهري : اكتفاؤه صلى الله عليه وسلم بالنطق بالشهادتين إنما هو في الأحكام الظاهرة ، لا فيما ينجي من الخلود في النار ، وقول الشامل : من مات بعد مضيّ ما يسع نظره وتركه اختياراً .. كافر ، وإن مات قبل مضي ما يسع ذلك مع ترك النظر اختياراً فيما أدرك منه . قولا القاضي : الأصح كفره ، بعد قوله : يمكن ألا يكفر .
First: that he is a believer not sinful for abandoning reflection. Second: that he is a believer but sinful if he abandons reflection while able. Third: that he is a disbeliever. His text in his Shamil, which he modeled on the Tawali‘ al-Baydawi: Taqlid is a firm belief in the statement of a non-infallible. So it excludes: belief in the statement of the Prophet, consensus, knowledge of the meaning of the two testimonies, the resurrection and the trial; either by a general proof which one is unable to articulate or resolve its doubts, or by a detailed proof which one is able to do both. So the believer with taqlid in these [matters] is without sin for abandoning his reflection if able, or with sin [if not]. Third: he is a disbeliever—according to the transmission of al-Muqitah with ‘Izz al-Din and al-Amidi arguing that most of those who entered Islam at his time, peace be upon him, were not knowers of the fundamental issues, and he judged their Islam. Al-Amidi transmitted from some mutakallimun and Abu Hashim, along with the implication of al-Fahri's statement: 'His, peace be upon him, sufficing with the utterance of the two testimonies is only in the outward rulings, not in what saves from eternal punishment in the Fire.' And the statement of al-Shamil: 'Whoever dies after enough time to reflect has passed and he abandons it deliberately... is a disbeliever, and if he dies before enough time passes for that, while abandoning reflection deliberately in what he perceived from it... there are two sayings of the Qadi: the more correct is his disbelief,' after his statement: 'It is possible that he not be declared a disbeliever.'
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a classification of three opinions regarding the person who accepts creed through taqlid (imitation). The first opinion holds that such a person is a believer and not sinful for omitting personal reflection. The second says he is a believer but commits a sin if he was capable of reflection. The third declares him a disbeliever. The author then cites the definition of taqlid from his work al-Shamil: taqlid is firm belief in the statement of a non-infallible (ghayr ma‘sum). This definition excludes belief based on prophetic statement, consensus, and knowledge of the meaning of the two testimonies, resurrection, and trial (fitna). Taqlid may rely on a general proof (dalil ijmalī) that one cannot elaborate, or a detailed proof that one can. Then the author returns to the third opinion, reporting that it is transmitted from al-Muqitah, ‘Izz al-Din, and al-Amidi, who argue that most early Muslims were not scholars of fundamental doctrines yet were deemed believers. Al-‘Amidi reports from some mutakallimun and Abu Hashim, and cites al-Fahri's view that the Prophet's acceptance of the mere utterance of the shahada applies only to worldly rulings, not salvation. Finally, al-Shamil's own position is that if one dies after a sufficient period for reflection and deliberately abandons it, he is a disbeliever; if he dies before that time elapses, there are two opinions from the Qadi, with the more correct one being that he is a disbeliever, though the Qadi also said it might not be so.

Key Terms
taqlid
nazar
dalil ijmalī
dalil tafsīlī
fitna
shahadatayn
muqallid

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think taqlid is always sufficient for faith.
Correction: This passage shows that many classical scholars required personal reflection for true faith, and the third opinion declares the muqallid a disbeliever if he abandons reflection deliberately.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is the third opinion mentioned regarding the person who practices taqlid?
A) He is a believer not sinful for abandoning reflection.
B) He is a believer but sinful if he abandons reflection while able.
C) He is a disbeliever.
D) He is neither a believer nor a disbeliever.

Key Takeaway
Taqlid in core beliefs is disputed; many scholars hold that mere imitation without reflection is insufficient for saving faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I believe in Allah,' do you truly know on what basis you believe, or are you simply repeating what you have heard since childhood? If you had to build your faith from scratch today, would it survive your own scrutiny?
?
Imagine standing before Allah tomorrow. Would He ask you, 'Did you follow the scholars?' or 'Did you seek the truth yourself?' What answer will you give, and how will you prepare for that moment starting now?
?
The passage warns that relying on mere imitation may not save you from the Fire. If your faith is borrowed from others, what one concrete step will you take this week to make it your own through personal reflection?

Key Technical Terms in Kalam: 'Alam, Azal, Ma La Yazal, Qadim
Sanusi defines four essential kalam terms: alam, azal, ma la yazal, qadim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of four essential kalam terms: alam, azal, ma la yazal, and qadim, and their significance in theological discourse.

Core Idea
In kalam, precise definitions of terms like 'alam and azal are not mere vocabulary—they are the bedrock of reasoned theological argument.

Before we can engage with any theological argument, we must share a precise vocabulary. Sanusi here clarifies four terms that appear constantly in kalam discussions about creation and eternity. Understanding these definitions will prevent confusion later.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation, noting each term's definition.
Pay attention to the opposite meanings of azal and ma la yazal.
2
Contrast azal and ma la yazal
Notice how these two terms are opposites: azal negates a beginning, while ma la yazal affirms one.
3
Understand qadim and azali
Recognize that qadim and azali are synonyms in kalam, referring to that which has no beginning.
4
Reflect on the world
The term 'alam includes everything besides Allah—an important boundary for tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تفسير الألفاظ المُحتاج إليها في هذا العلم : فمنها : لفظ العالم بفتح اللام ، ومعناه : كلُّ ما سوى الله تعالى ومنها : لفظ الأزلِ ، ويعنونَ بهِ : نفْيَ الأَوَّليَّةِ ؛ أي : ليسَ لهُ أَوَّلُ . ومنها : قولهم : ما لا يزالُ ، ويعنونَ بهِ : ما لهُ أَوَّل ، وهو ضدُّ الأزل ومنها : القديمُ ، ويعنونَ بهِ : الموجودَ الذي لا أَوَّلَ لوجودِهِ ، ويُسمونَهُ أيضاً : أزليّاً
And as for the interpretation of the terms needed in this science: among them is the word 'al-‘alam' (the world) with fathah on the lam, and its meaning: everything other than Allah, exalted is He. And among them is the word 'al-azal' (eternity without beginning), and by it they mean: the negation of beginning-ness; i.e., it has no beginning. And among them is their saying 'ma la yazal' (what does not cease), and by it they mean: that which has a beginning, and it is the opposite of al-azal. And among them is 'al-qadim' (the ancient), and by it they mean: the existent which has no beginning to its existence, and they also call it 'azaliyyan'.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage explains four technical terms commonly used in kalam. 1) العالَم (al-‘alam): the world, meaning everything other than Allah. 2) الأزل (al-azal): eternity with no beginning; it negates having a starting point. 3) ما لا يزال (ma la yazal): literally 'what does not cease', but technically means that which has a beginning; it is the opposite of azal. 4) القديم (al-qadim): the eternal, meaning an existent with no beginning to its existence; it is synonymous with 'azali'. These definitions are crucial for understanding kalam discussions about the world's creation and God's eternity.

Key Terms
العالم
الأزل
ما لا يزال
القديم
أزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'ma la yazal' is often mistakenly thought to mean 'eternal' or 'without end', but it actually means 'that which has a beginning'.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that ma la yazal means that which has a beginning (ma lahu awwal) and is the opposite of azal.

Checkpoint

B) The negation of beginning-ness
C) The world

Key Takeaway
The precise definitions of these terms, especially the opposite meanings of azal and ma la yazal, are critical for understanding kalam arguments about creation and eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Today, commit a moment to reflect on the fact that everything you love — your life, your health, your family — had a beginning and will have an end, but Allah alone is eternal. Does that produce in you a fear of losing them, or a deeper trust in the Eternal One?
?
The term 'ma la yazal' describes everything that has a beginning: your own existence, every joy, every trial. Are you living as if these temporary things are permanent? Name one thing you have been treating as if it will never end. How would your relationship with Allah change if you truly internalized that only He is 'azali'? Will you ask Him tonight to reorient your heart toward the Eternal?
