Three Foundational Kalam Terms
Defines three key kalam terms: the world, pre-eternity, and that which has a beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise technical meanings of three key kalam terms: al-'alam, al-azal, and ma la yazal.

Core Idea
Three terms define the boundaries of creation, pre-eternity, and temporal beginning.

The mutakallimun developed a precise technical vocabulary to discuss theological issues. This passage introduces three foundational terms that appear throughout kalam texts. Knowing these definitions is essential for following later arguments about God's attributes and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the literal English translation to grasp the basic meaning of each term.
Notice that the definition of 'ma la yazal' is incomplete in the Arabic but the meaning is clear from context.
2
Identify each term and its definition
There are three terms defined. For each, note the precise technical meaning given by the mutakallimun. Pay attention to how 'al-azal' is defined as negation of beginning, not simply 'eternal'.
3
Reflect on the importance of these definitions
Consider why the mutakallimun needed a specialized vocabulary. How does defining 'the world' as 'everything other than God' affect theological discussions about creation? Why is it important to distinguish 'azal' from 'ma la yazal'?
These definitions are building blocks for understanding God's attributes and the relationship between Creator and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمنها : لفظ العالم بفتح اللام ، ومعناه : كلُّ ما سوى الله تعالى . ومنها : لفظ الأزلِ ، ويعنونَ بهِ : نفْيَ الأَوَّليَّةِ ؛ أي : ليسَ لهُ أَوَّلُ . ومنها : قولهم : ما لا يزالُ ، ويعنونَ بهِ : ما لهُ أَوَّل ، وهو ضدُّ

And among them: their saying 'ma la yazal' (what does not cease), by which they mean: what has a beginning, and it is the opposite [of eternal].
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage defines three key terms: 'al-alam' (the world) is everything other than God. 'Al-azal' (pre-eternity) means having no beginning. 'Ma la yazal' (that which does not cease) is used by the mutakallimun to mean something that has a beginning (i.e., it begins to exist and then continues), which is opposite of azal. Note: the definition of 'ma la yazal' appears incomplete in the text but it is clearly the opposite of azal.

Key Terms
العالم
الأزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'al-azal' means eternal in both directions (past and future) or that it is synonymous with 'qadim' (ancient) without nuance.
Correction: The passage precisely defines 'al-azal' as negation of firstness (no beginning), not as eternal in the future. It is a term specific to the past aspect of eternity. The term for future eternity is often 'abad' or 'baqa''. While related, 'azal' and 'qadim' are not identical; 'qadim' can also refer to something ancient, but in God's case it implies no beginning.

Definition of al-Qadim (the Ancient)
Defines the term 'al-qadim' and its synonym 'azali'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical meaning of 'al-qadim' in kalam and its synonym 'azali'.

Core Idea
In kalam, 'al-qadim' means existence without a beginning — an attribute of Allah.

This short passage introduces a key term in Islamic theology: 'al-qadim' (the Ancient). The mutakallimun use this term to describe the eternal nature of Allah, defining it precisely as existence without a beginning. They also use the synonym 'azali'. Understanding this definition is essential for grasping later arguments about God's attributes.

Guided Path

Read the passage carefully

Logic and Technical Terms
beginner

Defining al-Da'im (the Everlasting)
Sanusi defines al-da'im and equates it with al-abadi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of al-da'im (the Everlasting) as an attribute of Allah and its synonym al-abadi.

Core Idea
Al-da'im is existence without end, never subject to non-existence.

In this brief passage, Sanusi defines one of the key attributes of Allah: al-da'im (the Everlasting). This term describes the quality of eternal future existence, meaning that Allah's existence never ceases. The author also introduces its synonym, al-abadi, to reinforce the concept. Understanding this definition is essential for distinguishing between different aspects of eternity in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation to grasp the basic meaning.
Notice the concise style used for definitions.
2
Identify the two key terms
Locate the terms 'al-da'im' and 'al-abadi' in the passage. Understand that they are synonyms referring to the same attribute.
3
Understand the definition of al-da'im
Focus on the definition: 'the existent whose existence does not cease' – meaning it never ends and is not subject to non-existence.
This contrasts with the term 'al-azali' (without beginning).
4
Distinguish from related terms
Note the common misunderstanding: do not confuse 'da'im' (without end) with 'azali' (without beginning). Both are eternal but from different perspectives.
This distinction will be useful in later lessons.

And among them: the word 'al-da'im', by which they mean: the existent whose existence does not cease, i.e., non-existence does not befall it, and they also call it 'al-abadi'.

Commentary
This passage defines 'al-da'im' as that which exists without cessation, never subject to non-existence. It is synonymous with 'al-abadi' (eternal future). This term contrasts with 'hadith' (temporal).

Core Idea
Al-hadith means created — existing after non-existence.

The mutakallimun use precise technical terms to discuss creation. One of the most basic is 'al-hadith', which describes everything that is not eternal. This short but crucial definition sets the foundation for distinguishing the Creator from creation.

Guided Path

2
Understand the contrast
Contrast al-hadith with al-qadim (the eternal). Everything other than Allah is hadith, including the universe, angels, souls, time itself.
This term applies to all created things.
3
Reflect on theological implications
If everything except Allah is hadith, then everything depends on Allah for its existence. This reinforces tawhid.
This is a key building block of kalam.

Definition of Jawhar and Its Divisions
Defines 'jawhar' as occupying space, then explains 'jawhar fard' and 'jism'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam definition of 'jawhar' as that which occupies space and its division into the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) and the divisible body (jism).

Core Idea
In kalam, 'jawhar' means any physical substance occupying space, not philosophical essence.

This lesson clarifies a foundational term in Islamic theology: 'jawhar' (substance). The mutakallimun defined it strictly as anything that occupies space, in contrast to philosophical or commonsense notions. Understanding this definition is essential before exploring further topics like the creation of bodies or the nature of atoms.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once to get a sense of the definition and examples.
Pay attention to the contrast with knowledge and color.
2
Analyze the definition of jawhar
Examine the key phrase: 'whose body occupies a void such that it prevents another from occupying its place.' This is the same as 'mutahayyiz' (occupying space).
3
Explore the two divisions
Notice the division based on divisibility: indivisible (jawhar fard) vs divisible (jism).
This atomic view is central to kalam cosmology.
4
Understand the naming of jism
The author explains when a tiny substance is called a body: only when combined with another. Each part of a composite is called a body because the composite is a body.
This clarifies the terminology applied to atoms and aggregates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : لفظ الجوهر ، ويعنون به : ما كانَ جِرْمُهُ يشغل فراغاً بحيثُ يمتنعُ أَنْ يَحُلَّ غيرُهُ حِيثُ حَلَّ ، وهو معنى المُتحيّز ؛ وذلك كالإنسانِ والحجر ، لا كالعلم واللون ، فإنْ كانَ الجوهر دقيقاً بحيث انتهى في الدّقَةِ إلى أنَّهُ لا يقبلُ الانقسام بوجه .. فهو المُسمَّى بالجوهر الفردِ ، وإِنْ كانَ يقبلُ الانقسام فهو المُسمَّى بالجسم ، ويُسمَّى كلُّ واحدٍ مِنْ أَجْزَائِهِ جسماً ، وإنَّما يمتنعونَ مِنْ تسمية الدقيقِ جسماً حالَ انفرادِهِ ، أما إذا انضم إلى غيره سموا كلَّ واحدٍ منهما جسماً ؛ لأنَّ حقيقة الجسم المؤلفُ ، وكلٌّ مِنَ الجوهرين عند الاجتماع يَصدُقُ عليهِ أَنَّهُ مُؤلَّفٌ
And among them: the word 'al-jawhar', by which they mean: that whose body occupies a void such that it prevents another from occupying its place, and that is the meaning of 'mutahayyiz' (occupying space), such as a human and stone, not like knowledge and color. If the jawhar is subtle such that it reaches the utmost subtlety so that it is not divisible at all, then it is called 'al-jawhar al-fard' (the indivisible atom). If it is divisible, then it is called 'al-jism' (body). Each of its parts is called a body, but they refrain from calling the subtle a body when isolated. However, when it joins another, they call each of them a body, because the reality of the body is that it is composite, and each of two jawhars upon combination is truly described as composite.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage defines 'jawhar' (substance) as that which occupies space (mutahayyiz). Examples: humans and stones. It distinguishes between the indivisible atom (jawhar fard), which cannot be divided further, and the body (jism), which is divisible. The author also explains the naming convention: a solitary tiny substance is not called a body, but when two combine, each is called a body because the composite (body) is formed. This covers the atomic theory of the mutakallimun.

Key Terms
الجوهر
الجوهر الفرد
الجسم
المتحيز
الانقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Jawhar refers to the essence or quiddity of a thing, as in philosophy.
Correction: In kalam, jawhar specifically means a physical substance that occupies space (mutahayyiz), like a body, not a metaphysical essence.

Checkpoint
When is a subtle substance called a 'jism' (body) according to this passage?
A) When it is divisible
B) When it is indivisible
C) When it is alone
D) When it combines with another substance

Key Takeaway
The term 'jawhar' in kalam refers specifically to a physical substance that occupies space, which is then divided into indivisible atoms and divisible bodies.

The Definition of Accident (al-'Arad)
Defines 'al-'arad' as non-spatial, dependent on substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of 'al-'arad' (accident) in kalam: that it does not occupy space and cannot exist independently, but only as a dependent attribute of a substance.

Core Idea
An accident is a non-spatial, dependent attribute; it cannot stand alone.

Before we can reason about the world and its Creator, we need a shared vocabulary for the things that exist. One of the most basic categories in kalam is 'accident' (al-'arad). This passage gives the technical definition that distinguishes accidents from substances, setting the stage for later arguments about the contingency of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, noticing the key terms. Then read the translation. Let the definition sink in before moving to analysis.
Pay attention to the phrase 'la tashghal faraghan' (does not occupy a void).
2
Identify the two negative characteristics
The definition gives two things an accident is NOT: it does not occupy space, and it does not subsist by itself. Mark these in the text.
3
Understand the positive characteristic
The definition then gives the positive relation: an accident's existence is dependent (tabi') on the substance. Reflect on the examples (knowledge, motion, rest).
Notice that the accident does not add to the spatial occupation of the substance.
4
Connect to common misunderstanding
Consider why someone might mistakenly think accidents are independent. The passage explicitly corrects this by stressing dependency.
This is a foundational distinction in kalam ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : لفظ العَرَضِ ، ويعنونَ بهِ : ما كانَتْ ذاتُهُ لا تشغل فراغاً ، ولا له قيام بنفسِهِ ، وإنَّما يكونُ وجوده تابعاً لوجودِ الجوهر ؛ كالعلم الذي يقوم بالجوهر ، وكالحركة والسكون ؛ فإنَّها لا تشغل فراغاً ، بل الفراغ الذي يشغلُهُ الجوهرُ قبلَ اتصافِهِ بها .. هو الفراغ الذي يشغلُهُ معَ اتصافه بها مِنْ غيرِ زيادةٍ
And among them: the word 'al-'arad', by which they mean: that whose essence does not occupy a void, nor does it subsist by itself, but its existence is dependent on the existence of the substance, like knowledge that subsists in the substance, and like motion and rest; for they do not occupy a void, rather the void occupied by the substance before being described by them is the same void it occupies when described by them, without any increase.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage defines 'al-'arad' (accident) as that which does not occupy space and cannot exist independently; it exists only as an attribute of a substance (jawhar). Examples: knowledge, motion, rest. The author notes that accidents do not add to the spatial occupation of the substance; the substance occupies the same space whether it has the accident or not.

Key Terms
العرض
القيام بالنفس
التابع
الفراغ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that accidents, like motion or rest, are independent entities that exist on their own.
Correction: In kalam, accidents cannot exist independently; their existence is entirely dependent on a substance. They do not occupy space and have no self-subsistence.

Checkpoint
What is the defining characteristic of an accident ('arad) according to this passage?
A) It occupies space
B) It exists independently
C) It does not occupy space and does not subsist by itself
D) It is divisible

Key Takeaway
An accident is a non-spatial attribute that depends entirely on a substance for its existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your knowledge of Islamic theology is an accident in your soul. When you reflect on what you've learned, do you feel a sense of ownership over it, as if it stands on its own? How can you remind yourself daily that your understanding is dependent on the One who sustains your very existence? What act of humility can you perform today to acknowledge that dependence?
?
The passage says accidents do not occupy space, yet you often treat your emotions and thoughts as if they are solid and permanent. Which accident—whether it be anger, pride, or even a false sense of security—do you cling to as if it were a substance? How can you release your grip on it, recognizing it as a passing, dependent attribute?
?
When the substance acquires an accident, the void it occupies does not increase. This means that your core self (the substance) remains unchanged by the accidents that come and go. How does this truth challenge the way you define yourself by your changing moods, achievements, or failures? What part of you is truly constant, and how can you anchor your identity there rather than in fleeting accidents?

Eternal and Originated: The Two Key Terms of Kalam
Sanusi defines the terms 'eternal' and 'originated'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of the terms 'eternal' (al-da'im) and 'originated' (al-hadith) as used in classical kalam.

Core Idea
Eternal means endless existence; originated means having a beginning.

Sanusi begins his theological lexicon by clarifying two fundamental terms: the eternal and the originated. These categories underpin every discussion about God and creation. Without a precise understanding of these definitions, later arguments about divine attributes and the cosmos would lack a firm foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the precise meaning.
Notice the concise style of the matn.
2
Identify the two definitions
Locate the definition of 'al-da'im' (eternal) and 'al-hadith' (originated). Write them in your own words.
Also note the synonym 'al-abadī' for eternal.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why these definitions are foundational. The eternal applies only to Allah, the originated to everything else.
This distinction is crucial for understanding tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : لفظ الدائم ويعنون به : الموجود الذي لا ينقضي وجوده ؛ أي : لا يلحقه عدم ، ويُسمونَهُ أيضاً : الأبدي . ومنها : لفظ الحادثِ ، ويعنونَ بِهِ : ما وُجِدَ بعدَ أَنْ كَانَ معدوماً .
And among them: the term 'al-da'im' (the eternal). By it they mean: the existent whose existence does not come to an end; i.e., non-existence does not affect it, and they also call it 'al-abadī' (the everlasting). And among them: the term 'al-ḥādith' (the originated). By it they mean: that which came into existence after it had been non-existent.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents definitions of two key terms in kalam: 'al-da'im' (the eternal) and 'al-ḥādith' (the originated). The author explains that 'al-da'im' refers to an existent that never ceases to exist, meaning it is not subject to non-existence. It is synonymous with 'al-abadī' (everlasting). Then 'al-ḥādith' is defined as something that comes into existence after a state of non-existence. These definitions are foundational for discussions about the attributes of God (eternal) and the creation of the world (originated).

Key Terms
الدائم
الحادث
الأبدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might confuse 'al-da'im' with 'al-azalī' (pre-eternal, without beginning), but here it only refers to endless duration.
Correction: This passage defines al-da'im solely in terms of future endlessness, not past eternity. The term 'eternal' in this context means 'everlasting' (without end), not 'without beginning'.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
In kalam, 'eternal' describes what has no end, while 'originated' describes what has a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
God alone is 'eternal' and 'everlasting'—His existence never ends. Your existence, however, will end one day. If you were to die tonight, would the people who know you most think you lived for the Eternal or for the temporary? What specific habit or priority needs to change to answer that question honestly?
?
The passage defines 'al-ḥādith' as that which came to be after non-existence. What if, instead of merely acknowledging this fact, you spent the next week intentionally reminding yourself that every breath could be your last? What would you do differently starting today?

4 min
Definition of Substance in Kalam
Sanusi defines substance and distinguishes indivisible atom from body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific kalam definition of substance (jawhar) as an entity occupying space and the distinction between the indivisible atom and the composite body.

Core Idea
Substance is what occupies space – nothing else does.

In classical kalam, 'jawhar' does not mean 'essence' in the abstract sense; it means a physical entity that occupies a void, preventing other entities from occupying its exact location. This foundational concept distinguishes substances from accidents. Sanusi then explains the two types of substances: the indivisible atom and the composite body, clarifying how kalam terminology handles them.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of jawhar
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi defines substance. Notice the condition: it occupies space and prevents others from occupying its location.
This is the core meaning of mutahayyiz.
2
Identify the two types
The passage divides substance into two types based on divisibility. Find the terms 'al-jawhar al-fard' and 'al-jism'.
3
Understand the naming rule
Sanusi explains that a single indivisible atom is not called a body when alone, but when combined with another, each is called a body because a body is composed.
This shows the importance of composition in defining a body.
4
Consider the footnote
The footnote presents an alternative view by Imams al-Ghazali and al-Razi. Note that they consider two combined substances as one body.
This is a nuance; the main definition remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : لفظ الجوهر ، ويعنون به : ما كانَ جِرْمُهُ يشغل فراغاً بحيثُ يمتنعُ أَنْ يَحُلَّ غيرُهُ حِيثُ حَلَّ ، وهو معنى المُتحيّز ؛ وذلك كالإنسانِ والحجر ، لا كالعلم واللون ، فإنْ كانَ الجوهر دقيقاً بحيث انتهى في الدّقَةِ إلى أنَّهُ لا يقبلُ الانقسام بوجه .. فهو المُسمَّى بالجوهر الفردِ ، وإِنْ كانَ يقبلُ الانقسام فهو المُسمَّى بالجسم ، ويُسمَّى كلُّ واحدٍ مِنْ أَجْزَائِهِ جسماً ، وإنَّما يمتنعونَ مِنْ تسمية الدقيقِ جسماً حالَ انفرادِهِ ، أما إذا انضم إلى غيره سموا كلَّ واحدٍ منهما جسماً ؛ لأنَّ حقيقة الجسم المؤلفُ ، وكلٌّ مِنَ الجوهرين عند الاجتماع يَصدُقُ عليهِ أَنَّهُ مُؤلَّفٌ (۱) . (۱) وهذا مذهب إمام الحرمين ، ومذهب الإمامين الغزالي والرازي أنهما جسم واحد ؛ لأنَّ كل واحد منهما لا يقبل القسمة ، وانظر « شرح لمع الأدلة » ( ص ٤٩ ) .
And among them: the term 'al-jawhar' (substance). By it they mean: that whose body occupies a void such that it is impossible for something else to occupy where it occupies, and this is the meaning of 'being in space' (al-taḥayyuz). This applies to a human and a stone, not to knowledge and colour. If the substance is fine to the extent that it reaches such fineness that it is not divisible in any way, then it is called 'the indivisible substance' (al-jawhar al-fard). If it is divisible, then it is called 'body' (jism). Each of its parts is also called a body, but they refrain from calling the fine substance a body while it is alone. However, when it joins another, they call each of them a body, because the reality of a body is that it is composed, and each of the two substances when they come together can truthfully be described as composed. (1) This is the doctrine of Imām al-Ḥaramayn, and the doctrine of the two Imāms al-Ghazālī and al-Rāzī is that they are one single body because each of them does not admit division. See 'Sharḥ Lumʿ al-Adilla' p. 49.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage defines 'al-jawhar' (substance) as an entity whose body occupies space, meaning it is spatially extended, and it is the meaning of 'al-mutaḥayyiz' (occupying space). Examples are humans and stones, as opposed to accidents like knowledge or color. It then distinguishes two types of substance: the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard), which is so fine it cannot be further divided, and the composite body (jism), which can be divided. The author notes the terminology: each part of a body is also called a body, but they avoid calling the indivisible atom a body while it exists alone; when it combines with another, then each is called a body because the reality of a body is to be composed. The footnote adds that according to certain scholars, the two substances when combined become one body because each is indivisible.

Key Terms
الجوهر
الجوهر الفرد
الجسم
المتحيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'jawhar' means 'essence' in general, like the philosophical essence of a thing.
Correction: In kalam, 'jawhar' specifically refers to a physical substance that occupies space. It does not refer to abstract essences.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of 'al-jawhar' (substance) in this passage?
A) That which does not occupy space
B) That which occupies space and cannot be divided
C) That whose body occupies a void
D) That which is composite

Key Takeaway
In kalam, a substance is defined as an entity occupying space, and it is either an indivisible atom or a composite body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that only substances truly occupy space, while accidents like colour and knowledge exist only in substances. What 'accidents' in your life—like status, possessions, or fleeting emotions—do you treat as if they are self-subsisting and real? How can you reorient your heart to prioritize the 'substances' of faith and obedience that alone have lasting reality? Commit to one concrete action today to shift your focus.
?
The indivisible atom is so small it cannot be divided further, yet when two atoms combine, they form a body. Consider the small, seemingly insignificant acts of disobedience or negligence in your daily life. Which 'atoms' of sin are you ignoring, thinking they are harmless alone, but they accumulate into a body of spiritual disease? Identify one such habit and resolve to uproot it before it compounds.
?
Sanusi clarifies that even an atom is called a body when joined with another. In your relationships, do you recognize each person as a distinct 'substance' occupying its own space, or do you try to crowd or impose on others? Reflect on how you can respect the God-given individuality of those around you, knowing that each soul is a unique substance that cannot be replaced. What specific change can you make today to honor their 'place'?

Definition of Accident in Kalam
Sanusi defines accident and illustrates its dependence on substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical kalam defines an accident (al-ʿaraḍ) as an entity that does not occupy space and cannot exist independently but inheres in a substance.

Core Idea
An accident is a real entity that exists only in a substance and does not occupy space.

Before we can discuss the attributes of Allah or created things, we need a precise understanding of the categories of existents. One key category is the accident—something that requires a substance to exist. In this passage, Imam Sanusi defines accident and gives clear examples to distinguish it from substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without stopping. Let the terms settle. Then read the literal translation to grasp the exact meaning.
The passage is concise; each phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the key characteristics
Notice that Sanusi states two essential features: the accident does not occupy space (لا تشغل فراغاً) and it does not subsist by itself (لا له قيام بنفسه).
3
Examine the examples
The examples of knowledge, motion, and rest help clarify the definition. They are real entities that exist in a substance but do not take up additional space.
The example of space is critical: the substance occupies the same void whether or not the accident is present.
4
Reflect on a common mistake
Some may think accidents are mere properties. The passage shows they are real existents, but their existence is dependent. Note the phrase 'وجودُهُ تابعٌ لوجود الجوهر' (its existence is dependent on the existence of the substance).
This sets the stage for later discussions on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : لفظ العَرَضِ ، ويعنونَ بهِ : ما كانَتْ ذاتُهُ لا تشغل فراغاً ، ولا له قيام بنفسِهِ ، وإنَّما يكونُ وجوده تابعاً لوجودِ الجوهر ؛ كالعلم الذي يقوم بالجوهر ، وكالحركة والسكون ؛ فإنَّها لا تشغل فراغاً ، بل الفراغ الذي يشغلُهُ الجوهرُ قبلَ اتصافِهِ بها .. هو الفراغ الذي يشغلُهُ معَ اتصافه بها مِنْ غيرِ زيادةٍ
And among them: the term 'al-ʿaraḍ' (accident). By it they mean: that whose essence does not occupy a void and does not subsist by itself; rather its existence is dependent on the existence of a substance, such as knowledge which inheres in a substance, and motion and rest; for they do not occupy a void, but the void that the substance occupies before being qualified by them is the void it occupies with being qualified by them without any increase.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage defines 'al-ʿaraḍ' (accident) as an entity that does not occupy space and cannot exist independently; it always inheres in a substance. Examples are knowledge (which exists in the substance) and motion and rest. The author clarifies that accidents do not occupy any additional space beyond what the substance occupies. When an accident like motion occurs in a substance, it does not cause the substance to occupy more space; the same void is used.

Definitions of Necessary, Impossible, and Contingent
Sanusi defines the three rational judgments with subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of the three rational judgments in Islamic theology and their division into necessary, impossible, and contingent, each with subtypes.

Core Idea
The three rational categories form the foundation of Islamic theological reasoning.

In this passage, Sanusi lays out the foundational conceptual framework for Islamic theology by defining the three modal categories of existence. These categories are essential for understanding how theologians reason about God, creation, and the relationship between them. As you read, notice how each category is defined in terms of conceivability and how they are further divided into what is known by necessity and by rational consideration.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the three categories and their subcategories.
Pay attention to the terms 'ضرورة' (necessity) and 'نظر' (consideration).
2
Identify the structure
The passage follows a pattern: define each term, then give examples for its two subdivisions.
This pattern makes the content easy to memorize.
3
Understand the stipulation
Focus on the phrase 'لذاتِهِ' (by itself) and how it modifies the contingent. Think about why this is important.
This prevents the contingent from being confused with necessary or impossible due to external factors.
4
Reflect on the footnote
Read the footnote about the example for contingent by consideration. Consider why the author chose that example and what the commentator suggests.
This shows scholarly attention to clarity of expression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : لفظ الواجب ، ويعنون به : ما لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقل عدمُهُ ؛ إِمَّا بالضرورةِ ؛ كالتحيز للجوهر ، وإِمَّا بالنظرِ ؛ كوجودِهِ تعالى وثبوتِ صفاتِ ذاتِهِ . ومنها : لفظ المستحيل ، ويعنونَ بهِ : ما لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقل وجوده ؛ إمَّا ضرورةً ؛ كوجودِ الضدَّينِ في محلّ واحدٍ وزمنٍ واحدٍ أو نظراً ؛ كوجودِ الشريك له جلَّ وعلا . ومنها : لفظ الجائز ، ويعنونَ بهِ : ما لا يلزمُ مِنْ تصورِ وجودِهِ ولا عدمِهِ محال لذاتِهِ ؛ إمَّا بالضرورة ؛ كوجودِ زيد ونحوه ، وإمَّا بالنظرِ ؛ كالثواب للمطيعينَ والعقابِ للكافرين . واحترز بقولِهِ : ( لذاتِهِ ( مِنْ صيرورة الجائز واجباً لأمر خارج عن ذاته ؛ وهو تعلق علم الله بوجودِهِ ؛ كالجنة والنار ، أو مستحيلاً ؛ لتعلقِ علم الله بعدم وقوعه ؛ كوجود الثواب للكافرين ، وحصولِ العقاب للمطيعين . (۱) مثَلَ بما ذكر للجائز النظري وإن كان بحسب الظاهر التمثيل بالعكس أولى .. لإيهام العقل وجوب ذلك بُدّاً . « عكاري » ( ق ٦٤ )
And among them: the term 'al-wājib' (necessary). By it they mean: that whose non-existence cannot be conceived in the mind; either by necessity, such as the occupation of space for a substance, or by consideration, such as His existence (Exalted) and the affirmation of the attributes of His essence. And among them: the term 'al-mustaḥīl' (impossible). By it they mean: that whose existence cannot be conceived in the mind; either by necessity, such as the existence of two opposites in one place and one time, or by consideration, such as the existence of a partner to Him (Glorious and Exalted). And among them: the term 'al-jā'iz' (contingent). By it they mean: that from whose conception neither its existence nor non-existence entails an impossibility by itself; either by necessity, such as the existence of Zayd and the like, or by consideration, such as reward for the obedient and punishment for the disbelievers. And he stipulates by his saying 'by itself' to exclude the case of the contingent becoming necessary due to an external cause, which is the attachment of God's knowledge to its existence, like Paradise and Hell, or impossible due to the attachment of God's knowledge to its non-occurrence, like the existence of reward for the disbelievers and the occurrence of punishment for the obedient. (1) He gave the example he mentioned for the theoretical contingent, although apparently the opposite example would be more appropriate, because it suggests to the mind that it is necessarily obligatory initially. 'Akkārī (q. 64).
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage defines the three modal categories of existence in Islamic theology: necessary (wājib), impossible (mustaḥīl), and contingent (jā'iz). Each is divided into what is known by necessity (bi-ḍarūra) and what is known by consideration (bi-l-naẓar). The necessary is that whose non-existence is inconceivable, either by necessity (like substance necessarily occupies space) or by consideration (like God's existence). The impossible is that whose existence is inconceivable, by necessity (like two opposites in same location) or by consideration (like a partner to God). The contingent is that whose existence and non-existence are both conceivable without entailing an impossibility in itself. The author then notes a stipulation: contingency is conditional on being considered in itself, because if God's knowledge is attached to its existence (like Paradise), it becomes necessary; if attached to its non-existence (like reward for disbelievers), it becomes impossible. The footnote comments on the choice of examples, suggesting the opposite might be clearer.

الواجب
المستحيل
الجائز
الضرورة
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'jā'iz' means 'permissible' in the legal sense.
Correction: Here it is a metaphysical category meaning 'possible' or 'contingent'.

Checkpoint

B) That whose non-existence is necessary
C) That from whose conception neither existence nor non-existence entails impossibility by itself
D) That which is known by necessity

Key Takeaway
The three rational judgments—necessary, impossible, and contingent—are defined by what is conceivable in the mind, and each is further divided into what is known by necessity and by rational consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of something you believe is absolutely necessary in your life—like a habit, a relationship, or a worldview. Now consider: is it truly necessary by itself, or could it become impossible if God's knowledge attached to its non-existence? How does this passage challenge the way you think about certainty in your own life?
?
The passage defines contingent things as those whose existence or non-existence is not inherently impossible. But then it says they can become necessary or impossible due to God's knowledge. Where do you stand in relation to God's knowledge? Is there an area of your life where you act as if your choices are purely contingent, ignoring that God's knowledge already determines their outcome? What would it mean to truly submit to that reality?
?

The Necessary Judgment, Origin-Branch Analogy, and the Free Agent Argument
Sanusi defines necessary judgment and explains origin, branch, and the need for a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a necessary judgment is always an essential attribute or its necessary concomitant, and how the origin-branch analogy supports the need for a free agent.

Core Idea
A necessary judgment is rooted in essence; nature cannot specify, so a free agent must.

This passage delves into foundational kalam concepts. It clarifies what a necessary judgment can be—only an essential attribute or its necessary concomitant—then uses the origin-branch analogy to illustrate dependence without causation. The discussion culminates in the argument that the world's uniformity yet diversity demands a free agent, not blind nature or cause.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of necessary judgment
Read the opening statement: a necessary judgment can only be an essential attribute or its necessary concomitant. Notice the terms 'essential attribute' (ṣifa nafsiyya) and 'necessary concomitant' (lāzim).
2
Examine the contradiction argument
The passage explains why specifying one of two similars with a necessary judgment leads to a contradiction. Follow the logic: similarity requires equality in essential attributes; specification breaks equality, making it a 'similar unlike a similar'.
This is the core of the proof.
3
Learn the origin-branch analogy
Find the definitions of origin (aṣl) and branch (farʿ). Origin is that from which another originates without causal effect; branch is the resulting thing. Note the example of water and plants.
This clarifies customary sequence versus real causation.
4
Grasp the need for a free agent
The passage concludes that all things need a free agent because nature and cause cannot specify one similar over another. The world's parts are similar yet specified differently, pointing to a free chooser.
Connect this to earlier proofs about the agent of your essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكم الواجبُ لا يكون إلا صفةً نفسية أو لازماً لها ] وقوله : ( لِمَا يلزمُ مِن اجتماعِ متنافيين ) هذا بيان لبطلان التالي ، وهو جواب الشرط ؛ أي : يلزم على اختصاص أحدِ المِثْلين بحكم واجب أن يكونَ مِثْلاً غيرَ مِثْلٍ ؛ يعني : لأنَّ التماثل يقتضي استواء المثلين في جميع صفاتِ النفسِ ؛ أي : الصفاتِ التي ليس لها وجود زائد على الذاتِ ، واختصاص أحدهما بحكم واجب ـ وهو لا يكونُ إلا صفةً نفسيةً أو لازماً لها (۱) - يُوجِبُ انفراد أحدهما عن نفسية ، فلا يشتركان في جميع صفاتِ النفس ، فلا يكونُ إِذا مِثْلاً له ، كيف وقد تحقَّقَ أَنَّهُ مثل له ؟! فقد لزم أن يكونَ مِثْلاً غير مثل ! وهو تهافت. وقوله : ( أصلِهِ وفرعِهِ ) يعني بالأصل : ما نشأ عنه غيرُهُ بحَسَبِ مجرى العادة مِنْ غيرِ تأثيرِ لهُ أصلاً ، وبالفرع : الغير الناشئ ؛ كالماء للنبات ونحو ذلك. وقوله : ( وأنَّ الجميع مفتقر إلى فاعل مختار ) يعني : لأنَّ الطبيعة والعِلَّةَ لا يُخصِّصانِ مِثْلاً عن مِثْلِ ، والعالم كله متماثل ، ومع ذلكَ قد اختص كلُّ جزء منه بما لم يثبت لمماثله ، وقد سبق تقرير ذلكَ فاعل ذاتِكَ ، والحال واحدٌ ، ولهذا المعنى استغنى عن ذكر ذلك هنا ، وهو مندرج في التشبيه بقوله : ( كافتقارك ) (1) كالناطقية صفة نفسية للإنسان، وقبول العلم والكتابة لازم لها . مفاد حامدي » ( ص ١٢٣ ) .
The necessary judgment is only an essential attribute or its necessary concomitant. And his statement: (Because what results from the conjunction of two contradictory things) – this is an explanation of the invalidity of the consequent, and it is the answer to the condition; i.e., it follows from the specification of one of two similars with a necessary judgment that it should be a similar unlike a similar; meaning: because similarity necessitates the equality of the two similars in all essential attributes – i.e., the attributes that have no existence in addition to the essence – and the specification of one of them with a necessary judgment – and it is only an essential attribute or its necessary concomitant – necessitates the uniqueness of one of them in an essential attribute, so they do not share in all essential attributes, so it then is not similar to it; how, when it has been verified that it is similar to it?! So it would entail that it is a similar unlike a similar! It is a contradiction. And his statement: (its origin and its branch) – he means by 'origin': that from which another originates according to customary course, without any effect of it at all; and by 'branch': the originating other; like water for plants and the like. And his statement: (And that all are in need of a free agent) – he means: because nature and cause do not specify one similar over another similar, and the entire world is similar, and despite that, each part has been specified with what is not established for its similar; and the determination of that has preceded in [the proof of] the agent of your essence, and the situation is one. And for this meaning, he dispensed with mentioning that here, and it is included in the analogy in his statement: (like your need) (1) Like 'rationality' is an essential attribute for man, and the acceptance of knowledge and writing is a necessary concomitant of it. From the benefit of Hamidi (p. 123).
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of key concepts in the previous proof. It begins by stating that a necessary judgment (ḥukm wājib) can only be an essential attribute (ṣifa nafsiyya) or a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of it. The author then comments on the phrase 'because what results from the conjunction of two contradictory things' – this is an explanation of why the consequent is invalid. If one of two similars is singled out for a necessary judgment, that judgment must be an essential attribute or its concomitant. But similarity requires equality in all essential attributes. Therefore, such specification would make them unequal, contradicting their similarity. This is a contradiction (tahāfut). Next, the author explains 'origin' (aṣl) and 'branch' (farʿ): the origin is that from which something else arises according to customary course without any actual causal effect; the branch is the resulting thing, like water for plants. Finally, the author explains that all things are in need of a free agent because nature (ṭabīʿa) and cause (ʿilla) do not specify one similar over another. The world is entirely similar, yet each part has properties not found in its similar. This specification points to a free agent, as established earlier. The author notes that this point is already covered and is included in the analogy 'like your need' (kāftiqārik). The footnote gives an example: rationality (nāṭiqiyya) is an essential attribute of man, and the capacity for knowledge and writing is a necessary concomitant.

Key Terms
الحكم الواجب
الصفة النفسية
اللازم
الأصل
الفرع
الطبيعة
العلة
التخصيص
المماثلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'origin' and 'branch' imply real causal efficacy, as in a cause-effect relationship.
Correction: The author clarifies that origin and branch are based on customary sequence without any actual effect of the origin on the branch; the dependence is purely existential, not causal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what type of judgment can be a 'necessary judgment' (ḥukm wājib)?
A) Any attribute that is always present.
B) Only an essential attribute (ṣifa nafsiyya) or its necessary concomitant.
C) Any judgment that is universally true.
D) A judgment that results from a cause.

Key Takeaway
A necessary judgment is always an essential attribute or its concomitant; the origin-branch analogy illustrates existential dependence; and the world's specification proves the necessity of a free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence depends on a free Agent who specifies every detail, why do you still act as if your plans are the ultimate deciders of your life? What would change today if you truly internalized that?
?
The argument shows that nature cannot specify one similar over another. When you look at your own unique traits—your personality, circumstances, and trials—do you see them as random or as deliberately chosen for you? How does that affect your gratitude and patience?
?
You have read that a necessary judgment must be rooted in essence. Reflect: Are your own judgments and commitments merely superficial, or are they essential to who you are? What would it mean for your faith to be an essential attribute rather than a passing accident?

Refuting the Concept of Ḥāl (Intermediate State)
Sanusi refutes the concept of ḥāl and affirms no intermediary between existence and non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verifiers (al-muḥaqqiqūn) reject the concept of an intermediate state between existence and non-existence, and how this refutation underpins the reality of attributes.

Core Idea
There is no third category: everything is either existent or non-existent.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a subtle objection about the status of attributes. Some argued that attributes are additional to bodies but not fully existent—they are in a middle state. Sanusi presents the decisive response of the verifiers: the very notion of an intermediate state is impossible. This lesson will show you why the argument fails and what it means for the reality of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the objection and response.
Pay attention to the logical flow: statement, objection, response.
2
Identify the key terms
Note 'ḥāl', 'verifiers', and 'intermediary'. Understand their roles in the argument.
3
Analyze the logic
The objection claims attributes can be additional without existing because they are intermediate. The response says no intermediate is possible. Consider why this is a knock-down argument.
If there is no third category, then anything additional must be existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المُحققون على نفي الحالِ ، وأن لا واسطة بين الموجودِ والمعدوم ] فإن قالوا : نحنُ ممَّنْ يقولُ بالحالِ والواسطة بين الوجود والعدم ، فتُسلَّمُ أنَّ للأجرام صفات زائدة عليها ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ زيادتها وجودها ؛ لاحتمال أن تكون واسطة بين الوجود والعدم قلنا : المحققونَ أَنَّ الحال محال (۲) ، وأنَّهُ لا واسطة بين الوجودِ والعدمِ
The verifiers are upon the negation of the intermediate state, and that there is no intermediary between the existent and the non-existent. ] Then if they say: We are among those who affirm the intermediate state and the intermediary between existence and non-existence, so it is conceded that bodies have attributes additional to them, but it does not follow from their addition that they exist, because it is possible that they are intermediary between existence and non-existence. We said: The verifiers hold that the intermediate state is impossible, and that there is no intermediary between existence and non-existence.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. The verifiers (al-muḥaqqiqūn) deny the concept of ḥāl, an alleged intermediate state between existence and non-existence. Some opponents argue that bodies have additional attributes (accidents) that are not fully existent but in a middle state, thus avoiding the conclusion that they exist. The verifiers respond that ḥāl is impossible (muḥāl) and that there is no third category; everything is either existent or non-existent. Therefore, if attributes are additional, they must be existent, undermining the objection.

Key Terms
حال (intermediate state)
مُحقِّقون (verifiers)
واسطة بين الوجود والعدم (intermediary between existence and non-existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that attributes can be additional to a substance without being fully existent, existing in an intermediate state between existence and non-existence.
Correction: The verifiers hold that such an intermediate state is impossible; everything is either existent or non-existent, so if attributes are additional, they must exist.

Checkpoint

A) It is possible.
B) It is impossible.
C) It is necessary.
D) It is debated.

Key Takeaway
The verifiers reject the concept of an intermediate state between existence and non-existence, affirming that everything is either existent or non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

For instance, do you claim to trust in Allah (tawakkul) but still rely heavily on your own plans? How can you truly live out that trust without a middle ground?
?
The verifiers insist on clear-cut distinctions. Think of a belief or action in your life that you have been keeping in a 'maybe' or 'sort of' state—not fully accepting it as true, not fully rejecting it. How does this lesson challenge you to decide definitively? Commit to one decision today.

The Self-Refuting Argument for the Existence of Accidents
Sanusi presents a self-refutation argument against denying accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the denial of accidents is self-refuting because the denial itself must be an accident.

Core Idea
Denying accidents is self-refuting because your denial is itself an accident.

This passage presents a clever argument from later theologians to refute those who deny the existence of accidents. The argument forces the opponent into a dilemma: either his denial exists or it does not, and in either case the existence of accidents is proven.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentative structure come through before you interpret.
Notice the dilemma structure: 'either... or...'
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The passage presents two possibilities: either the opponent's denial of accidents exists, or it does not. Locate these two options in the text.
3
Examine the first horn: denial does not exist
If the opponent says his denial does not exist, he becomes irrational—like one who speaks and then denies speaking. This removes the need for a response.
The author gives two reasons why the response is not needed.
4
Examine the second horn: denial exists
If the opponent concedes his denial exists, then that denial is an additional attribute beyond his essence—exactly what is meant by 'accident.' Thus he admits accidents exist.
5
Conclude the argument
Both options lead to the conclusion that accidents exist. The opponent cannot escape.
This is a classic self-refutation argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أذكياء المتأخرين في جواب مَنْ منع وجود الأعراض : نزاعكم لنا وقولكم : ( لا نُسلّم وجودَ الأعراض ) إمَّا أَنْ تقولوا : إِنَّ هذا النزاع منكم لنا موجود ، أو معدوم ؛ فإن قلتُم : ( لا وجود له ) خرجتُم عن طور العقلاء ، وسقطَتْ عنا وظيفة جوابكم من وجهين : أحدهما : أنَّكم فى عِدادِ مَنْ لا عقل له ؛ لأنَّ مَنْ لا عقل له هو الذي يقولُ كلاماً ثم يردفُهُ على الفور بقولِهِ : ما قلتُ شيئاً ، ومَنْ لا عقل له لا يُحتاج إلى جوابه · وثانيهما : إقراركم بأنكم لم تنازعونا ولا خالفتمونا ، فقد كفيتمونا مؤنة جوابكم . وإنْ سلَّمتُم أنَّ نزاعكم لنا وُجِدَ منكم .. فلا شك أنَّ ذلك النزاع أمرٌ زائد على الذاتِ، وهو الذي نعني بالعرض، فقد سلَّمتُم وجود العرضِ .
The clever ones among the later scholars, in answer to one who denies the existence of accidents: Your dispute with us and your statement: 'We do not concede the existence of accidents' – either you say: this dispute of yours with us exists, or it does not exist. If you say: 'It has no existence', you have left the scope of rational people, and the duty of answering you falls away from us in two respects: First, that you are counted among those who have no intellect; because one who has no intellect is he who says something and then immediately follows it with his statement: 'I said nothing', and one who has no intellect does not require a response. And second, your admission that you have not disputed with us nor opposed us, so you have spared us the trouble of answering you. And if you concede that your dispute with us exists from you, then there is no doubt that that dispute is an additional thing beyond the essence, and that is what we mean by accident; thus you have conceded the existence of the accident.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a polemical argument refuting those who deny the existence of accidents (الأعراض). The author, likely summarizing the clever response of later theologians, presents a dilemma. He addresses the opponent who says 'We do not concede the existence of accidents.' The argument proceeds by forcing the opponent to admit either that his own denial exists or does not exist. If he says his denial has no existence, he undermines his own rationality because he is speaking and then immediately contradicting himself, thus he is like someone with no intellect. Moreover, by saying his denial has no existence, he admits he never actually disputed, so there is no need to answer him. If, on the other hand, he concedes that his dispute exists, then he admits that there is something additional to his essence (a quality of disputing), which is precisely what is meant by 'accident' (عارِض). Therefore, he has implicitly conceded the existence of accidents. The argument is clever and leverages the opponent's own act of denial as proof of the category he denies.

Key Terms
عرض (accident)
ذات (essence)
نزاع (dispute)
وجود (existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying accidents is a logically sound position that does not lead to contradiction.
Correction: The denial itself is an attribute (accident) of the denier, so the denial is self-refuting.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what happens if the denier of accidents says his denial does not exist?
A) He admits accidents exist.
B) He is considered irrational and his response is not needed.
C) He proves that accidents are eternal.
D) He must provide a different argument.

Key Takeaway
The existence of accidents is undeniable because the very act of denying them is itself an accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue against a belief, have you ever considered that your act of arguing itself might undermine your position? How can you apply Sanusi's logical test to your own deeply held views today?

The opponent is forced to admit that his denial exists as an accident. Are there any claims you make that, if examined closely, contradict the very act of making them? What would it take for you to honestly acknowledge that inconsistency?
?
This argument shows that denying accidents is self-refuting because the denial itself is an accident. In what areas of your life do you unknowingly rely on something you claim does not exist? How will you actively search for such contradictions in your own thinking?

Refuting the Intermediate State and the Concessive Proof
Sanusi refutes the intermediate state and gives a concessive proof for creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the concept of an intermediate state (al-ḥāl) between existence and non-existence, and how he provides a concessive argument that even if such states existed, the proof for the temporality of the world remains intact.

Core Idea
Even if states existed, they would still prove the world's creation.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a sophisticated objection: that bodies have attributes that are neither existent nor non-existent but in an intermediate 'state'. He first denies such states outright, then goes further: even if we grant their existence, they still necessitate the temporality of bodies. This clever concessive argument shows the strength of the kalam proof for creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of argument.
Notice the two parts: the refutation and the concessive argument.
2
Identify the objection and responses
Find where the objectors speak (فإن قالوا) and where Sanusi gives his first response (قلنا) and then the concessive response (سلَّمنا).
The concessive response is the key insight.
3
Understand the concessive argument
Sanusi concedes the intermediate for argument's sake but shows it still requires temporality of bodies. See why the attributes' fixedness alone suffices.
This shows the proof is robust.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Sanusi says the only way to harm the proof is to deny the attributes entirely, which is impossible. Note the pragmatic note about unnecessary debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المحققون على نفي الحالِ ، وأن لا واسطة بين الموجودِ والمعدوم ] فإن قالوا : نحنُ ممَّنْ يقولُ بالحالِ والواسطة بين الوجود والعدم ، فتُسلَّمُ أنَّ للأجرام صفات زائدة عليها ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ زيادتها وجودها ؛ لاحتمال أن تكون واسطة بين الوجود والعدم . قلنا : المحققونَ أَنَّ الحال محال ، وأنَّهُ لا واسطة بين الوجودِ والعدمِ . سلَّمنا ثبوت الواسطة ، فيلزمُ أنَّ الأجرام تلازم صفاتٍ ثابتةً وجبَ لها الحدوث ، فيلزم حدوثها ضرورةً ، فقد تم البرهان على حدوثِ العالم على أكمل وجهِ بمُجرَّدِ ثبوتِ هذهِ الصفاتِ ، وإن لم تنته إلى درجة الوجودِ ، فالقدح بعدم وجودِها مع تسليم ثبوتها لا يضرُّ شيئاً في دليل الحدوثِ ، وإِنَّما يضرُّ بالدليل الإصرار على عدمها ، وهو باطل على الضرورة ، وقد أطال المتكلمون معهم في الاستدلال على وجودها مِنْ غيرِ حاجةٍ إليهِ أصلاً
The investigators are upon the negation of the state (al-ḥāl), and that there is no intermediate between the existent and the non-existent. For if they say: 'We are among those who affirm the state and the intermediate between existence and non-existence, so it is conceded that bodies have attributes additional to them, and it does not follow from their addition that they exist, because it is possible that they be an intermediate between existence and non-existence.' We say: The investigators hold that the state is impossible (muḥāl), and that there is no intermediate between existence and non-existence. We concede the existence of the intermediate, then it follows that bodies are inseparable from fixed attributes for which temporality (al-ḥudūth) is necessary, so their temporality necessarily follows. Thus the proof for the creation of the world has been completed in the most perfect manner merely by the affirmation of these attributes, even if they do not reach the degree of existence. So the objection by their non-existence, while conceding their fixedness, does not harm the proof of temporality at all. Rather, the proof is harmed by insisting on their non-existence, and that is false by necessity. And the theologians have prolonged with them in proving their existence without any need for that at all.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage addresses the concept of 'the state' (الحال) and the claim that there is an intermediate state between existence and non-existence. The investigators (المحققون) deny the state and assert no intermediate exists. The objectors claim they affirm the state, so bodies have additional attributes (like whiteness, etc.) that are not fully existent but 'states'. The author's first answer is to reiterate the position of the investigators: the state is impossible, and no intermediate exists. Then the author makes a concessive argument: even if we concede the existence of such an intermediate, it still follows that bodies are inseparably connected to these fixed attributes. And these attributes necessitate temporality (they are changeable), so the bodies themselves must be temporal (created). Thus the proof for the temporality of the world is complete, even if those attributes are not fully existent. Criticizing the proof by saying those attributes are not existent does not harm it, because the proof only requires them to be fixed. The only way to harm the proof is to insist that the attributes are non-existent entirely, but that is impossible by rational necessity. The author notes that theologians have unnecessarily prolonged argument about existence of these attributes.

Key Terms
حال (state)
واسطة (intermediate)
حدوث (temporality)
أجرام (bodies)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that an intermediate state between existence and non-existence would undermine the proof for the world's creation.
Correction: Sanusi shows that even if such states existed, they are still attributes that necessitate temporality, so the proof remains intact.

Checkpoint
What is the position of the investigators (المحققون) regarding the state (الحال)?
A) They affirm it as an intermediate.
B) They deny it and say there is no intermediate.
C) They remain silent.
D) They consider it necessary for bodies.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi denies the concept of an intermediate state, but even if it is granted, the attributes of bodies necessitate their temporality, thus the proof for creation remains valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that even if you grant a doubtful premise, the truth still stands firm. What concessions have you made in your personal faith that have actually weakened your certainty in the Creator? Are there half-beliefs or comfortable positions you need to abandon to stand on solid ground?
?
Sanusi did not insist on proving the existence of attributes; he used only their fixedness. Do you demand fully visible, tangible evidence for God, or can you be satisfied with the logical necessity that creation points to a Creator? What does your impatience with invisible proofs say about the state of your heart?
?
When you observe the changing attributes of your own body, like aging, moods, and health, do you actively recall that these changes prove that you are not self-sufficient but need a Creator every moment? Or does this truth pass you by without transforming your daily awareness? How can you embed this proof into your daily life so that it strengthens your conviction every day?

Refuting the Theory of Latency and Manifestation
Sanusi refutes the theory of latency and manifestation by showing it leads to contradiction and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two refutations Sanusi uses against the theory of latency and manifestation: the conjunction of opposites and the dilemma leading to infinite regress.

Core Idea
Latency theory self-destructs: it either conjoints opposites or triggers infinite regress.

In this passage, Sanusi answers the second objection to the proof of accidents. The objectors claimed that accidents can be latent and then manifest, avoiding the need for accidents to be continuously created. Sanusi shows this leads to the conjunction of opposites and infinite regress, thus refuting the theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the argument's structure emerge.
Notice the use of two 'if' conditions.
2
Identify the two refutation points
Find where Sanusi presents the first refutation (conjunction of opposites) and the second (dilemma leading to regress).
3
Understand the first refutation
Sanusi argues that if rest is latent in a moving substance, then motion and rest coexist at the same time in the same locus, which is impossible.
This uses the principle that opposites cannot coexist.
4
Understand the second refutation
Sanusi presents a dilemma: either the alternating states (latency and manifestation) cease to exist when the other appears (violating their own principle), or both are latent/manifest simultaneously, leading to infinite regress.
Both horns are absurd.
5
Reflect on the overall refutation
Consider why this argument successfully refutes the theory of latency and manifestation. How does it preserve the correct position on accidents?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الثاني ؛ وهو ادّعاءُ الكُمونِ والظُهورِ : أَنَّهُ يؤدّي إلى اجتماع الضدَّينِ في المحل الواحدِ ؛ لأنَّ الجوهر إذا تحرَّكَ والسكونُ كامن فيه زمن حركتِهِ . . اجتمعَ الضدَّانِ فِيهِ ضرورةً ! وأيضاً فالكُمونُ والظُّهورُ اللذان قاما بالعرض ويتعاقبانِ عليهِ ؛ إنْ كانَ ينعدم أحدهما عند وجود الآخر .. فقد نقضوا أصلهم في كمون الأعراض ، ولزمهم ما فرُّوا منه ؛ وهو ملازمة الجواهر للحوادثِ ، وإن قالوا بكمونهما وظهورهما أيضاً لزم التسلسل .
The answer to the second [objection] — which is the claim of latency (al-kumūn) and manifestation (al-ẓuhūr): That it leads to the conjunction of two opposites in one locus; because if a substance (jawhar) moves while rest is latent in it during the time of its motion, then the two opposites necessarily come together in it. And also, latency and manifestation, which inhere in the accident and alternate upon it: if one of them becomes non-existent upon the existence of the other, then they have violated their own principle concerning the latency of accidents, and that which they fled from becomes incumbent upon them — namely, the inseparability of substances from temporally originated things. And if they say that both are latent and manifest as well, then an infinite regress (tasalsul) follows.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the second objection, which claims that accidents are latent (كُمون) and then manifest (ظهور). The author refutes it in two ways. First, the theory leads to the conjunction of opposites (اجتماع الضدين) in the same locus. For example, if a substance moves while rest is latent within it during the motion, then opposite qualities (motion and rest) coexist in the same substance at the same time, which is impossible. Second, the alternation of latency and manifestation in an accident: if one becomes nonexistent when the other appears, then the objectors have abandoned their own principle of latency of accidents (because they admit that the latent state can cease to exist). This would force them to accept what they were trying to avoid: that substances are inseparable from temporally originated accidents (ملازمة الجواهر للحوادث). If they instead claim that both states (latency and manifestation) are simultaneously latent and manifest, that leads to an infinite regress (التسلسل), which is also impossible. Thus the theory is refuted.

Key Terms
كُمون (latency)
ظُهور (manifestation)
اجتماع الضدين (conjunction of opposites)
تسلسل (infinite regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The theory of latency avoids the problem of accidents requiring continuous creation.
Correction: The theory leads to conjunction of opposites or infinite regress, which are greater absurdities.

Checkpoint
What is one consequence of the theory of latency and manifestation according to the argument?
A) It proves the existence of a creator.
B) It leads to the conjunction of opposites.
C) It explains the transmission of accidents.
D) It avoids infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The theory of latency and manifestation is refuted because it entails either the conjunction of opposites or an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a contradiction between two beliefs you hold, do you tend to twist definitions or ignore the evidence to preserve comfort, like the proponents of latency? What would it cost you to accept the logical conclusion instead?
?
Is there a 'hidden' sin or harmful habit you keep latent in your heart, hoping it won't manifest? How does this lesson's warning that latency leads to greater absurdities challenge you to expose it now?
?
The objectors tried to avoid one problem (substances inheriting accidents) but fell into worse ones. In your spiritual life, are you avoiding a small obedience out of fear of difficulty, only to fall into a greater disobedience? What first step can you take today to correct that?

Why Kumun and Zuhur Fail: Sanusi's Refutation
Sanusi refutes the objection of latency and manifestation by showing it leads to impossible consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the theory of latency and manifestation (kumun and zuhur) is rationally impossible because it leads to the conjunction of opposites or infinite regress.

Core Idea
The theory of latency and manifestation cannot escape the problem of accidents.

Sanusi now addresses the second objection raised against the argument from accidents: the claim that accidents can be latent within substances and then manifest. He shows that this theory is self-defeating by demonstrating that it necessarily leads to either the impossible conjunction of opposites or an infinite regress. Let's examine his reasoning carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the logical structure: the author presents two consequences of the theory.
The passage is a refutation of the second objection.
2
Identify the first argument
The first argument shows that if stillness is latent in a moving substance, then opposites (motion and stillness) coexist in the same locus, which is impossible.
This is a direct contradiction of the law of non-contradiction.
3
Identify the second argument
The second argument examines the alternation of latency and manifestation in an accident. It presents a dilemma: either one vanishes (violating their own principle) or both remain (leading to infinite regress).
The dilemma forces the opponent into an impossible position.
4
Reflect on the overall refutation
Consider why this refutation is effective: it shows that the theory cannot avoid the very problem it was designed to solve—the dependence of substances on accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الثاني ؛ وهو ادّعاءُ الكُمونِ والظُهورِ : أَنَّهُ يؤدّي إلى اجتماع الضدَّينِ في المحل الواحدِ ؛ لأنَّ الجوهر إذا تحرَّكَ والسكونُ كامن فيه زمن حركتِهِ . . اجتمعَ الضدَّانِ  ِفيهِ ضرورةً ! وأيضاً فالكُمونُ والظُّهورُ اللذان قاما بالعرض ويتعاقبانِ عليهِ ؛ إنْ كانَ ينعدم أحدهما عند وجود الآخر .. فقد نقضوا أصلهم في كمون الأعراض ، ولزمهم ما فرُّوا منه ؛ وهو ملازمة الجواهر للحوادثِ ، وإن قالوا بكمونهما وظهورهما أيضاً لزم التسلسل .
The answer to the second [objection], which is the claim of latency (kumūn) and manifestation (ẓuhūr): that it leads to the conjunction of two opposites in one locus, because when a substance (jawhar) moves while stillness is latent in it during the time of its movement, then the two opposites necessarily meet in it. Also, the latency and manifestation that inhere in the accident (ʿaraḍ) and alternate upon it: if one of them becomes nonexistent at the existence of the other, then they have violated their own principle regarding the latency of accidents, and they are compelled to what they fled from, namely the necessary connection of substances to temporal events. And if they say that both remain latent and manifest, then an infinite regress (tasalsul) necessarily follows.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the second objection, which is the theory of latency (kumūn) and manifestation (ẓuhūr). The author presents two arguments. First, if a substance moves while stillness is latent within it during the motion, then opposites—motion and stillness—coexist in the same locus, which is impossible. Second, consider the alternation of latency and manifestation in an accident. If one vanishes when the other appears, then the opponents violate their own principle that accidents remain latent. This forces them to accept the very conclusion they sought to avoid: that substances are never free from accidents. If they instead claim both latency and manifestation persist together, an infinite regress (tasalsul) follows because each state would require another state to contain it.

Key Terms
الكُمون
الظهور
الضدان
التسلسل
العرض
الجوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The theory of kumun and zuhur successfully explains how substances can be free from accidents.
Correction: Sanusi shows it fails because it leads to impossible consequences like conjunction of opposites or infinite regress.

Checkpoint
What is one consequence of the theory of kumun and zuhur according to this passage?
A) It proves the eternity of the world
B) It leads to the conjunction of two opposites in one locus
C) It avoids infinite regress
D) It denies the existence of accidents

Key Takeaway
The theory of latency and manifestation is rationally untenable because it either forces the coexistence of opposites or leads to an infinite regress.

?

What step can you take today to stop fleeing and embrace the reality this lesson demands?

Why Accidents Cannot Be Transmitted
Sanusi argues that transmission of accidents would reverse their essence and lead to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that accidents cannot be transmitted from one locus to another or become self-subsistent, and how this preserves the definition of an accident.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot transmit; doing so would reverse their essence and lead to infinite regress.

Sanusi continues his refutation of objections to the contingency of the world. Here he addresses the possibility of accidents moving between substances or becoming self-subsistent. Using motion as a case study, he shows that such ideas contradict the very definition of an accident and lead to absurd consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then its literal translation. Note the two objections being addressed: transmission of accidents and switching between self-subsistence and inherence.
The passage is concise; each sentence carries logical weight.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage presents two arguments against transmission: (1) reversal of essence (ḥaqīqa), and (2) infinite regress (tasalsul). Identify where each begins.
The example of motion is used for the first argument.
3
Understand the reversal of essence argument
Sanusi says motion's essence is the transfer of a substance. If motion itself were transmitted or self-subsistent, it would no longer be what it is — a reversal of its essence. Reflect on why this is a conceptual contradiction.
This argument relies on the definition of an accident as something that inheres in a substance.
4
Understand the infinite regress argument
If an accident could transmit, then that act of transmission would itself be an accident requiring a locus, leading to an endless chain of accidents inhering in accidents. This is absurd (impossible).
The phrase 'قيام المعنى بالمعنى' (inherence of a meaning in a meaning) is a hallmark of absurdity in kalam.
5
Connect to the bigger picture
This passage safeguards the definition of accidents as non-self-subsistent attributes that cannot migrate, which is crucial for proving the contingency of the world and the existence of a Creator.
These objections likely come from philosophers or theologians who denied the standard Ashʿarī view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الثالث ؛ وهو انتقالُ الأعراضِ مِنْ محلَّ إلى محلّ وعن الرابع ؛ وهو انتقالها من قيام بنفسها إلى قيام بمحل وبالعكس : أنَّ كلّاً مِنَ الأمرين يؤدّي إلى قلْبِ حقيقةِ العرضِ ؛ فإِنَّ الحركة مثلاً حقيقتها انتقال جوهرٍ مِنْ حيّز إلى حيز ، فلو قامَتْ بنفسها أو انتقلَتْ هي .. لزم قلبُ هذه الحقيقة . وأيضاً : لو انتقلَتْ لزمَ قيامُ انتقال بها ، وذلك الانتقال ينتقل أيضاً، فيقومُ بهِ انتقال ؛ وذلك يؤدي إلى التسلسل، وقيام المعنى بالمعنى .
The answer to the third [objection], which is the transmission of accidents (intiqāl al-aʿrāḍ) from one locus to another, and to the fourth [objection], which is their transmission from self-subsistence to inherence in a locus and vice versa: that each of these two matters leads to reversing the essence (ḥaqīqa) of the accident. For example, motion's essence is the transfer of a substance from one space to another. So if it were self-subsistent or if it itself transmitted, then this essence would be reversed. Also, if it transmitted, then a transmission would inhere in it, and that transmission would also transmit, so a transmission would inhere in it, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul) and the inherence of a meaning in a meaning.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage addresses two related objections: (3) that accidents can be transmitted from one locus to another, and (4) that accidents can switch between self-subsistence and inherence in a locus. The author argues that both violate the essence of an accident. Taking motion as an example, its essence is the transfer of a substance from one space to another. If motion were self-subsistent or if it could itself be transmitted (i.e., move from one substance to another), then motion would cease to be what it is—a transfer of a substance. Furthermore, if motion could transmit, then that act of transmission would itself be an accident in need of a locus, and that transmission would also transmit, leading to an infinite chain of accidents inhering in accidents, which is absurd.

Key Terms

قلب الحقيقة

Misconception: Accidents can be transferred from one substance to another, or can exist without a substance.
Correction: Such ideas contradict the very definition of an accident as a non-self-subsistent attribute that inheres in a substance. Using motion as an example, Sanusi shows that transmission would reverse motion's essence and lead to infinite regress, both of which are impossible.

Key Takeaway

Definitions of Dawr and Tasalsul
Sanusi defines circularity and infinite regress and notes their prior proven impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of circular reasoning (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul) as technical terms in kalam, and why both are considered impossible.

Core Idea
Circularity and infinite regress are two logical impossibilities that kalam arguments reject.

Sanusi now clarifies two key logical concepts that are frequently invoked in kalam proofs: circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul). These are types of impossible chains of dependence. Understanding their precise definitions is essential before we can apply the proof of their impossibility, which has already been given earlier.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the definitions. Then read the literal translation to understand the meaning precisely.
Notice the concise style of the fa'idah (benefit).
2
Identify the definition of dawr
Find the part that defines dawr: it is the dependence of a thing upon that which it depends upon, either directly or indirectly. This is a circular dependency.
The phrase 'either in one degree or in multiple degrees' covers both direct and indirect circularity.
3
Identify the definition of tasalsul
Find the part that defines tasalsul: it is the arrangement of infinite matters, i.e., an unending linear chain.
Unlike dawr, there is no return to the starting point.
4
Reflect on the impossibility
Sanusi states that the proof of their impossibility has preceded. This means the earlier argument (likely from lesson 2) already established that such chains are impossible.
This lesson is a definitional summary, not a new proof.

Source Text
