Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a brief benefit (fa'idah) providing technical definitions of two key logical terms used in kalam arguments: dawr (circular reasoning) and tasalsul (infinite regress). Dawr is defined as a thing depending on that which it depends on, either directly (one step) or through multiple steps (circular chain). Tasalsul is defined as an arrangement of infinite matters, i.e., an unending chain of causes or terms. The author notes that the proof of their impossibility has already been presented earlier in the text.

Definitions of Circularity and Infinite Regress
Sanusi defines circularity and infinite regress and notes their impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul) and why they are considered impossible in kalam reasoning.

Core Idea
Circularity and infinite regress are logical impossibilities that break causal chains.

Sanusi now introduces two technical terms that are fundamental to many kalam arguments: dawr (circularity) and tasalsul (infinite regress). Understanding these definitions is essential before examining proofs that rely on their impossibility. This passage gives concise definitions and notes that their impossibility has already been demonstrated.

2

The Impossibility of Union (Ittihad) and Its Proof
Sanusi defines union and proves its absolute impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of union (ittihad) and why it is rationally impossible, along with the three-case proof that demonstrates its impossibility.

Core Idea
Union is absolutely impossible in every conceivable scenario.

Sanusi continues his argument by showing that since God cannot be in need of a locus, union with a locus is also impossible. He defines union as two things becoming one, then provides a rational proof that covers every possibility, proving that union cannot occur between any two things — whether eternal or contingent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the logical structure.
2
Understand the definition of union
Note that union means two things becoming one; this is the key concept.
3
Analyze the three-case proof
Examine the exhaustive cases: both remain, both vanish, one vanishes. See why each fails to produce union.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا استحال افتقارُهُ إلى محلَّ استحال اتحاده به ، ومعنى الاتحاد : صيرورة الشيئين شيئاً واحداً ، وهو محال مطلقاً في القديم والحادثِ وبرهانُهُ : أنَّ أحدَ الشيئين إذا اتَّحد بالآخرِ : فإن بقيا على حالهما فهما اثنان لا واحدٌ ، فلا اتحاد ، وإِنْ عُدِما كانَ الموجود غيرهما ، وإِنْ عُدِمَ أحدهما دونَ الآخرِ امتنع الاتحاد ؛ لأنَّ المعدومَ لا يكون عينَ الموجود .
And if it is impossible for Him to be in need of a locus, then it is impossible for Him to unite with it. The meaning of union (al-ittihad) is two things becoming one thing. It is absolutely impossible in the eternal and the contingent. Its proof: that if one of two things unites with the other: either they both remain as they are — then they are two, not one, so there is no union; or both cease to exist — then what exists is other than them; or one of them ceases without the other — then union is impossible, because the non-existent cannot be identical with the existent.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of union (ittihad) and proves its absolute impossibility. The author begins by linking the impossibility of God's need for a locus (mahall) to the impossibility of His union with one. He then defines union as two things becoming one thing. He asserts that union is impossible in all cases, whether the things are eternal or contingent. The proof consists of three exhaustive possibilities when two things are said to unite: (1) they both remain as they were — in which case they are still two, so no union occurred; (2) they both cease to exist — then what exists is something entirely new, not union; (3) one ceases while the other remains — then the non-existent cannot be identical with the existent, so union is impossible. Thus union is logically impossible in all scenarios.

Key Terms
اتحاد
محل
قديم
حادث
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think union means a mixture of two things into a third, preserving both in a new form.
Correction: The proof shows that if both remain, they are still two; if both vanish, a third thing appears; if one vanishes, the non-existent cannot be identical to the existent. Thus no form of union is possible.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of union (ittihad) according to the author?
A) Two things becoming one thing
B) One thing subsisting in another
C) The merging of two substances
D) The annihilation of both things

Key Takeaway
Union is rationally impossible in all cases, as proven by the exhaustive three-case argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Refutation of the Unionists' Argument from Lack of Evidence
The author argues absence of proof for union does not prove non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the absence of proof for a claim does not logically disprove it, illustrated by the refutation of the unionist argument.

Core Idea
Absence of proof is not proof of absence.

This passage tackles a common logical fallacy often exploited by proponents of heretical doctrines. The unionists argue that since there is no clear proof that God unites with His creation, such union must be impossible. Imam al-Sanusi refutes this by reminding us of a fundamental logical principle: a proof necessitates its implication (tard), but not its converse ('aks). In other words, lack of evidence for X does not entail the non-existence of X. Let's examine this concise but powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then the translation. Notice the logical terms tard and 'aks.
The passage is short but dense with logical reasoning.
2
Identify the logical principle
The author states a consensus: a proof requires its direct implication (tard), not its converse ('aks). Understand what tard and 'aks mean in logic.
Tard means that if the proof is true, the conclusion follows. 'Aks means the reverse: if the conclusion is true, the proof would be true. The principle says only tard is necessary.
3
See the application to the unionist claim
The unionists claim that because there is no proof of union, union does not exist. The author points out that this is using the converse ('aks) fallacy.
4
Note the scornful conclusion
The author shows the absurd consequence: if this fallacy were valid, lowly creatures like beetles could be considered gods. This highlights the error.
5
Reflect on why this fallacy is common
Think about modern debates where people say 'there's no evidence, so it's false.' This lesson guards against that error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل الاتحادِ ، وبإجماع أرباب العقول أنَّ الدليل يلزم طردُهُ لا عكشه ، فلا يلزم إذاً مِنْ عدمِ دليل الاتحادِ في هذه الحوادثِ عدم المدلول اتحاد الكلمة بها ، وما أخسَّ مذهباً يُفضي إلى تجويز تكونَ الخنفساء والجُعَلُ وغيرهما آلهة !
The proof of union, and by consensus of the intellects, that the proof necessitates its implication (tard) not its converse ('aks). So it does not necessarily follow from the absence of proof of union in these events the non-existence of the intended union of the Word with them. And how base a doctrine that leads to permitting that the dung beetle, the beetle, and others could be gods!
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage refutes a logical error made by proponents of union. The author invokes a consensus that a proof requires its direct implication (tard) not its converse ('aks). In other words, the absence of a proof for union does not logically entail that the union does not exist. The author then scornfully notes the absurd consequences of such fallacious reasoning: it would allow lowly creatures like beetles to be considered gods.

Key Terms
دليل
طرد
عكس
اتحاد
مدلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If there is no proof for something, it must not exist.
Correction: The logical principle is that a proof implies its conclusion (tard), but the absence of proof does not imply the absence of the conclusion. This fallacy is called 'denying the antecedent' or confusing converse with implication.

Checkpoint
What logical principle does the author use to refute the unionists' argument?
A) A proof requires its converse ('aks), not its implication (tard).
B) A proof requires its implication (tard), not its converse ('aks).
C) A proof requires both implication and converse equally.
D) A proof has no logical constraints.

Key Takeaway
Absence of proof for a claim does not logically disprove that claim; a proof only necessitates its direct implication, not its converse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you dismissed a truth claim simply because you lacked evidence for it? How will you guard against this logical error in your search for knowledge about God?
?
The unionists' flawed reasoning led them to absurd conclusions about God. Is there any area of your beliefs where you are relying on 'absence of proof' as 'proof of absence'? What specific step will you take to correct that?
?
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum to expose the foolishness of the unionists. Are you willing to apply the same logical rigor to your own assumptions, even when they are comfortable? What is one assumption you will examine today?

Understanding 'Fadlan' in Kalam Discourse
The commentator explains the parsing and semantic range of 'fadlan' and its use in the Aqida.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical function and semantic role of the word 'fadlan' in Arabic theological discourse, particularly how it indicates the impossibility of a higher item while deeming the lower item improbable.

Core Idea
'Fadlan' marks the lower item as improbable and the higher as impossible.

The word 'fadlan' may seem like a simple connector, but it carries a precise logical function in kalam texts. In this passage, the commentator unpacks its grammatical root and its role in negations, showing how it establishes a hierarchy of impossibility. Let's dive into the details.

Guided Path
1
Parse 'fadlan' as a verbal noun
Begin by understanding that 'fadlan' is derived from the verb 'fadala' meaning 'remained', and it functions as a verbal noun referring to a preceding negation.
2
Identify its position in a sentence
Notice that 'fadlan' always occurs between a negation and an affirmation, either explicitly or implicitly, as shown in the examples.
3
Grasp its dual semantic function
Understand that 'fadlan' makes the lower item (the one negated) improbable, and the higher item (the one after 'an') impossible.
This is the core logical role.
4
Avoid the common mistake
Learn that 'fadlan' does not mean 'transcending' (tajawuzan), as some mistakenly claim, but rather indicates impossibility of the higher item.
Al-Taftazani confirms this.

And (fadlan) is a verbal noun of a deleted verb, i.e., fadala fadlan, meaning 'remained'. Its pronoun refers to the denial or negation understood from what preceded it, because it occurs between a negation and a confirmation, either verbally — like 'So-and-so does not look at the poor, let alone (fadlan ‘an) give to him' — or semantically — like 'Aspirations fall short of the lowest number, let alone (fadlan ‘an) rising above it'; i.e., they did not reach it, let alone surpass it. And similar is the wording of the 'Aqida, for its meaning is: pre-eternity was not described by positiveness, let alone the most specific character. And the intention is to deem the lower — i.e., that which negation entered — far-fetched [from occurrence], like looking at the poor and reaching the aspirations in the two examples, and to deem what is above it — i.e., that which 'an entered — impossible, considering it as something impossible that cannot occur, like giving and rising above in those examples. Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and semantic explanation of the word 'fadlan' (فضلاً) as used in the author's 'Aqida poem. The author first parses 'fadlan' as a verbal noun (masdar) of a deleted verb, meaning 'remained' (baqiya). The pronoun attached to it refers to the negation understood from the context. He then explains that 'fadlan' is used as a middle term between a negation and a confirmation, either explicitly in wording or implicitly in meaning. He gives two examples: one verbal ('He does not look at the poor, let alone give to him') and one semantic ('Aspirations fall short of the lowest number, let alone rising above it'). The function of 'fadlan' is to deem the lower item (the one negated) far-fetched from occurring, and to deem the higher item (the one following 'an') impossible. The meaning in the 'Aqida is that pre-eternity is not even described by positiveness, so it is far-fetched that it be characterized by the most specific attribute. He then clarifies that 'fadlan' does not mean 'transcending' (tajawuzan); rather, the correct interpretation is that the lower item is deemed improbable and the higher item impossible, as stated by al-Taftazani in his gloss on al-Kashshaf.

Key Terms
فضلاً (fadlan)
مصدر (masdar)
نفي (nafy)
إثبات (ithbat)
استبعاد (istib‘ad)
استحالة (istihala)
تفتازاني (Taftazani)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'fadlan' means 'transcending' and that the improbable item in examples like 'He does not look at the poor, let alone give to him' is the lack of looking or the falling short.
Correction: In reality, 'fadlan' makes the lower item (looking) improbable and the higher item (giving) impossible. The lack of looking is not the improbable item; rather, looking itself is deemed improbable, and giving is impossible.

Checkpoint

A) It means 'transcending' and indicates that giving is more important.
B) It deems the lower item (looking) far-fetched and the higher item (giving) impossible.
C) It connects two equal possibilities.
D) It emphasizes that both looking and giving are impossible.

Key Takeaway
'Fadlan' functions as a grammatical particle that indicates the lower item is improbable while the higher item is impossible, serving as a powerful rhetorical tool in theological negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make statements about what Allah cannot or does not do, are you aware of the precise implications of the words you use? How does understanding 'fadlan' change how you will speak about divine attributes?

The 'fadlan' construction forces us to think in terms of levels: improbable lower, impossible higher. Where in your own life have you settled for an improbable excuse when you should have faced an impossible challenge? How can this lesson sharpen your honesty?

Use of 'Fadlan' in Arabic Between Negation and Affirmation
Sanusi explains the linguistic function of 'fadlan' with examples and applies it to 'al-ʿaqīda'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise semantic function of the Arabic word 'fadlan' in establishing a hierarchy of negation and impossibility, and how this applies to theological terms like 'al-ʿaqīda'.

Core Idea
'Fadlan' builds a logical ladder: the lower rung is negated, the higher rung is impossible.

This lesson examines a key Arabic word used in classical texts to express a logical progression from a denied lesser to an impossible greater. Sanusi clarifies its grammatical and semantic behavior through examples, then applies it to a theological context, correcting a common misinterpretation. Understanding 'fadlan' is essential for reading nuanced arguments in kalam and related disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the explanation, then read the literal English translation. Let the author's own examples and structure come through before analysis.
2
Identify the core concept
Notice how 'fadlan' functions: it places something mentioned before it as remote from occurrence (negated), and something after it as impossible. This is the key idea.
3
Study the examples
Look at the two examples: 'not looking at the poor, let alone giving' and 'ambitions fell short of the smallest number, let alone rising above'. See how the first part is negated and the second is impossible.
4
Apply to 'al-ʿaqīda'
See how the same structure applies to the theological context: pre-eternity is not characterized by affirmation, let alone specificity. The more specific (al-akhass) is made impossible.
5
Review the correction
Read the part where Sanusi corrects a mistaken view that 'fadlan' means just 'exceeding' and that the remote element is the lower one. Understand the correct interpretation from al-Taftazani.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنَّما يقعُ مُتوسطاً بين نفي وإثباتٍ لفظاً ؛ نحو : فلان لا ينظرُ إلى الفقير فضلاً عن إعطائِهِ ، أو معنى ؛ نحوَ : تقاصرَتِ الهمم عن أدنى العددِ فضلاً عن أن تترقَّاهُ ؛ أي: لم تبلغه فضلاً عن الترقي . ونحوه لفظُ العقيدة ؛ إذْ معناه : لم يتّصف القدمُ بالثبوتِ فضلاً عن الأخصيَّةِ ، والقصد فيه إلى استبعاد الأدنى ـ أعني : ما دخلَهُ النفي - بمعنى عده بعيداً عن الوقوع ؛ كالنظر إلى الفقير وبلوغ الهمم في المثالين ، واستحالة ما فوقه - أعني : ما دخلَتْهُ ) عن ) - بمعنى عدِّهِ بمنزلة المحالِ الذي لا يمكنُ وقوعُهُ ؛ كالإعطاء والترقي فيهما ، وهو مِنْ قولك : أنفقتُ الدراهم ، والذي فَضَلَ منها كذا ؛ أي : بقي ؛ فالمعنى في المثالينِ : انتفاء العطاء بالكليَّةِ ، والذي نُفِيَ منه عدمُ النظر ، وانتفى الترقي ، وبقي منه التقاصر . والمعنى في تركيب العقيدة : انتفَتْ في القدمِ الأخصيَّةُ وبقي منهُ عدم الثبوتِ . والأحسنُ : أَنَّهُ لا محلّ لهذه الجملة ، وإن جعلها بعضُهم حالاً ، ومِنَ الخطأ في حلّ هذا التركيب : ما يُقالُ : إِنَّ ( فضلاً ) بمعنى ( تجاوزاً ) وإنَّ المستبعدَ في المثالين هو عدم النظرِ وقصور الهمم ، قالَهُ التفتازاني في ( حاشيته على الكشاف »
It occurs intermediate between negation and affirmation in wording, like: 'So-and-so does not look at the poor, let alone (fadlan) giving him,' or in meaning, like: 'The ambitions have fallen short of the smallest number, let alone rising above it'; i.e., they did not reach it, let alone ascending. And similar is the word 'al-ʿaqīda'; since its meaning: pre-eternity was not characterized by affirmation, let alone specificity. The intent is to regard the lower — i.e., what enters under negation — as remote from occurrence, like looking at the poor and the aspirations in the two examples, and to regard what is above it — i.e., what enters under 'an' — as impossible that cannot occur, like giving and ascending in them. It is derived from your saying: 'I spent the dirhams, and what exceeded (fadala) them is such-and-such; i.e., remained.' So the meaning in the two examples: the giving is completely negated, and what is negated from it is the lack of looking, and the ascent is negated, and the falling short remains. And the meaning in the structure of al-ʿaqīda: specificity is negated in pre-eternity and from it remains the lack of affirmation. The best [opinion] is that this sentence has no grammatical position, although some make it a circumstantial clause. And from the error in analyzing this structure is what is said: that 'fadlan' means 'exceeding' and that the remote in the two examples is the lack of looking and the shortcoming of ambitions. This was said by al-Taftazani in his gloss on al-Kashshaf.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic analysis of the word 'fadlan' (فضلًا) used in Arabic to express a progression from a lesser to a greater. Sanusi explains that it occurs intermediate between negation and affirmation, establishing a hierarchy: the lower item is considered unlikely, and the higher item is considered impossible. He gives two examples: one concerning looking versus giving, and another concerning ambitions versus ascending. He then applies the same logic to the word 'al-ʿaqīda' (العقيدة) in a theological context, showing that it implies negation of the more specific (al-akhass) while leaving the lack of the less specific (al-thubut). The passage proceeds to correct a misinterpretation by some who take 'fadlan' to simply mean 'exceeding' and who mistakenly think the remote element is the lower one in the examples. Sanusi cites al-Taftazani's gloss on al-Kashshaf to support the correct understanding. The explanation follows the author's order: first the definition, then examples, then the derived meaning, then the application to 'al-ʿaqīda', then the correction of an erroneous analysis.

Key Terms
فضلًا (fadlan)
العقيدة (al-ʿaqīda)
النفي والإثبات (negation and affirmation)
القدم (pre-eternity)
الأخص (the more specific)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some interpret 'fadlan' simply as 'exceeding' and think the remote (impossible) element is the lower item (e.g., lack of looking).
Correction: Actually, 'fadlan' establishes that the lower item is negated (but possible), while the higher item is completely impossible, as per the examples and al-Taftazani's correction.

C) To mean 'exceeding' in a positive sense
D) To connect two equal statements

Key Takeaway
The word 'fadlan' functions as a logical operator linking a negated lower case to an impossible higher case, used precisely in Arabic discourse and especially in theological arguments.

Commonality in Narrower and Broader Attributes
Sanusi argues that commonality in the narrower necessitates commonality in the broader, but not vice versa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical relationship between commonality in narrower and broader attributes, and why the narrower implies the broader but not vice versa.

Core Idea
The narrower always implies the broader, but not the reverse.

Sanusi presents a precise logical distinction: when two entities share a more specific attribute (like rationality), they must also share the more general attribute (like animality). However, sharing a general attribute does not force them to share a specific one. This distinction is crucial for understanding debates about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Pay attention to the key terms like الاشتراك, الأعم, الأخص, الملزوم, اللازم.
Notice the logical structure: two conditional statements.
2
Identify the two directions
The passage discusses two logical directions: from narrower to broader (necessary) and from broader to narrower (not necessary). Identify the examples given.
Rationality (narrower) necessitates animality (broader).
3
Understand cause vs. concomitant
The author distinguishes between the narrower being a cause (علة) versus a necessary concomitant (لازم). Understand why the opponents' mistake is treating it as a cause.
The narrower is a necessary concomitant, not a cause.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion clarifies what is denied and what is affirmed. Reflect on why this distinction matters in theological discussions.
This is a key logical tool in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاشتراك في الأعم ؛ لاستحالة وجودِ الملزوم بدون لازمه ؛ كالاشتراك في الناطقيَّةِ التي هي للإنسان أخص ؛ فإنَّهُ يلزم منه الاشتراك في الأعم الذي هو الحيوانية ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ وجودِ الاشتراك في الأعم الذاتي - كالحيوانية للإنسان مثلاً ـ وجود الاشتراك في الأخص كالناطقيَّةِ لهُ ؛ إذْ لا يلزمُ مِنْ وجودِ اللازم وجود الملزومِ والحاصلُ : أنَّ الذي ننكرُهُ عليهم جعلهم الاشتراك في الأخص عِلَّةً للاشتراك في الأعم ، أما كونه ملزوماً لهُ فمما لا شك فيهِ
Commonality in the broader is impossible without the existence of the implied (malzum) without its necessary concomitant (lazim); like commonality in rationality (natiqiyya) which is more specific (akhass) to man, for it necessitates commonality in the broader which is animality (hayawaniyya). And it is not necessary from the existence of commonality in the essential broader — like animality for man for example — the existence of commonality in the more specific like rationality for him, because the existence of the necessary concomitant does not necessitate the existence of the implied. The conclusion: what we deny them is their making commonality in the more specific a cause (illa) for commonality in the broader; as for it being a necessary concomitant of it, that is beyond doubt.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is a logical argument about the relationship between commonality in broader and narrower categories, likely in the context of attributes. The author argues that if two things share a narrower attribute (like rationality specific to humans), then they must necessarily share the broader attribute (like animality) because the narrower cannot exist without the broader. However, the reverse is not true: sharing a broader attribute does not entail sharing a narrower one. He distinguishes between the broader being a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the narrower (meaning the narrower implies the broader) versus being a cause (illa). The author denies the opponents' claim that commonality in the narrower is a cause for commonality in the broader, but concedes that it is a necessary concomitant.

الأخص
الملزوم
اللازم
الناطقيَّة
الحيوانية
علَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Commonality in a broader attribute automatically implies commonality in a narrower attribute.
Correction: The passage clarifies that commonality in the broader does not necessitate commonality in the narrower; only the reverse is true.

Key Takeaway
Commonality in a more specific attribute necessarily implies commonality in the more general attribute, but not the reverse.

The Impossibility of Devoidness from All Attributes
Sanusi proves the impossibility of an essence being devoid of all its possible attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a substrate cannot be devoid of all the kinds of attributes it can accept, and how this principle applies to both created beings and God.

Core Idea
No essence can be empty of all the attributes it can receive — neither created nor eternal.

Sanusi presents a foundational principle of Islamic theology: the impossibility of an essence being devoid of all its possible attributes. He argues that if it were possible for an essence to lack some attributes, it could lack all, which is absurd. This principle applies to both originated substances and God Himself, establishing that God's attributes are eternal and necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the structure: conditional statement, then application to two cases.
2
Understand the core argument
Grasp the conditional: if devoid of some possible then devoid of all possible is possible. But devoid of all is impossible, so devoid of some is impossible.
This is a logical argument based on equal relation.
3
Apply to created beings
Identify why substances cannot be without the genus of accidents (motion, rest, etc.). This establishes the principle for the originated.
4
Apply to God
See how the same principle requires that God cannot be without the attributes indicated by His actions (knowledge, power, will, life). This leads to the eternity of all attributes.
This is a key proof in divine attributes.

فلو جازَ خلوها عن جميع بعض أجناس صفاتها التي تقبلها لجازَ خلوها عن جميع تلك الأجناس المقبولة ضرورة استواء نسبة الجميع إليها ، لكن خلو الذاتِ عن ما تقبلُهُ مِنْ أجناس الصفاتِ محال مطلقاً ؛ أعني : في حق الحادثِ وفي حقِّ القديم، فخلوها عن بعض أجناس صفاتها التي تقبلها محال . أما استحالةُ الخلوّ عنِ الجميع في حق الحادثِ : فلانا نعلم على الضرورة استحالة عرو الجواهر عن جنس الأكوان ؛ وهي الحركة والسكون ، والاجتماع والافتراقُ ، فيجب ألا تعرى عن سائر أجناس الأعراض التي تقبلها . وأما في حق القديمِ جلَّ وعلا : فلانا نعلم قطعاً استحالة عروهِ عمَّا دلَّ عليه فعله ؛ مِنَ العَلمِ والقدرةِ والإرادة والحياة ؛ إذ لو انتفت عنه هذه الصفاتُ لاستحالَ أنْ يُوجِدَ فعلاً مِنْ أفعالِهِ ، فيلزم عدمُ المصنوعاتِ كلّها مع تحقق وجودها ، وهو محال على الضرورة ، وإذا استحال عروه عن هذهِ الصفاتِ لزم استحالة عروه عن سائر الصفاتِ التي يقبلُها ؛ لما عرفتَ مِنْ وجوب استواء نسبة المقبولة إلى الذاتِ القابلة لها ، وإذا ثبت التلازم بينَ وجودِ ذاتِهِ تعالى وبين جميع صفاتِهِ التي ينصف بها .. لزم أن تكون كلها قديمةً ؛ إذ لو كانَ شيء منها حادثاً والفَرْضُ أَنَّهُ لا يمكنُ أنْ يعرى عن صفةٍ مِنْ صفاتِهِ. . لَلزِمَ أنْ يكونَ وجود ذاتِهِ حادثاً ، لكن الحدوث على ذاتِهِ مستحيل ؛ لما عرفتَ مِنْ وجوبِ قدمِهِ ، فَفَرْضُ الحدوثِ في صفةٍ مِنْ صفاتِهِ مستحيل .
If it were permissible for it to be devoid of some of the kinds of attributes that it accepts, then it would be permissible for it to be devoid of all those accepted kinds, due to the necessity of the equality of the relation of all of them to it. But the essence being devoid of what it accepts from the kinds of attributes is absolutely impossible; I mean: in the case of the originated and in the case of the Eternal. So its being devoid of some kinds of attributes that it accepts is impossible. As for the impossibility of being devoid of all in the case of the originated: it is because we know with necessity the impossibility of substances being devoid of the genus of accidents; these are motion and rest, and conjunction and separation. So it must be that they are not devoid of the remaining kinds of accidents that they accept. And as for the case of the Eternal, may He be exalted: it is because we know with certainty the impossibility of His being devoid of what His action indicates: knowledge, power, will, and life. For if these attributes were negated from Him, it would be impossible for Him to bring into existence any of His actions; thus the non-existence of all the created things would follow despite the realization of their existence, and that is necessarily impossible. And if His being devoid of these attributes is impossible, then His being devoid of the remaining attributes that He accepts is also impossible, because of what you know of the necessity of the equality of the relation of what is accepted to the accepting essence. And if the necessary connection between the existence of His Essence, exalted is He, and all of His attributes with which He is described is established, then it follows that they are all eternal. For if any of them were originated, and the assumption is that it is impossible for Him to be devoid of any of His attributes, then it would be necessary that the existence of His essence be originated. But origination upon His essence is impossible, because of what you know of the necessity of His eternity. Thus, supposing origination in any of His attributes is impossible.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the impossibility of a substrate (ذات) being devoid of all the kinds of attributes it can receive. It begins by stating a conditional: if it were possible for it to be devoid of some of those kinds, then it would be possible for it to be devoid of all, because the relation of all kinds to the essence is equal. But it is absolutely impossible for any essence to be devoid of what it can receive—whether in the created or the eternal. Therefore, being devoid of some is also impossible. Then Sanusi applies this to two cases: first, the case of originated things (الحادث): we know by necessity that substances (جواهر) cannot be devoid of the genus of accidents called الأكوان (motion, rest, composition, separation). Hence they cannot be devoid of any other accident they can receive. Second, the case of God (القديم): we know with certainty that He cannot be devoid of the attributes indicated by His actions—knowledge, power, will, life. If He lacked these, He could not act, which would lead to the nonexistence of all created things despite their existence—impossible. Thus He cannot be devoid of any other attribute He can receive. This leads to the conclusion that all His attributes are eternal because they are necessarily concomitant with His essence; if any were originated, His essence would be originated, which is impossible given His necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The principle of impossibility of devoidness only applies to created beings, not to God.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that the impossibility of being devoid of what it accepts is absolute, applying to both the originated (الحادث) and the Eternal (القديم). He then applies it to God, proving that God cannot be without the attributes indicated by His actions.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is it impossible for God to be devoid of any of His attributes?
A) Because the world's existence depends on them
B) Because His actions indicate His attributes
C) Because the relation of all attributes to Him is equal
D) Because substances are necessarily characterized by accidents

Key Takeaway
No essence can be empty of all the attributes it can receive, which for God means all His attributes are eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The principle of equality of relation implies that if you affirm one attribute of God, you must affirm all. Are there any attributes of God that you downplay or ignore in your daily thoughts? How can you intentionally remind yourself of a neglected attribute this week?
?
Sanusi argues that if God lacked certain attributes, He could not act. When you struggle to see God's action in your life, do you unconsciously doubt His attributes? What specific step can you take to trust in His eternal knowledge and power even when you don't understand.

Two Demands from the Principle: World's Temporality and God's Attributes' Eternity
Sanusi summarizes the two conclusions derived from the principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi derives two fundamental theological conclusions—the temporality of the world and the eternity of God's attributes—from the single principle that a receiver cannot be devoid of its possible attributes.

Core Idea
From one principle, two inferences: the world is temporal, God's attributes are eternal.

Sanusi has established the principle that a substrate cannot be stripped of the attributes it is capable of receiving. Now he demonstrates the power of that principle by applying it to two cases: the observable world (الشاهد) and the unseen God (الغائب). In this passage, he shows how the same logical connection yields opposite conclusions—temporality for the world, eternity for God—depending on what we already know about the essence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle
The passage begins by reminding us of the principle (قاعدة): impossibility of a receiver being devoid of its possible attributes. Recognize that this is a universal rational truth.
2
Follow the first inference (witnessed)
Sanusi applies it to the world: since its attributes are temporal, its essence must also be temporal. Trace the reasoning from attributes to essence.
3
Follow the second inference (absent)
Next, applied to God: since His essence is eternal, His attributes must also be eternal. Notice the symmetry with the first inference.
4
Grasp the general rule
The final sentence summarizes the logic: the mutual entailment (تلازم) works both ways—from attributes to essence and from essence to attributes.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
This reasoning strengthens the proof for the world's creation (temporality) and for God's attributes being eternal and inseparable from His essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا تعرفُ أنَّ استحالة عروّ القابلِ عما يقبلُهُ مِنَ الصفاتِ قاعدة يثبت لنا بها مطلبان : أحدهما في الشاهد : وهو حدوث العالم ؛ لأنَّهُ لمَّا قامَ البرهانُ على حدوثِ صفاتِهِ عرفنا منه حدوث ذاتِهِ ؛ لما علمنا من استحالة عراً الذاتِ عمَّا تقبلُهُ مِنَ الصفاتِ والثاني في حقِّ الغائبِ : وهو وجوب القدم لجميع صفاته تعالى ؛ لأنَّهُ لمَّا قامَ البرهان على وجوبِ قدمِهِ جلَّ وعلا .. عرفنا منهُ وجوبَ القدم لكلِّ صفةٍ مِنْ صفاتِهِ تعالى ؛ لما علمنا أيضاً من استحالة عروّ الذات عما تقبلُهُ مِنَ الصفاتِ . والحاصلُ : أَنَّهُ لمَّا انعقد التلازم بينَ كلَّ ذاتٍ وبينَ أجناس مقبولِها مِنَ الصفاتِ . . صح أنْ يُستدَلَّ بما عُلِمَ مِنْ حدوثِ أحدهما على حدوث الآخر ، كما يُستدَلُّ بما عُلِمَ مِنْ قدم أحدهما على قدم الآخرِ .
And by this you know that the impossibility of the receiver being devoid of what it accepts from the attributes is a principle by which we establish two demands: One of them in the witnessed (the world): it is the temporality of the world; because when the proof of the temporality of its attributes was established, we knew from it the temporality of its essence, due to our knowledge of the impossibility of the essence being devoid of what it accepts from the attributes. And the second in the case of the absent (God): it is the necessity of eternity for all of His attributes, exalted is He; because when the proof of the necessity of His eternity was established, may He be exalted, we knew from it the necessity of eternity for every attribute of His, exalted is He; due to our knowledge also of the impossibility of the essence being devoid of what it accepts from the attributes. And the conclusion: that when the necessary connection was established between every essence and the kinds of what it accepts from the attributes, it is valid to infer from the known temporality of one of them to the temporality of the other, just as one infers from the known eternity of one of them to the eternity of the other.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is a summary of the two applications of the principle. Sanusi states that the impossibility of a receiver being devoid of its possible attributes is a foundational principle (قاعدة) that yields two demands (مطلبان). First, in the witnessed realm (الشاهد), i.e., the physical world: we already know that the world's attributes are temporally originated (by proof from accidents), so using the principle, we infer that the world's essence itself is temporally originated. Second, in the absent realm (الغائب), i.e., God: we know that God's essence is eternal (by prior proof), so using the same principle, we infer that all His attributes are eternal, because His essence cannot be devoid of them. The general point is that the necessary connection (تلازم) between a substrate and its possible attributes allows inference from the temporality of one to the temporality of the other, and from the eternity of one to the eternity of the other.

Key Terms

مطلبان
شاهد
غائب
حدوث العالم
قدم الصفات
تلازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the principle only works one way: from attributes to essence (or vice versa).
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the necessary connection (تلازم) works both ways. From the temporality of attributes we infer temporality of essence; from the eternity of essence we infer eternity of attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do we infer from the temporality of the world's attributes?
A) The temporality of God's attributes
B) The eternity of the world
C) The temporality of the world's essence
D) The impossibility of the world's existence

Key Takeaway
The principle of necessary connection between essence and its possible attributes allows us to infer from the known temporality/eternity of one to the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world's very essence is temporal because its attributes are, what does that mean for your attachment to material things? Are you living as if this world will last forever, or are you preparing for what is eternal?
?
God's attributes are eternal—His knowledge, power, mercy. Have you ever considered that His attributes are unchanging? How does that affect your trust in His promises? Are you relying on His eternal mercy to cover your fleeting sins?
?
The principle shows a necessary connection between what you are (essence) and what you do (attributes). Do your actions consistently reflect your beliefs? Is your character stable, or do you change like the world? What one attribute do you need to align with your faith today?

Objection and Answer on Temporal Attributes
Sanusi responds to an objection that assuming God's attributes temporal would lead to circularity or infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a key objection against the doctrine of God's attributes and how Sanusi responds by distinguishing between the case of created substances and the case of God.

Core Idea
An objection may not apply equally to God and creation.

In this passage, Sanusi anticipates a logical objection: if God's attributes were temporal, wouldn't that lead to circularity or infinite regress? He begins his answer by pointing out a crucial difference between the way attributes are necessary for created substances and for God. Understanding this distinction is essential for defending the classical Ash'ari position on God's eternal attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage starting from 'ولو فرضت حادثة' to understand the hypothetical scenario and the claimed logical problem.
The objection is quoted before 'هذا جواب'.
2
Follow the answer's structure
Notice how Sanusi first acknowledges the point about the originated world, then distinguishes it from the case of the Eternal.
The answer begins with 'هذا جواب عن سؤال'.
3
Identify the key difference
The impossibility of substances being devoid of accidents is known by necessity (بالضرورة), but the same claim for God may not be granted by the opponent.
Look for 'معلوم بالضرورة' vs 'قد لا يسلم لكم'.
4
Reflect on the implication
The answer shows that the objection does not automatically refute the argument for attributes; it depends on whether the premise is accepted.
This is a crucial move in kalam argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ولو فُرِضَتْ حادثةً لَلزِمَ الدورُ أو التسلسل ؛ لتوقفِ إحداثها عليها ) هذا جواب عن سؤال استشعرْتُ ورودَهُ ، وتقريرُهُ أَنْ يُقالَ : ما ذكرتُم في حقِّ الحادثِ مِن استحالة عرو الجواهر عن بعض ما تقبلُهُ - وهو الأكوان - فيلزم ألا تعرى عن سائر ما تقبله .. مُسلَّم ؛ لأنَّ استحالة عروها عن الأكوانِ معلوم بالضرورة ، وأما ما ذكرتُم في حقِّ القديمِ مِنِ استحالةِ عروهِ تعالى عن العلم والقدرة والإرادة والحياة فيلزم استحالة عروه عن سائر ما يقبلُهُ مِنَ الصفاتِ .. قد لا يُسلَّم لكم
His statement: 'And if they were supposed to be temporal, then circularity or infinite regress would follow, because their causing to come into existence depends on them.' This is an answer to a question whose occurrence I sensed, and its explanation is that it is said: What you mentioned regarding the originated, about the impossibility of substances being devoid of some of what they accept—which is the accidents of motion etc.—so that they are not devoid of the rest of what they accept, is accepted; because the impossibility of them being devoid of those accidents is known by necessity. But as for what you mentioned regarding the Eternal, exalted is He, about the impossibility of His being devoid of knowledge, power, will, and life, and so the impossibility of His being devoid of the rest of what He accepts from the attributes—this may not be accepted by you.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is an objection and the beginning of its answer. The objector (or a hypothetical question) says: If you assume the world's attributes are temporal, you can infer the world's essence is temporal, but for God, if you assume His attributes are temporal, then those attributes would need to be brought into existence, which would require other attributes, leading to either circularity (دور) or infinite regress (تسلسل). The author answers by distinguishing the two cases: In the case of the world (الحادث), the impossibility of being devoid of the primary accidents (الأكوان) is known by necessity, so the inference to other attributes holds. In the case of God, the objector may not grant that the impossibility of being devoid of knowledge, power, etc. is established in the same way; thus the objection does not necessarily apply. The answer is cut off at 'قد لا يسلم لكم', indicating the opponent might not accept the premise for God.

Key Terms

تسلسل
توقف
إحداث
أكوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection that temporal attributes lead to circularity or infinite regress applies equally to both created substances and God.
Correction: Sanusi argues that the impossibility of created substances being devoid of accidents is known by necessity, but the same premise for God—that He cannot be devoid of attributes—may not be granted by the opponent. Thus the objection does not automatically apply to God.

Checkpoint
What is the objection against the argument for God's attributes?
A) It leads to the world's destruction
B) It leads to circularity or infinite regress
C) It contradicts the principle of causality
D) It requires too many attributes

Key Takeaway
The objection that temporal attributes lead to circularity or infinite regress is countered by distinguishing the necessary attachment of accidents to substances from the necessary attachment of attributes to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a logical argument that seems to disprove a belief about God, do you automatically accept its premises without checking whether they apply to God? How will you train yourself to pause and verify each premise before concluding?
?
Sanusi distinguishes between what is known by necessity for creation and what is accepted for God. In your own life, where have you unconsciously treated God as if He were bound by the same limitations as created beings? What will you do today to consciously remind yourself of His transcendence?
?
The objection in this lesson could lead someone to doubt God's attributes. Have you ever allowed a clever-sounding argument to weaken your certainty about God, even when that argument rested on an analogy that might not hold? Will you commit to examining the foundations of such arguments before letting them affect your faith?

The Metaphor of Naming Recompense as Knowledge
Author explains the metaphor of calling recompense 'knowledge'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic metaphor behind calling recompense 'knowledge' and how it relates to the category of naming the object of relation by the name of the relation.

Core Idea
Calling recompense 'knowledge' is a metaphor, not a literal equation.

Theologians sometimes speak of recompense as 'knowledge'. This is not a literal statement but a recognized metaphorical usage. In this brief passage, Sanusi clarifies the linguistic mechanism: it falls under the category of naming the object of relation by the name of the relation itself. This lesson will unpack that concept and its prevalence in Arabic discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the short Arabic sentence once, then read the literal translation. Notice the compact style and the key terms.
The passage begins with 'وعزّ' which is a continuation from the previous page.
2
Identify the metaphor category
The passage states that this naming is 'من باب تسمية المتعلق باسم المتعلق'. Understand that the object of relation is called by the name of the relation. This is the metaphorical mechanism.
3
Reflect on the commonality
The author emphasizes that this metaphor is 'شائع في اللسان' (common in language). Consider why such metaphors are frequent in theological terminology.
This insight helps prevent literal misinterpretation.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to confirm you grasp the type of linguistic figure described.
The correct answer is the metaphor of naming the object of relation by the name of the relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعزّ ، وتسمية الجزاء بالعلم مِنْ بابِ تسمية المتعلق باسم المتعلقِ ، وهو مجاز شائع في اللسان .
And He, the Exalted, and naming the recompense as knowledge is of the category of naming the object of relation by the name of the relation, and it is a common metaphor in language.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This is a brief explanatory note about a linguistic figure of speech. The author states that calling the recompense (جزاء) by the term knowledge (علم) is a metaphorical usage (مجاز) that falls under the category of naming the thing that is related (المتعلق) by the name of the relation itself. This type of metaphor is common in Arabic speech.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that calling recompense 'knowledge' is a literal equation or that recompense is actually knowledge.
Correction: It is a recognized metaphor: the object of relation (recompense) is named by the name of the relation (knowledge), which is common in Arabic.

Checkpoint

The Four Meanings of al-Ḍarūrī
Classification of four meanings of ḍarūrī with exclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four distinct meanings of the term ḍarūrī and why only one of them is negated for God's knowledge.

Core Idea
The term ḍarūrī has four meanings; only the fourth is impossible for Allah.

Sanusi clarifies a crucial semantic point: the word 'necessary' (ḍarūrī) is used in different senses. To avoid confusion, he quotes a classification distinguishing ontological, epistemological, and psychological senses. This passage is essential for understanding later discussions on divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the four enumerated meanings.
The passage is a quotation from 'the proposer' (al-muqtariḥ).
2
Identify each of the four meanings
Go through each meaning: (1) what is not within created power, (2) known without proof, (3) known without prior reflection, (4) accompanied by harm/need.
Meanings 2 and 3 are specific to sciences; meaning 1 applies also to actions.
3
Note the exclusion
Sanusi states that the fourth meaning is the one impossible for God's knowledge, not the other three.
This prevents misunderstanding when the term is used in theological arguments.
4
Reflect on why this distinction matters
Without this clarification, one might mistakenly think God's knowledge cannot be self-evident in any sense. Understanding the polysemy is key to kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقولُ : قال المقترح : ( الضروري يُطلق على أربعة معانٍ : ما ليس بمقدور بالقدرة الحادثة، ونقيضُه المكتسب؛ وهو المقدور بها، وهذا لا يختص بالعلم، بل يُقالُ : حركة ضرورية أي : غير مقدورة بالقدرة الحادثة الثاني : ما عُلِمَ بغير دليل الثالثُ : ما عُلِمَ مِنْ غيرِ تقدُّمِ نظر، وهذان مُختصَّانِ بالعلوم . الرابع : ما قارنه ضررٌ وحاجةٌ ؛ كعلم الإنسانِ جوعَهُ وألمَهُ ، وهذا المعنى الأخيرُ هو المستحيل في حقٌّ علم الباري جلَّ وعلا ، دونَ المعاني الثلاثةِ ، ولأجلهِ امتنع إطلاق لفظ الضروري عليه
We say: The proposer said: 'Al-ḍarūrī (the necessary/self-evident) is used for four meanings: (1) what is not within the power of created power, and its opposite is the acquired (al-muktasab), which is that which is within its power; and this is not specific to knowledge, but rather it is said: "a necessary movement" meaning not within the power of created power. (2) what is known without proof; (3) what is known without prior reflection; and these two are specific to sciences. (4) what is accompanied by harm and need, like a person's knowledge of his hunger and pain. This last meaning is the one impossible for the knowledge of the Exalted Creator, unlike the three meanings, and for this reason the term 'necessary' (ḍarūrī) is prevented from being applied to Him.'
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. The author quotes a proposer who lists four meanings of the term 'ضروري'. The first meaning is ontological: what is not within created power, e.g., involuntary movements. The second and third are epistemological: known without proof, or known without prior reflection. The fourth is psychological: knowledge accompanied by harm/need, like pain. The author then clarifies that when we say God's knowledge cannot be 'ضروري', we mean in the fourth sense only, not the others. This is important because otherwise one might mistakenly think God's knowledge is not self-evident in other valid senses.

Key Terms
ضروري
مقدور
مكتسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all meanings of ḍarūrī are impossible for God.
Correction: Only the fourth meaning (knowledge accompanied by harm/need) is impossible for God; the other three are not.

Checkpoint
What is the fourth meaning of 'ضروري' according to the proposer?
A) Known without proof
B) Known without reflection
C) Accompanied by harm/need
D) Not within created power

Key Takeaway
The term 'ḍarūrī' has four meanings; only the fourth (knowledge accompanied by harm/need) is impossible for Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience knowledge of your own hunger or pain, do you recognize that this knowledge is accompanied by a sense of need and vulnerability? How does realizing that Allah's knowledge is utterly free from such need affect your trust in His wisdom when He decrees hardship for you?
?
The passage teaches that the term 'ḍarūrī' has multiple meanings, and only one is inappropriate for Allah. In your own life, are there times when you incorrectly assume that a concept applies to Allah in a way that actually limits Him? How can you cultivate precision in your understanding of Allah's attributes?
?
Consider the first meaning: 'what is not within created power.' Your existence and many of your actions are beyond your control. Are you at peace with your limitations, or do you struggle against them? How does knowing that Allah's knowledge encompasses all—including what is beyond your power—bring you to trust Him more?

The Meaning of Badīhī and Its Impossibility for God
Sanusi defines badīhī and explains its impossibility for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of badīhī as a type of immediate knowledge and why it cannot be attributed to Allah.

Core Idea
Badīhī implies suddenness, which contradicts God's eternal knowledge.

This lesson explores the term 'badīhī' (evident/intuitive knowledge). Sanusi explains its similarity to necessary knowledge (ḍarūrī) but clarifies a crucial distinction: badīhī arises suddenly without prior premises. The very concept of suddenness implies temporality, making it impossible to apply to Allah's timeless knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on badīhī
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how Sanusi introduces badīhī in relation to ḍarūrī and then explains its impossibility for God.
2
Understand the similarity and difference
Badīhī is like ḍarūrī in being immediate, but it lacks the elements of harm or need. Grasp this distinction.
3
Analyze the etymology
The term comes from 'bada'a' meaning sudden occurrence without prior premises. See how this entails temporality.
4
Reflect on why it cannot apply to God
Since God's knowledge is eternal and not subject to sudden events, the term badīhī is inapplicable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا يمتنع إطلاقُ لفظ البديهي على علمـه تعالى ، وهـو كالضروري ، إلا أنَّه لا يقترن بضررٍ ولا حاجة ، وإنَّما استحال إطلاقه على علمه جل وعلا لأنَّهُ يشعرُ بالحدوثِ ؛ إذْ يُقالُ : بده النفس الأمرُ؛ إذا أتاها بغتةً بغيرِ سابقة شعور بمقدِّماتِ تُغَلِّبُ على الظنِّ وجودَهُ . والحاصلُ : أنَّ العلم الحادث ينقسم ثلاثة أقسام : ضروري ، وبديهي ، وكسبي ، ولا يُطلَقُ واحدٌ منها على علمهِ تعالى
Likewise, applying the term 'badīhī' (evident/intuitive) to His knowledge is prohibited. And it is like 'ḍarūrī', except that it is not accompanied by harm or need. Rather, its application to His knowledge is impossible because it suggests temporality (ḥudūth); for it is said: 'the matter occurred suddenly to the soul' when it comes to it suddenly without prior awareness of premises that make its existence probable. The conclusion is that temporal knowledge is divided into three types: ḍarūrī, badīhī, and kasbī (acquired). None of them is applied to His knowledge.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage defines 'بديهي' as similar to 'ضروري' but without harm/need. The reason it cannot apply to God is that 'بديهي' implies a sudden occurrence to the mind, which entails temporality (ḥudūth). Since God's knowledge is eternal, it cannot be sudden. The author then concludes that all three types of temporal knowledge are inapplicable to God.

Correction: Badīhī is similar to ḍarūrī (both are immediate) but differs in that it lacks the elements of harm or need and specifically implies suddenness.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The term badīhī cannot apply to Allah because it entails temporal suddenness, incompatible with His eternal knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Commit to a specific verbal reminder (e.g., while saying 'Alhamdulillah' after a sudden blessing, explicitly recall that it was always known to Allah).

The Threefold Division of Temporal Knowledge
Sanusi enumerates the three types of temporal knowledge and states their inapplicability to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that temporal knowledge is divided into three categories—ḍarūrī, badīhī, kasbī—and that none of them apply to Allah.

Core Idea
All human knowledge is temporal and divided; God's knowledge is beyond all categories.

Sanusi concludes his discussion by summarizing that all temporal knowledge falls into one of three types: necessary (ḍarūrī), intuitive (badīhī), or acquired (kasbī). He then firmly states that none of these categories can be applied to Allah's knowledge, underscoring the absolute transcendence of the Divine attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding passage
Read the Arabic sentence that summarizes the division of temporal knowledge.
2
Identify the three types
Notice the three terms: ḍarūrī, badīhī, kasbī. Understand that they are all types of temporal (ḥādith) knowledge.
3
Understand the ruling
Sanusi clearly says that not a single one of these is applied to God's knowledge. This is a definitive rule.
4
Reflect on the implications
This reinforces that God's knowledge is unlike any human knowledge—it is eternal and not subject to the categories of temporal occurrence.

Proof of Inner Speech and Mu'tazili Objection
Sanusi presents the proof for inner speech and the Mu'tazili objection and conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof for inner speech (al-kalam al-nafsi) and how the Mu'tazili objection reduces it to will or knowledge, and why the dispute is over distinguishing it from those.

Core Idea
Inner speech is proven by the inner experience of command, distinct from outward expressions, and separate from will and knowledge.

Imam Sanusi presents a powerful argument for the existence of inner speech, grounded in our own experience of commanding and prohibiting. He then anticipates a Mu'tazili reduction that tries to explain away this distinct attribute. This passage clarifies what is at stake in the debate over Allah's attribute of speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the first part of the passage where the People of Truth argue from the inner state of commanding. Notice the key phrase 'ṭalaban jāzim' (categorical demand).
This argument is based on ordinary human experience.
2
Understand the Mu'tazili objection
The Mu'tazila claim that what is found in the soul is just will or knowledge. See how they reduce the two types of speech (command and report) to these attributes.
The objection tries to deny a distinct attribute.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Sanusi states that both sides agree there is some meaning in the soul; the dispute is whether it is a distinct attribute. Understand why this distinction matters for theology.
The conclusion highlights the area of agreement and disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حُجَجُ إثباتِ الكلام النفسي
واحتج أهلُ الحقِّ على إثباتِهِ شاهداً : بأنَّ الآمر والناهي يجد حالةَ أمره ونهيهِ مِنْ نَفْسِهِ طلباً جازماً بالضرورة ، ويدلُّ عليه بالعباراتِ المختلفة ، وما يعرضُ لهُ الاختلافُ مغاير لما لا يعرضُ لهُ الاختلافُ ، ولأنَّ العباراتِ بالجعْلِ والمواضعة والتوقيف(٢) ، وما في النفس حقيقة عقلية لا بالجعل والتوقيف
وزعمَتِ المعتزلة : أنَّ ما يجده الطالب في نفسِهِ يرجع إلى إرادةِ الامتثالِ ، ويردُّونَ الخبر إلى العلم بنظم الصيغة
فالحاصلُ : الاتفاقُ على وجدانِ أصل المعنى في النفس ، وإنَّما النزاع في تمييزه عن الإرادة والعلم .
Proofs of the Establishment of Inner Speech. And the People of Truth argued for its establishment through [an argument from] evidence: that the commander and prohibitor finds in himself the state of his commanding and prohibiting as a categorical demand (ṭalaban jāzim) by necessity, and he indicates it through different expressions. That which is subject to difference is different from that which is not subject to difference. And because expressions are by convention (jaʿl), mutual agreement (muwāḍaʿa), and revelation (tawqīf), while what is in the soul is a rational reality, not by convention and revelation. And the Muʿtazila claimed that what the seeker (ṭālib) finds in his soul reduces to the will (irāda) to comply, and they reduce the report (khabar) to knowledge (ʿilm) of the composition of the formulation. The conclusion: agreement on the finding of the original meaning in the soul; rather, the dispute is over distinguishing it from will and knowledge.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a proof (ḥujja) for the existence of inner speech (al-kalām al-nafsī) and a response to the Muʿtazilī objection. The author first states that the People of Truth (Ahl al-Ḥaqq) prove inner speech using a witnessed (shāhid) argument: a person who commands or prohibits finds in his soul a state of command or prohibition that is a categorical demand (ṭalab jāzim) by necessity. He indicates this state through various expressions (such as words, writing, signs). Since the expressions can differ (e.g., different languages) while the inner meaning remains the same, what is subject to difference (the expressions) must be different from what is not subject to difference (the inner meaning). Furthermore, expressions are established by convention (jaʿl), mutual agreement (muwāḍaʿa), and revelation (tawqīf), but what is in the soul is a rational reality (ḥaqīqa ʿaqliyya) not dependent on convention. Therefore, inner speech is distinct from the spoken words. Then the Muʿtazila object: what the seeker (the one who commands) finds in himself is nothing but the will (irāda) to comply, and in the case of informing, it is knowledge (ʿilm) of the composition of the formulation. The author responds by summarizing that there is agreement that there is some meaning found in the soul; the only disagreement is whether to distinguish it from will and knowledge. So the Muʿtazila admit the existence of something in the soul but categorize it under will or knowledge, while the Ahl al-Ḥaqq say it is a distinct attribute: inner speech.

Key Terms
الكلام النفسي
الأمر
النهي
الطلب الجازم
الإرادة
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inner speech is just the same as will (irada) or knowledge (ilm).
Correction: The proof shows that the inner state of command is a categorical demand (talab jazim), which is essentially different from mere will or knowledge, because a command is a demand for compliance, not just a wish or a piece of knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the basic proof that the People of Truth use for the existence of inner speech (al-kalam al-nafsi)?
A) That a commander finds a state of categorical demand in himself which he indicates through different expressions.
B) That the Quran is eternal.
C) That God's speech must be similar to human speech.
D) That the Mu'tazila deny any inner meaning.

Key Takeaway
The proof for inner speech rests on the immediate awareness of a categorical demand in the soul, which is distinct from both will and knowledge, and from the outward expressions that vary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you internally command yourself to do something or prohibit yourself, do you sense that inner demand as distinct from just wanting or knowing? How does that immediate experience strengthen your belief in Allah's attribute of speech?
?
If the Mu'tazila reduce inner speech to will and knowledge, what would that imply about your personal accountability when you 'hear' conscience commanding you? Does your conscience have authority beyond your own will?
?
The proof uses your own inner awareness of commanding to establish a reality. When you recite the Qur'an silently, do you experience that meaning as something 'in your soul' that is distinct from the words? How should that affect your reverence for the uncreated speech of Allah?

Defending the Tawkīd Argument from a Poetic Objection
Sanusi defends the emphatic verbal noun argument against an objection using a poetic example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the emphatic verbal noun argument against the objection that poetry uses tawkīd metaphorically, and why the counter-accusation of circular reasoning fails.

Core Idea
Poetry cannot undermine a definitive rational proof that God's speech transcends letters and sounds.

In the previous lesson, Sanusi argued that the emphatic verbal noun 'taklīman' in the verse prevents any metaphorical interpretation, establishing that God truly speaks. An opponent now objects with a line of poetry where a similar emphatic is used metaphorically. This lesson unpacks Sanusi's layered response: he first distinguishes the verse from the poetic metaphor, then counters the opponent's charge of circular reasoning by pointing to the prior definitive proof that grounds the Ahl al-Sunna's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the objection begins with a poetic line, followed by the author's response and further rebuttals.
The passage is dense; take your time to follow the flow of argument and counter-argument.
2
Identify the main objection and the initial response
Identify the opponent's objection using the poetic line, and then find Sanusi's first response: that the poetry involves subsidiary metaphor (isti'āra tābi'a) while the verse lacks any indicator of metaphor.
Sanusi concedes tawkīd prevents metaphor in the verse, but only if it is the meaningful type of emphasis. He then explains why the poetic line is different.
3
Examine the circular reasoning charge
The opponent then accuses Sanusi's response of begging the question (musādara). Understand why: the opponent assumes speech is only letters and sounds, which is exactly what is in dispute.
This is a key moment: the opponent's objection itself presupposes the very point at issue.
4
Analyze Sanusi's final rebuttal
Sanusi counters that the Ahl al-Sunna only used the verse as evidence after establishing through definitive rational proof (burhān qaṭ'ī) that speech is not confined to letters and sounds. Hence the inference from the verse is valid and not circular.
Sanusi also adds that relying on a single ambiguous poetic line to overturn linguistic scholarship is weak.
5
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Connect this defense to the broader theme: rational proof (burhān) precedes and grounds scriptural evidence, protecting it from rhetorical objections.
This lesson shows how kalam integrates reason and revelation in a non-circular way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَكَى الْخَرُّ مِنْ عَوْفٍ وَأَنْكَرَ جِلْدَهُ وَعَجَّتْ عَجِيجاً مِنْ جُذَامَ الْمَطَارِفُ
سلَّمنا دفع التوكيد المجاز ، لكن إنَّما يدفعُهُ في الآية أن لو وقع بالمعنوي الذي يدفعُ توهم المجاز في النسبة؛ إذْ فيها وقع النزاع في الآية،
لا في المسندِ ؛ لأنَّ الكلامَ حقيقةً قد وقع ، وإِنَّما النزاعُ : ممَّنْ وقعَ ؟ قلتُ : الجواب عنِ الأَوَّلِ : أنَّ البيتَ مِنْ بابِ الاستعارة التبعية لوقوعها في الفعل ، والاستعارة مطلقاً مبنيّة على تناسي التشبيه ، حتى قال فيها طائفةٌ مِنْ علماء البيانِ : إنَّها حقيقة لغوية ، فصح التوكيد فيها للمبالغة في دخولِ المشبَّه في جنس المشبه به ، والآية لا قرينة فيها على الاستعارة ، بخلافِ البيتِ ؛ فإنَّ قرينة الاستعارة فيه : إسناد العجيج إلى ما لا يتأتى منه حقيقةً .
إلا أنَّهُ لا يُسلَّمُ هذا الجوابُ مِنْ ورودِ الاعتراض عليه بالمصادرة عن المطلوب ؛ إذ الخصم يدَّعي أنَّ الكلام ليس إلا الحروف والأصوات ، وقد اسند في الآية إلى مَنْ لا يتأتّى منه ، فهو عندَهُ كإسنادِ العجيج في البيتِ إلى المطارفِ ، للكنَّ أهلَ السنَّةِ رضيَ اللهُ إِنَّما استدلُّوا بالآيةِ بعدَ أنْ قامَ لهم البرهانُ القطعي على عدمِ انحصار الكلام في الحروفِ والأصواتِ ، فصح الاستدلال بها ، ولا يُعترَضُ بالبيتِ لما سبق ، وأيضاً فادّعاء هدم علماء اللسان بمجرَّدِ بيت شعري يحتمل أموراً . . لا يخفى ضعفه
The weak one from 'Awf wept and denied his skin, and the scarves from Judham clamoured with loud cries. We concede that the emphatic [tawkīd] prevents metaphorical interpretation, but it only prevents it in the verse if it occurred as a meaningful [emphasis] that prevents the suspicion of metaphor in the attribution; because the dispute in the verse occurred concerning the attribution, not the predicate; for the speech truly occurred, and the dispute is only: from whom did it occur? I said: The answer to the first [objection] is that the line of poetry is of the type of subsidiary metaphor (isti'āra tābi'a) because it occurs in the verb, and metaphor absolutely is based on forgetting the simile, to the extent that a group of scholars of rhetoric have said that it is a linguistic reality; so the emphatic (tawkīd) in it is valid for hyperbole in the entry of the simile'd into the genus of the simile'd-to. But the verse has no indication of metaphor, unlike the line of poetry; for the indication of metaphor in it is the attribution of crying to that which cannot truly do it. However, this answer is not conceded due to the arrival of an objection against it of circular reasoning (musādara 'alā al-maṭlūb); for the opponent claims that speech is nothing but letters and sounds, and it has been attributed in the verse to one from whom it cannot truly come, so in his view it is like the attribution of crying in the line to the scarves. But the Ahl al-Sunna, may Allah be pleased with them, only used the verse as evidence after the definitive proof had been established for them that speech is not confined to letters and sounds, so the inference by it is valid, and the objection by the line is not accepted for what preceded. Also, the claim of demolishing the scholars of language by merely a poetic line that admits of [various] interpretations... its weakness is not hidden.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section. The author has previously argued that the emphatic verbal noun in the verse 'وَكَلَّمَ اللَّهُ مُوسَىٰ تَكْلِيمًا' prevents any metaphorical interpretation, thereby proving that God truly speaks. An opponent objects by citing a line of poetry where an emphatic verbal noun is used in a clearly metaphorical context. The author responds by analyzing the poetic line: it involves a subsidiary metaphor (isti'āra tābi'a) occurring in the verb, which is built on 'forgetting the simile,' and some rhetoricians consider such metaphors to be linguistic realities, allowing the emphatic to be used for hyperbole. The verse lacks any such indicator of metaphor. The opponent then accuses this response of circular reasoning, because the Ahl al-Sunna are assuming that speech is not limited to letters and sounds, which is precisely what is in dispute. The author counters that the Ahl al-Sunna only used the verse as evidence after establishing through definitive rational proof (burhān qaṭ'ī) that speech cannot be confined to letters and sounds. Hence, the argument is not circular. Furthermore, relying on a single ambiguous poetic line to undermine a consensus of scholars of language is weak.

Key Terms
التوكيد
المجاز
الاستعارة التبعية
المصادرة
البرهان القطعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The presence of an emphatic verbal noun (tawkīd) in metaphorical poetry proves that the tawkīd in the verse can also be metaphorical.
Correction: The poetic line is a subsidiary metaphor (isti'āra tābi'a) where metaphor is expected and the tawkīd is used for hyperbole, while the verse has no indicator of metaphor and is grounded in a prior rational proof that Allah's speech is not letters and sounds.

Checkpoint
Why does the opponent's objection involve circular reasoning (musādara)?
A) The opponent assumes speech is only letters and sounds, which is exactly what is in dispute.
B) The poetic line is anachronistic.
C) The emphatic in the verse does not prevent metaphor.
D) The Ahl al-Sunna have no proof.

Key Takeaway
The definitive rational proof (burhān qaṭ'ī) that Allah's speech is not limited to letters and sounds precedes and validates the scriptural inference, protecting it from dialectical objections based on poetry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you truly sense that you are encountering the eternal speech of Allah, beyond letters and sounds? What would change in your heart if you fully internalized this?
?
The opponent's accusation of circular reasoning is subtle. Are there any of your own beliefs about Allah where you might be unconsciously assuming what you want to prove? How can you ensure your faith is built on solid reasoning first?
