The Necessary Chain: Definition and Possibility
Definition of the necessary chain with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the necessary chain is and why it does not entail impossibility, using the examples of eternal bliss and torment.

Core Idea
Endless future chains are possible, unlike impossible infinite regress.

The concept of the necessary chain (السلسلة اللازمة) often causes confusion because people assume any endless chain is impossible. In this passage, the author clarifies that the necessary chain is a chain of order that can continue indefinitely into the future, just like the eternal rewards and punishments of the afterlife. This distinction is key to many theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand what the author means by 'the necessary chain' and 'the chain of order'.
Note that the author uses the term 'chain of order' to describe a sequence where each obstacle is followed by another.
2
Consider the objection and response
The author mentions that some might think this chain leads to impossibility. Identify why he says that and how he responds using the examples of Paradise and Hell.
The key is that impossibility only applies to regress that has no end, but future chains can be endless.
3
Reflect on the examples
Contemplate how the bliss of Paradise and the torment of Hell are endless but not impossible. This illustrates that not all endless sequences are problematic.
This connects to the broader theological context of the vision of God (ru'ya).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السلسلة اللازمة هي سلسلة الترتيب؛ بأن يوجد بعد كل مانع مانع، على أنه لو كانت السلسلة اللازمة سلسلة الترتيب لزم محال؛ إذ غايته لزوم عدم انقطاع الموانع في المستقبل، وذلك لا استحالة فيه؛ كنعيم أهل الجنة، وعذاب أهل النار. الثاني: امتناع كون الرؤية مطلقا مرئية، وحجته: ما سبق من التسلسل. قلت: وهو مردود إن كان يسلم أن الوجود مصحح للرؤية. الثالث: استحالة أن يرى الإنسان رؤية نفسه، وتجويز أن يرى رؤية غيره، وكأنه يرى قائل هذا عدم لزوم التسلسل في رؤية الغير؛ لجواز أن يدرك الإنسان إدراك غيره أو لا يدركه لمانع، ثم يعدم الله ذلك المحل الثاني الذي هو محل الرؤية المدركة، فتنعدم هي والموانع، فينقطع التسلسل عند ذلك. قلت: ولا يخفى ضعف هذا الثالث أيضا؛ لأنه إن كان يجوز رؤية المانع فقد لزمه من التسلسل عند عدم كون رؤية الغير حال وجودها مرئية ما لزم عند عدم كون رؤية نفسه مرئية له، وإن كان لا يجوز رؤية الموانع فذلك يقطع التسلسل في رؤية نفسه ورؤية غيره، كما ذكرناه عن القاضي في تصحيح قول الشيخ الأشعري. وبالجملة: فالحق من هذه الأقوال إن سلم أن الوجود هو المصحح للرؤية ما ذهب إليه الشيخ بضميمة جواب القاضي رحمه الله تعالى، والله أعلم.
The necessary chain is the chain of order; that is, after every obstacle there exists an obstacle, such that if the necessary chain were the chain of order, an impossibility would follow; for its ultimate end is the continuity of obstacles in the future, and that is not impossible; like the bliss of the people of Paradise and the torment of the people of Hell. Second: the impossibility of vision [itself] being seen absolutely, and its proof is what preceded from infinite regress. I say: it is refuted if it is admitted that existence is the cause of visibility. Third: the impossibility that a person see his own vision, and the permission that he see the vision of another, and it is as if the proponent of this sees that there is no necessary infinite regress in seeing the vision of another; because it is possible that a person perceive the perception of another or not perceive it due to an obstacle, then God annihilates that second locus which is the locus of the perceived vision, so it and the obstacles cease to exist, and the infinite regress is thereby cut off. I say: the weakness of this third is also not hidden; because if he permits seeing the obstacle, then there follows for him from the infinite regress when the vision of another is not seen while it exists, what followed when his own vision is not seen by him; and if he does not permit seeing obstacles, then that cuts off the infinite regress in seeing his own vision and the vision of another, as we mentioned from al-Qadi in correcting the saying of Shaykh al-Ash'ari. In summary: the truth among these statements, if it is admitted that existence is the cause of visibility, is what the Shaykh held with the addition of the response of al-Qadi, may God have mercy on him. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the concept of the necessary chain (al-silsila al-lāzima) and three objections related to the vision of God (ru'ya). The author begins by defining the necessary chain as a chain of order (silsilat al-tartīb) where after every obstacle there exists another obstacle. He argues that if the necessary chain were like that, it would lead to an impossibility only if it implied an infinite regress with no end, but in fact it can be possible if it refers to future events that are endless, like the eternal bliss of Paradise and torment of Hell. Then the author presents three objections: The second objection (it seems the first was not mentioned here) is that vision itself cannot be seen (i.e., a vision cannot be the object of another vision), and its proof relies on infinite regress. The author responds that this is refuted if one admits that existence is what makes a thing visible, implying that a vision exists and thus could be seen. The third objection is that a person cannot see his own vision (i.e., the mental image of his own sight), but can possibly see the vision of another. The proponent argues that seeing another's vision does not lead to infinite regress because the second locus (the vision of another) can be annihilated by God along with obstacles. The author refutes this by pointing out that if one allows seeing the obstacle (i.e., the intermediate vision), then the same infinite regress applies when the vision of another is not seen; and if one does not allow seeing obstacles, then that cuts off the regress in both cases. The author then concludes that the correct position, given the premise that existence makes things visible, is that of Shaykh al-Ash'ari with the addition of al-Qadi's response.

Key Terms
السلسلة اللازمة
التسلسل
سلسلة الترتيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any chain that has no end is impossible (like infinite regress).
Correction: The author distinguishes between impossible infinite regress (which has no beginning and no end) and a possible endless future chain (which has a beginning but no end). The latter is exemplified by eternal Paradise and Hell.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to show that a chain can be endless without being impossible?
A) The chain of causes in creation.
B) The bliss of Paradise and the torment of Hell.
C) The vision of God.
D) The chain of prophets.

Key Takeaway
The necessary chain, as a chain of order, is possible and not inherently impossible, as seen in endless future events like Paradise and Hell.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author gives the eternal bliss of Paradise and torment of Hell as examples of an endless necessary chain. Which of these two futures are you building a chain towards? What obstacle (sin, distraction, or negligence) is blocking you from building the chain that leads to eternal bliss?
?
If every action you take is followed by another obstacle or opportunity, what is the pattern of your chain? Are you actively removing obstacles that distance you from Allah, or are you letting them accumulate into an endless chain of regret?
?
The necessary chain is defined as a sequence of obstacles (mawāniʿ) that continue without end. In your daily life, what are the obstacles that prevent you from drawing closer to Allah? How can you break the chain of those obstacles today, knowing that the future is endless and every moment counts?

Refutation of Objections on Vision of God
Presentation and refutation of two objections about vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two objections related to the vision of God (ru'ya) — that vision itself cannot be seen and that one cannot see one's own vision — and how the author refutes them using the premise that existence is the cause of visibility.

Core Idea
Existence makes things visible; thus vision can be seen and regress can be avoided.

After clarifying the necessary chain, the author addresses two objections that attempt to use infinite regress to deny the possibility of seeing God. The first objection claims that vision itself cannot be seen, and the second that one cannot see one's own vision. The author responds by invoking the principle that existence (الوجود) is what makes something visible, and by analyzing the cases of seeing one's own vision versus another's.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection and its refutation
The second objection says that vision (الرؤية) cannot itself be seen, based on infinite regress. The author refutes it by stating that if existence is the cause of visibility, then vision, which exists, can be seen.
This refutation relies on the premise that existence is the cause of visibility (المصحح للرؤية).
2
Read the third objection and its refutation
The third objection says a person cannot see his own vision but can see another's. The author refutes it by analyzing two scenarios: if obstacles can be seen, regress follows; if not, regress is cut off. Either way, the objection fails.
The author notes the weakness of this objection and references al-Qadi's correction of al-Ash'ari's view.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the correct position is that of Shaykh al-Ash'ari combined with al-Qadi's response, given that existence is the cause of visibility.
This concludes the discussion on vision.

Defending the Two-Fold Division of Kalam: Objection and Response
Defense of the two-fold division of kalam with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical defense of the two-fold division of kalam into report and request, and why attempts to add a third category lead to absurdity.

Core Idea
The two-fold division of speech is not arbitrary but logically necessary.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a sophisticated objection-and-answer argument defending the reduction of all types of kalam to just two: khabar (report) and talab (request). An opponent claims that a third category is possible, but the response shows that such a move leads to logical contradiction. The passage illustrates how rational consistency demands the two-fold division.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The passage begins by restating that all divisions of kalam reduce to report and request. Understand this foundational claim before engaging with the objection.
The types include inquiry, promise, and threat, all considered subtypes of report.
2
Follow the objection
Some masters object that the division is not necessarily limited to two; one could posit a third category that relates to the two like they relate to each other. Grasp the logical symmetry they propose.
3
Understand the counter-objection
The defender responds by applying the same logic to divine attributes: the opponent could similarly posit a meaning outside knowledge and power, leading to an infinite regress. See how the objection backfires.
This is a dialectical move showing the opponent's argument is too strong.
4
Grasp the specific absurdity
The core argument: if a single meaning were both report and request, it would both contradict and not contradict prohibition—an impossibility. Recognize this as a reductio ad absurdum.
Command contradicts prohibition; report does not. So a single entity cannot have both properties.
5
See the conclusion
The passage concludes that to avoid this logical absurdity, some scholars adopted multiplicity in divine attributes (multiple eternal speech attributes). Notice how this reinforces the two-fold division.
This shows the practical theological implication of rejecting the division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من حيث المتعلق فقط، والاستخبارُ والوعد والوعيد يرجع جميع ذلك إلى الخبرِ، فرجعَتِ الأقسام كلُّها إلى الخبرِ والطلبِ (1) تحته وانتصربعض الشيوخ لمذهب الشيخ والجمهور؛ فقال: لم تنحصر أقسام الكلام فيما ذُكِرَ؛ فكما جازَ ردُّ الأقسام إلى القسمين جازَ في العقلِ أنْ يكونَ قسم آخرُ نسبتُهُ إلى القسمين في الاندراج كنسبة الأقسام إلى القسمين، فقيل له: وكذا الخصم يدَّعي أنَّه لم تنحصر أيضاً أقسام المعاني فيما ذكر منها، فيجوزُ في العقلِ أنْ يكونَ ثَمَّ معنى نسبتُهُ إلى العلم والقدرةِ كنسبة العلم إلى سائر العلوم . فإن قيل: يلزمُ ثَمَّ أَنْ يُضادَّ وألا يُضادَّ . قيل: وذلك لازم أيضاً هنا؛ فإنَّ الخبرَ لا يُضادُّ النهي، والأمر يُضادُّه، فلو كان معنى واحدٌ خبراً طلباً .. لضاد ولم يُضادَّ، وذلك هو المحالُ الذي ذكرتُم مِنْ حيث المعقولُ، ولأجل استقامة الجري على هذا المسلكِ العقلي صارَ قومٌ إلى التعدُّدِ في الكلام؛ هرباً مِنْ لزومِ هذا المحال
Only from the standpoint of relation, and istikhbar (inquiry), promise and threat all return to khabar (report), so all divisions return to khabar and request. Some masters supported the doctrine of the shaykh (Sanusi) and the majority: they said, 'The divisions of kalam are not limited to what was mentioned; just as it is valid to reduce the divisions to two divisions, it is valid in reason that there be another division whose relation to the two divisions in inclusion is like the relation of the divisions to the two divisions.' He was told: 'Likewise, the opponent claims that the divisions of meanings are also not limited to what was mentioned among them, so it is valid in reason that there be a meaning whose relation to knowledge and power is like the relation of knowledge to the other sciences.' If it is said: 'Then it would necessitate that it both contradict and not contradict.' It is said: 'That is also necessary here; for khabar does not contradict prohibition, while command contradicts it; so if a single meaning were both khabar and request, it would both contradict and not contradict, and that is the absurdity which you mentioned from the standpoint of reason. And for the sake of consistency in this rational approach, some people adopted multiplicity in kalam, fleeing from the necessity of this absurdity.'
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer argument defending the view that all types of speech (kalam) reduce to two categories: khabar (report) and talab (request). The author begins by summarizing the premise: istikhbar, promise, and threat all revert to khabar, so the overall division is only khabar and talab. Some masters then defend this position against the claim that the division is incomplete. They argue that just as it is valid to reduce all types to two, it is equally valid in reason to posit a third type that is included under them analogously. The opponent counters that the same could be said for attributes like knowledge and power: one could imagine a meaning that relates to both, leading to an infinite regress. Then a specific objection is raised: if there were a single meaning that is both khabar and talab, it would both contradict prohibition (since khabar does not contradict, but command does) and not contradict – an impossibility. The response is that this same impossibility would apply to the opponent's own analogy of knowledge and power. The passage concludes that to avoid this absurdity, some scholars adopted multiplicity in the divine attributes (i.e., multiple eternal attributes of speech), fleeing the logical consequence. The reasoning is tightly dialectical, showing that the two-fold division is logically consistent and that objections against it backfire.

خبر
طلب
استخبار
وعد
وعيد
أمر
نهي
تضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The division of speech into report and request is arbitrary and could be expanded to include more categories.
Correction: The passage shows that any attempt to add a third category leads to logical contradiction, demonstrating that the two-fold division is rationally necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did some people adopt multiplicity in speech (ta'addud fi al-kalam)?
A) Because they rejected the division into khabar and talab.
B) To avoid the logical absurdity of a single meaning being both contradictory and not contradictory.
C) Because they followed Ibn Kullab's seven-attribute theory.
D) Because they believed speech is created.

Key Takeaway
The two-fold division of kalam is logically necessary; denying it leads to contradiction, which prompted some to adopt multiple attributes of speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult theological objection, do you tend to flee from the tension like the scholars who adopted multiplicity, or do you patiently work through the logic to find the consistent answer? How can you cultivate the courage to follow reason where it leads, even when it challenges your comfort?
?
This passage shows that holding contradictory beliefs about divine speech leads to absurdity. Are there areas in your own understanding of Allah where you hold two conflicting ideas without realizing it? Take a moment to examine your heart—what contradiction might you be tolerating?
?
The defender in this passage did not dismiss the objection but met it with rigorous reasoning. How often do you dismiss doubts about your faith rather than engage them honestly? What step will you take today to face one such doubt with the same rational discipline shown here?

Definition of Unity and Its Divisions
Sanusi defines unity quoting two authorities and classifies it into real and relative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of unity (al-wahda) according to al-Baydawi and al-Juwayni, and the distinction between real unity (al-wahid al-haqiqi) and relative unity (al-wahid al-idafi).

Core Idea
Unity has two forms: absolute indivisibility (God) and relative indivisibility (created things).

Before diving into the proofs of God's oneness, Sanusi establishes the conceptual ground by defining what 'unity' means. He draws on two major authorities, al-Baydawi and al-Juwayni, to show that unity is not a single concept but has a real and a relative sense. This distinction is crucial because it allows us to speak of God's unique oneness while still using the same word for unity in other contexts.

Guided Path
1
Read the preliminary note
Sanusi introduces the chapter on oneness and states the need for a preliminary on the meaning and divisions of unity. Read this opening to understand the purpose of the passage.
2
Study al-Baydawi's definition
Al-Baydawi defines unity as a thing being indivisible into parts sharing the same essence. Notice how this definition covers two types: real unity (no division at all) and relative unity (divisible into unequal parts).
Real unity applies only to Allah; relative unity applies to composite things like the human body.
3
Understand what is excluded
Al-Baydawi's definition excludes things divisible into equal parts, like a group of identical drops. This clarifies that relative unity requires parts that are different in essence.
4
Consider al-Juwayni's definition
Al-Juwayni gives a simpler definition: 'the one is that which is not divisible.' This emphasizes indivisibility as the core meaning of unity.
Al-Juwayni's definition is more general and does not distinguish between real and relative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصلُ في الوحدانية هذا فصلُ الوحدانية ، وينبغي أن يُقدَّمَ قبل الشروع في شرح مسائله مُقدِّمة في معنى الوحدة وفي أقسامها ، فنقولُ : أمَّا معنى الوَحدةِ : فقالَ ناصر الدين البيضاوي في « طوالعِهِ » : هي كون الشيء بحيثُ لا ينقسم إلى أمور متشاركة في الماهية . وتعريفه شامل للواحدِ الحقيقي ؛ وهو ما لا ينقسم أصلاً ، وللواحدِ الإضافي ؛ وهو ما ينقسم، لكن لا إلى أمور مستوية في الحقيقة ؛ كالإنسان المنقسم إلى الأعضاء المختلفة ؛ مِنْ يدِ ورِجْل ورأس ونحوها ؛ فإنَّها غير مستوية في الماهية ، ويخرجُ مِنَ التعريف ما انقسم إلى أمورٍ متساوية في الماهية ؛ كجماعةِ نُقط مِنْ عسل أو ماء ونحوهما . وقال الإمامُ في الإرشادِ : الواحد في اصطلاح الأصوليين هو الشيء الذي لا ينقسم
Chapter on Oneness. This is the chapter of Oneness. Before commencing explanation of its issues, a preliminary on the meaning of unity and its divisions should be presented. We say: As for the meaning of unity: Nasir al-Din al-Baydawi said in his Tawali': 'It is the thing being such that it is not divisible into things sharing in the essence (mahiyya).' This definition includes the real one (al-wahid al-haqiqi), which is what is not divisible at all, and the relative one (al-wahid al-idafi), which is divisible but not into things equal in reality, like man divided into different limbs: hand, foot, head, etc., for they are not equal in essence. And excluded from the definition is what is divided into things equal in essence, like a group of drops of honey or water and the like. And al-Imam (al-Juwayni) said in al-Irshad: 'The one (al-wahid) in the terminology of the usuliyyun is the thing that is not divisible.'
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. It opens a new chapter on Oneness (al-wahdaniyya). The author first presents a preliminary discussion on the meaning of unity (al-wahda) and its divisions. He quotes two authorities: al-Baydawi and al-Juwayni. Al-Baydawi's definition: unity is the state of a thing not being divisible into things that share the same essence. This definition covers two types: real unity (al-wahid al-haqiqi), which is absolutely indivisible (only God), and relative unity (al-wahid al-idafi), which is divisible but its parts are not equal in essence, e.g. a human body with different organs. It excludes things divisible into equal parts, like a group of identical drops. Then al-Juwayni gives a simpler definition: the one is that which is not divisible. The author uses these to set the stage for discussing divine oneness.

Key Terms

واحد حقيقي
واحد إضافي
قسم
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity only applies to God.
Correction: Sanusi shows there is also relative unity applicable to created things like the human body.

Checkpoint
According to al-Baydawi's definition, which of the following is an example of relative unity (al-wahid al-idafi)?
A) A group of identical drops of water
B) A human being with different limbs
C) The essence of God
D) A pile of identical coins

Key Takeaway
Unity is not a single concept; real unity (absolute indivisibility) applies only to Allah, while relative unity (divisible into unequal parts) applies to composite creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The definition of real unity excludes any division. God is not composed of parts. Does your inner concept of God sometimes include imagined 'parts' (e.g., His mercy separate from His justice)? How can you purify your tawhid based on this lesson?
?
If everything other than Allah is a relative unity, divisible into dissimilar parts, then nothing in creation is truly self-sufficient. How does this affect your reliance on people, wealth, or status?

The Six Types of Unity in Kalam
Sanusi enumerates six types of unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six distinct senses in which a thing can be called 'one' in Islamic theology, and why distinguishing them is essential for discussing tawhid.

Core Idea
"One" is not a single concept — theology recognizes six distinct types of unity.

The passage you are about to read is a concise classification from Sanusi's foundational text. Before he can affirm that God is truly one, he must clarify what 'oneness' can mean. This list of six types — from the true one to accidental unity — provides the precise vocabulary needed to avoid confusion when discussing divine unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once without analysis. Let the author's classification come through clearly.
Notice that the list is introduced with 'fakathira' (they are many), but only six are given.
2
Identify each type of 'one'
Locate all six Arabic terms: al-wahid al-haqiqi, al-wahid bi al-shakhs, al-wahid bi al-jins, al-wahid bi al-naw', al-wahid bi al-fasl, al-wahid bi al-'arad.
The first type, al-wahid al-haqiqi, is unique to God; the others apply to created things.
3
Understand the theological significance
Reflect on why Sanusi lists these: to distinguish God's oneness from created oneness. The true one (haqiqi) is indivisible in every respect.
This classification prevents equivocation when discussing tawhid.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you can recall the six types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أقسام الوحدة: فكثيرة؛ الواحد الحقيقي، والواحد بالشخص، والواحد بالجنس، والواحد بالنوع، والواحد بالفصل، والواحد بالعرض.
As for the divisions of unity: they are many: the true one (al-wahid al-haqiqi), the one by individual (al-wahid bi al-shakhs), the one by genus (al-wahid bi al-jins), the one by species (al-wahid bi al-naw‘), the one by differentia (al-wahid bi al-fasl), and the one by accident (al-wahid bi al-‘arad).
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of the different senses in which a thing can be called 'one.' The author lists six types. 1. The true one (al-wāḥid al-ḥaqīqī): that which is truly indivisible in every respect, applicable to God. 2. The one by individual (al-wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ): numerical identity, e.g., a particular person. 3. The one by genus (al-wāḥid bi al-jins): unity in genus, e.g., all humans share the same genus 'animal.' 4. The one by species (al-wāḥid bi al-naw‘): unity in species, e.g., all humans share the species 'rational animal.' 5. The one by differentia (al-wāḥid bi al-faṣl): unity in the defining difference, e.g., all humans share the differentia 'rational.' 6. The one by accident (al-wāḥid bi al-‘araḍ): accidental unity, e.g., a white thing and a sweet thing both being white. This classification helps avoid equivocation when discussing divine unity and other theological topics.

Key Terms
الواحد الحقيقي
الواحد بالشخص
الواحد بالجنس
الواحد بالنوع
الواحد بالفصل
الواحد بالعرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'one' only means numerical oneness.
Correction: In theology, 'one' has multiple senses; the true one (al-wahid al-haqiqi) is the most fundamental for tawhid, distinct from created unities.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Recognizing the six types of unity prevents confusion and highlights that God's oneness is the true, indivisible one—al-wahid al-haqiqi.

Look at the list of six types of unity. What specific change will you make this week to correct that?

The lesson shows that many things can be called 'one' in different ways, but only Allah deserves the label 'true one.' The Seven Divisions of Unity
Sanusi enumerates seven kinds of unity and explains their classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven kinds of unity (al-wāḥid) and the logical reasoning that underlies their classification.

Core Idea
Unity is not one: there are seven distinct types based on divisibility and predication.

Sanusi presents a meticulous classification of the concept of 'unity' (al-waḥdah). Before we can speak of God's oneness, we must understand the different ways things can be called 'one' in logic and language. This passage lays out seven divisions and the reasoning behind them, showing how each type of unity relates to essence, accident, and predication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once with translation. Notice the list of six main types and the subsequent subdivisions.
The passage begins abruptly listing types; the rationale comes later.
2
Identify the seven divisions
Find the seven final categories: real, individual (with two subtypes), generic, specific, differentia, accidental (with two subtypes). Count them.
The individual and accidental each split into two, giving 1+2+1+1+1+2 = 7.
3
Understand the rationale
The text provides a reasoning tree: a one is either indivisible (real) or divisible. Then it splits based on predication and aspect of unity. Follow the logical divisions.
The reasoning starts from 'that which cannot be divided in any way'.
4
Map the examples
Note the examples: Zayd for individual, Zayd and Amr in humanity for specific, man and horse in animal for generic, etc. Connect each example to its division.
Examples help solidify the abstract categories.
5
Reflect on the purpose
Ask why theologians need this classification. It helps clarify that God's unity is unique (real one) and cannot be of any other kind.
This connects to tawḥīd and avoiding anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أقسام الوحدة : فكثيرةٌ ؛ الواحد الحقيقي، والواحد بالشخص، والواحد بالجنس، والواحد بالنوع، والواحد بالفصل، والواحد بالعرض .
ثم الواحد بالشخص : إما واحد بالاتصال، أو واحد بالاجتماع؛ ويسمى الواحد بالتركيب، والواحد بالارتباط.
ثم الواحد بالعرض : إما واحد بالمحمول، وإما واحد بالموضوع.
فهذه أقسام سبعة، ووجه التقسيم إليها : أن الواحد : إما أن يكون بحيث لا ينقسم بوجه من الوجوه أو لا، والأول : الواحد الحقيقي، والثاني : إما أن يكون بحيث يمتنع حمله على كثيرين؛ كزيد؛ فهو الواحد بالشخص، وإما أن يكون بحيث لا يمتنع حمله على كثيرين، ولا بد أن يكون واحداً من وجه كثيراً من وجه، ويجب تغاير الوجهين لتنافيهما، وإذا كانا كذلك فجهة الواحد : إما أن تكون نفس الماهية لمعروض الكثرة، أو جزءاً منها، أو خارجاً عنها. والأول : هو الواحد بالنوع؛ كاتحاد زيد وعمرو في الإنسانية. والثاني ـ وهو جزء الماهية - : إما أن يعم حقيقتين فأكثر؛ وهو الواحد بالجنس؛ كاتحاد الإنسان والفرس في الحيوان، أو يختص بحقيقة واحدة؛ وهو الواحد بالفصل؛ كاتحاد زيد وعمرو في النطق.
والثالث ـ وهو الواحد بالعرض - : قسمان؛ لأنه إما أن تكون جهة الاتحاد محمولة على المتعدد؛ كاتحاد القطن والثلج في حمل البياض عليهما، ويسمى الواحد بالمحمول، أو تكون جهة الاتحاد موضوعة له؛ كاتحاد الضاحك والكاتب في وضع الإنسان لهما؛ أي : يحملان
As for the divisions of unity (al-waḥdah): they are many: the real one (al-wāḥid al-ḥaqīqī), the individual one (al-wāḥid bi-al-shakhṣ), the generic one (al-wāḥid bi-al-jins), the specific one (al-wāḥid bi-al-nawʿ), the differentia one (al-wāḥid bi-al-faṣl), and the accidental one (al-wāḥid bi-al-ʿaraḍ). Then the individual one: either one by connection (ittiṣāl) or one by aggregation (ijtimāʿ); it is called the one by composition (tarkīb) and the one by linkage (irtibāṭ). Then the accidental one: either one by predicate (maḥmūl) or one by subject (mawḍūʿ). So these are seven divisions. The reason for dividing them is that the one: either is such that it cannot be divided in any way or not. The first is the real one. The second: either is such that its predication of many is impossible, like Zayd; it is the individual one. Or it is such that its predication of many is not impossible, and it must be one in one respect and many in another, and the two respects must be different because they are mutually exclusive. If so, then the aspect of unity: either it is the essence itself of the substrate of multiplicity, or a part of it, or outside it. The first is the specific one; like the unity of Zayd and ʿAmr in humanity. The second—and it is a part of the essence—: either it encompasses two or more realities, and it is the generic one; like the unity of man and horse in animal, or it is specific to one reality, and it is the differentia one; like the unity of Zayd and ʿAmr in rationality. And the third—and it is the accidental one—: two kinds; because either the aspect of unity is predicated of the multiple, like the unity of cotton and snow in the predication of whiteness upon them, and it is called the one by predicate; or the aspect of unity is a subject for it, like the unity of 'the laughing' and 'the writing' in the subjection of man for them, i.e., they are predicated
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage classifies the kinds of unity (al-waḥdah). It first lists six types: real (ḥaqīqī), individual (shakhṣ), generic (jins), specific (nawʿ), differentia (faṣl), and accidental (ʿaraḍ). Then it subdivides the individual into two: by connection (ittiṣāl) and by aggregation (ijtimāʿ), also called by composition and linkage. It subdivides the accidental into two: by predicate (maḥmūl) and by subject (mawḍūʿ). The text states these yield seven divisions. The rationale is: a one is either indivisible in every respect (real one) or divisible. The divisible is further divided into that which cannot be predicated of many (individual) and that which can. The latter must be one in one respect and many in another. The aspect of unity can be the essence itself (giving the specific one, e.g., Zayd and ʿAmr share humanity), a part of the essence (which is either general—giving the generic one, e.g., man and horse share animal—or specific—giving the differentia one, e.g., Zayd and ʿAmr share rationality), or outside the essence (giving the accidental one, which is either the aspect is predicated of the multiple—like whiteness on cotton and snow—or the multiple are predicates of a subject—like 'laughing' and 'writing' are predicated of man). The text concludes abruptly with 'i.e., they are predicated'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All seven divisions are distinct and mutually exclusive categories at the same level.
Correction: Some divisions are subtypes: individual one includes two subtypes (connection/aggregation), accidental one includes two subtypes (predicate/subject). The categories form a hierarchy, not a flat list.

Checkpoint

A) It can be predicated of many

D) It is accidental

Key Takeaway
The concept of unity is systematically analyzed into seven types, with the real one being absolutely indivisible and all others involving some aspect of divisibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Seven Types of Unity
Sanusi enumerates and explains the seven types of unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven types of unity in Islamic theology and how they are derived from the fundamental division between the indivisible true one and the divisible one.

Core Idea
The seven types of unity form a logical taxonomy that clarifies how things can be one.

This passage from Sanusi presents a detailed classification of the concept of 'the one' (al-wāḥid). It is not about God's oneness directly but about the logical types of unity used to analyze creation. Understanding these types helps avoid confusion when discussing God's unity, which is entirely different and indivisible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: the passage moves from a major division to subdivisions.
2
Identify the initial major division
Find the first division: the one that is indivisible (the true one) vs. the one that is divisible. This is the foundation of the taxonomy.
The true one (al-wāḥid al-ḥaqīqī) is not further subdivided.
3
Trace the subdivisions of the divisible one
The divisible one is further divided based on whether it can be predicated of many. Follow the branches: one by individual, then species/genus/differentia, and one by accident.
Each subdivision has specific examples like Zayd, animal, whiteness, etc.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why Sanusi goes through all these divisions. How does this taxonomy help in discussing God's oneness?
This logical analysis shows that created unity is always composite in some sense, unlike God's simple unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الواحد بالشخص : إما واحدٌ بالاتصال، أو واحد بالاجتماع؛ ويُسمَّى الواحد بالتركيب، والواحد بالارتباط. ثم الواحد بالعَرَضِ : إمَّا واحد بالمحمول، وإما واحد بالموضوع. فهذه أقسام سبعة، ووجه التقسيم إليها : أنَّ الواحد : إمَّا أَنْ يكون بحيثُ لا ينقسم بوجهِ مِنَ الوجوه أو لا، والأوَّلُ : الواحد الحقيقي، والثاني : إما أنْ يكونَ بحيثُ يمتنع حمله على كثيرينَ ؛ كزيد ؛ فهو الواحد بالشخص، وإما أن يكون بحيثُ لا يمتنع حمله على كثيرين، ولا بدَّ أنْ يكونَ واحداً مِنْ وَجْهِ كثيراً مِنْ وَجْهِ، ويجب تغاير الوجهين لتنافيهما، وإذا كانا كذلك فجهة الواحدِ : إِمَّا أَنْ تكونَ نفس الماهية لمعروض الكثرة، أو جزءاً منها، أو خارجاً عنها . والأوَّلُ : هو الواحد بالنوع؛ كاتحادِ زيد وعمرو في الإنسانية . والثاني ـ وهو جزء الماهية - : إمَّا أنْ يعم حقيقتين فأكثرَ؛ وهو الواحد بالجنس؛ كاتحادِ الإنسان والفرس في الحيوانِ، أو يختص بحقيقة واحدة؛ وهو الواحدُ بالفَصْلِ؛ كاتحادِ زيد وعمرو في النُّطْقِ. والثالث ـ وهو الواحدُ بالعَرَضِ - : قسمانِ؛ لأنَّهُ إِمَّا أَنْ تَكونَ جهةً الاتحادِ محمولة على المتعدِّدِ؛ كاتحادِ القطن والثلج في حَمْلِ البياضِ عليهما، ويُسمَّى الواحد بالمحمول، أو تكونَ جهة الاتحادِ موضوعةً له؛ كاتحادِ الضاحك والكاتب في وضع الإنسان لهما؛ أي : يُحملان عليه، ويُسمَّى الواحد بالموضوع. ثم الواحد بالشخص القابلُ للقسمةِ : إِمَّا أَنْ تكونَ الأقسام التي تحصل بالقسمة متشابهةً بالاسم والحد، وهو الواحد بالاتصال، سواء كانَ قَبوله للقسمة لذاته كالمقدارِ، أو لغيره كالجسم البسيط؛ فإنَّهُ يقبلها بواسطة المقدارِ، أو تكونَ الأقسام مختلفةً؛ كالبدنِ المنقسم إلى الأعضاء المختلفة، وهو الواحد بالاجتماع، ويُسمَّى الواحد بالتركيب والواحد بالارتباطِ.
Then the one by individual (al-wāḥid bi-l-shakhṣ): either one by continuity (ittiṣāl) or one by aggregation (ijtimāʿ); and it is called one by composition (tarkīb) and one by connection (irtibāṭ). Then the one by accident (al-wāḥid bi-l-ʿaraḍ): either one by predicate (maḥmūl) or one by subject (mawḍūʿ). So these are seven divisions. The basis of the division is that the one: either is such that it is indivisible in any respect, or not. The first: the true one (al-wāḥid al-ḥaqīqī). The second: either such that it cannot be predicated of many, like Zayd, so it is the one by individual; or such that it can be predicated of many, and it must be one in one respect and many in another respect, and the two respects must be different because they are mutually exclusive. If so, then the aspect of unity: either it is the essence itself of the subject of multiplicity, or a part of it, or external to it. The first: the one by species (al-wāḥid bi-l-nawʿ), like the unity of Zayd and ʿAmr in humanity. The second—which is a part of the essence—either applies to two or more realities: it is the one by genus (al-wāḥid bi-l-jins), like the unity of man and horse in animal; or is specific to one reality: it is the one by differentia (al-wāḥid bi-l-faṣl), like the unity of Zayd and ʿAmr in rationality. The third—which is the one by accident—has two types: either the aspect of unity is predicated of the multiple, like the unity of cotton and snow in the predicate whiteness (bayāḍ) applying to them, and it is called one by predicate; or the aspect of unity is a subject for them, like the unity of the laughing and the writing in the subject man (insān) for them—i.e., they are predicated of it—and it is called one by subject. Then the one by individual that is divisible: either the divisions obtained by division are similar in name and definition, which is the one by continuity, whether its capacity for division is essential, like magnitude (miqdār), or accidental, like a simple body (jism basīṭ), for it receives division through magnitude; or the divisions are different, like the body divided into different organs, which is the one by aggregation, called one by composition and one by connection.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage classifies the concept of 'the one' (al-wāḥid) into seven logical types, a standard kalam taxonomy used to analyze unity. The author begins by listing two preliminary subdivisions: the one by individual (al-wāḥid bi-l-shakhṣ) is subdivided into one by continuity (ittiṣāl) and one by aggregation (ijtimāʿ), also called one by composition (tarkīb) and one by connection (irtibāṭ). The one by accident (al-wāḥid bi-l-ʿaraḍ) is subdivided into one by predicate (maḥmūl) and one by subject (mawḍūʿ). He then states these are seven divisions and explains the basis of division. The first major division is whether the one is indivisible in any respect (the true one) or not. The second major division (the divisible one) is further divided based on predicability: if it cannot be predicated of many, it is the one by individual; if it can be predicated of many, it is one in one respect and many in another, requiring two different respects. The respect of unity can be the essence itself (one by species), a part of the essence (either common to many realities as genus or specific as differentia), or external to the essence (one by accident, which is either by predicate or subject). Finally, the one by individual that is divisible is split: if its divisions are similar, it is one by continuity (e.g., magnitude or a simple body receiving division through magnitude); if divisions are different, it is one by aggregation (e.g., a body with different organs). This logical framework is foundational for discussing God's oneness, which negates all forms of composition and multiplicity.

الواحد بالنوع
الواحد بالجنس
الواحد بالفصل

Misconception: Some may think that the 'one by individual' (e.g., Zayd) is indivisible like the true one.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the one by individual is divisible, unlike the true one which is indivisible in any respect.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The seven types of unity show that all created unity involves some form of composition or multiplicity, unlike God's absolute oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life appears continuous like a flowing river, but the passage says continuity is divisible. In what daily habit do you assume an unbroken 'flow' that is actually composite, and how can you redirect that reliance to the truly indivisible One?
?
The 'one by aggregation' describes your body as a collection of different organs. You often say 'I' as if it is a simple self. Where in your character do you see a conflict of parts (desires, duties) that reveals your dependence on the One who is not composed?
?
The passage teaches that your humanity (species-unity) is shared with others. How does this truth humble your pride and remind you that your ultimate identity is not in being human but in being a servant of the uniquely One?

Types of Unity (al-Wāḥid) in Classical Logic
Sanusi classifies types of unity: by subject, by person, and by connection vs. composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars classified different meanings of 'one' (al-wāḥid) to clarify that God's oneness transcends all division.

Core Idea
The oneness of God negates all forms of divisibility.

Before we can properly understand the uniqueness of God's oneness, we need to see what kinds of 'oneness' exist in the world. Sanusi introduces a philosophical classification of unity, distinguishing between unity by subject, unity by person, and the various ways something can be divisible. This lays the groundwork for affirming that Allah's oneness is unlike any created unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, then look at the translation. Notice the terms used for each type of oneness.
The passage is dense but concise.
2
Identify the two primary categories
The passage first mentions 'one by subject' and then 'one by person'. The latter is divisible. Can you see how they are related?
3
Distinguish the divisible subtypes
The divisible 'one by person' is split into two: one where parts are similar (one by connection) and one where parts are different (one by composition). Find the examples given.
The example of magnitude helps understand 'one by connection'.
4
Reflect on theological significance
Consider why this classification is important for kalam: it shows that everything created has some form of division, but Allah's oneness is beyond all of these.
This prepares for affirming tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عليه ، ويُسمَّى الواحد بالموضوع ثم الواحد بالشخص القابلُ للقسمةِ : إِمَّا أَنْ تكونَ الأقسام التي تحصل بالقسمة متشابهةً بالاسم والحد ، وهو الواحد بالاتصال ، سواء كانَ قَبوله للقسمة لذاته كالمقدارِ ، أو لغيره كالجسم البسيط ؛ فإنَّهُ يقبلها بواسطة المقدارِ ، أو تكونَ الأقسام مختلفةً ؛ كالبدنِ المنقسم إلى الأعضاء المختلفة ، وهو الواحد بالاجتماع ، ويُسمَّى الواحد بالتركيب والواحد بالارتباطِ .
upon it, and it is called 'one by subject,' then 'one by person' which is divisible: either the parts obtained by division are similar in name and definition, and it is 'one by connection,' whether its divisibility is due to itself like magnitude, or due to another like a simple body—for it accepts division through magnitude—or the parts are different, like the body divided into different organs, and it is 'one by composition,' and it is called 'one by synthesis' and 'one by connection.'
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage classifies the different meanings of 'one' (al-wāḥid) as a logical and philosophical concept. It begins by mentioning the 'one by subject' (al-wāḥid bil-mawḍūʿ) and 'one by person' (al-wāḥid bil-shakhṣ) which is divisible. For the divisible one, it distinguishes two cases: first, the parts obtained through division are identical in name and definition—this is 'one by connection' (al-wāḥid bil-ittiṣāl), which applies whether the thing is divisible by itself like magnitude, or through another like a simple body which is divisible through magnitude. Second, the parts are different, like a body divided into different organs—this is 'one by composition' (al-wāḥid bil-ijtimāʿ), also called 'one by synthesis' (al-wāḥid bil-tarkīb) and 'one by connection' (al-wāḥid bil-irtibāṭ). The author explains the basis for each type, setting the stage for understanding that God's oneness negates all these kinds of division.

Key Takeaway

Critique of the reported view of Imam al-Haramayn on created power
Sanusi rejects the claim that created power affects acts non-independently.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi rejects even a mitigated attribution of effect to created power in Ash'ari theology.

Core Idea
Created power has no effect at all; only Allah creates actions.

In this lesson, Sanusi addresses a view attributed to Imam al-Haramayn that attempts a middle path: created power affects actions non-independently. Sanusi cuts off this compromise, declaring it an undesirable statement not to be held. This reinforces the core Ash'ari principle that all causal efficacy belongs solely to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully and its literal translation. Note the structure: the report is presented, then qualified, then rejected.
The footnote markers (1) and (2) are from the original text.
2
Identify the reported view
What exactly is being attributed to Imam al-Haramayn? Created power affects acts, but not independently, only according to God's decrees.
This is an attempted middle ground between Qadariyya and strict Ash'arism.
3
Understand Sanusi's judgment
Sanusi calls it 'undesirable' (marghūb ʿanh) and says it is not correct to hold. This is a clear rejection.
Pay attention to the strong language: 'لا يصح القول به'
4
Connect to the Qadariyya error
The Qadariyya claimed independent effect. The reported view tries to avoid that, but still ascribes some effect to creation, which is the problem.
Ash'arism says only Allah creates actions; humans acquire them.
5
Reflect on the broader principle
This lesson shows that in Ash'ari theology, any attribution of effect to created power is unacceptable, even if it's non-independent.
This preserves tawhid in causal efficacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما نقل عن إمام الحرمين في تأثير القدرة الحادثة ، وكذا ما نقل عن القاضي والأستاذ وما نقل عن إمام الحرمين من أن له قولاً بأن القدرة الحادثة تؤثر في الأفعال (1)، لكن لا على سبيل الاستقلال كما تقول القدرية ، بل على أقدار قدرها الله تعالى (2) . . فهو قول مرغوب عنه ، لا يصح القول به
What was narrated from Imam al-Haramayn regarding the effect of the created power, and likewise what was narrated from al-Qadi and al-Ustadh, and what was narrated from Imam al-Haramayn that he had a statement that the created power affects the acts (1), but not independently as the Qadariyya say, but rather according to decrees which Allah has decreed (2)... it is an undesirable statement; it is not correct to hold it.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage is an evaluation of a specific theological report attributed to Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) and others (al-Qadi and al-Ustadh) concerning the role of created power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) in human actions. The reported view is that created power does affect actions, but not independently (istiqlāl) as the Qadariyya (who affirm human free will and power independent of God) would hold, but rather according to decrees that God has determined. Sanusi immediately rejects this view, calling it 'undesirable' (marghūb ʿanh) and not valid to hold. The passage functions as a doctrinal warning: even a mitigated attribution of effect to created power is unacceptable in the Ash'ari framework, where all effect belongs to God alone. The commentary follows the author's order: first stating the report, then the qualification, then the rejection.

استقلال
قدرية

Correction: Sanusi states this is an undesirable statement and not correct; all effect belongs to Allah alone.

Key Takeaway
Even a qualified, non-independent effect for created power is rejected in Ash'ari theology; all efficacy is Allah's alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Sanusi calls the reported view 'undesirable' and 'not correct to hold.' Are there any beliefs you hold that are comfortable but theologically unsound? How can you subject your own views to the same rigorous standard? Are you willing to abandon a cherished notion if it conflicts with the strict monotheism of causation?

The Kasb Theory: The Servant's Role in Acts
Al-Shahrastani explains and defends al-Qadi's kasb theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Shahrastani's distinction between God's creation of an act's existence and the servant's acquisition of its specific quality, and how the servant's power affects only that quality.

Core Idea
The servant's power shapes the moral quality of the act, not its existence—God does the rest.

This lesson unpacks a key debate in Ash'ari theology: how can humans be responsible for acts that God creates? Al-Shahrastani, siding with al-Qadi, argues that while God creates the act's existence, the servant's power determines its specific nature—prayer or theft. This distinction saves human accountability without compromising divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem of kasb
The passage begins by clarifying what the 'accident' (al-ḥāditha) affects: not existence, but the most specific description of the act. This sets up the central distinction.
Notice the disagreement between al-Qadi (states) and al-Ustādh (aspects).
2
Distinguish invention from acquisition
Al-Shahrastani explains: motion as motion is invented by God; the particular quality of that motion (prayer, theft) is acquired by the servant. The servant's power only touches that quality.
This is the heart of the theory.
3
See how moral responsibility follows
The specific quality is what commands and prohibitions address, and what reward or punishment is for. The servant's essence becomes the locus of the act, so he is described as moving, praying, etc.
4
Reflect on the Mu'tazili contrast
Al-Shahrastani later contrasts this with the Mu'tazila, but that is for the next lesson. For now, focus on the positive theory.
This lesson only covers the first part of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحادثة تؤثر في أخص وصف الفعل لا في وجوده، إلا أن القاضي يقول: إن أخص وصف الفعل حال، والأستاذ ينفي الأحوال، ويقول: إن أخص وصف الفعل وجه واعتبار. واختار الشهرستاني مذهب القاضي، وفرق بين وجهي الاختراع والكسب: بأن الحركة من حيث هي حركة تنسَب إلى فعل الله تعالى إيجاداً واختراعاً، ويلزم من ذلك علمه بها من جميع وجوهها وأنه لا يفعل في ذاته ولا يتصف بها اتصاف قيام، فلا تضاف إليه من العبد من حيث خصوصها، فيقال: أوجدها وأحدثها، ولا يقال: إنه متحرك بها، وتنسَب إلى العبد من حيث خصوصها؛ وهو كون تلك الصفة صلاة أو غصباً أو سرقةً، ولا تأثير لقدرة العبد إلا في ذلك الوجه، ولا يشترط علمه بالفعل من كل وجه، وذاته محل فعله وكسبه، وتكون صفة له، فيقال: إنه متحرك وساكن ومصل وغاصب، وإن اتصل به أمر فوقع على موافقته.. سمي طاعة وعبادة، وإن اتصل به نهي فوقع على خلافه.. سمي معصية وجريمة، وذلك الوجه هو المكلف به، وهو الذي توجه الخطاب به، فقيل: صل، ولا تسرق، ولا تغصب، وهو المقابل بالثواب والعقاب، والمدح والذم، لا من حيث إنه موجود؛ فإن ذلك الوجه لا تختلف فيه الأفعال. وقال: وهذا أعدل من قول المعتزلة؛ فإنهم أثبتوا الأشياء على حقائقها في العدم، والفاعل لا يفعل فيها غير الوجود عندهم، وهي حال لا يختلف معقولها باختلاف الحقائق، والتكليف لم يتوجه بطلب تلك الحال، بل الخصوصيات، وباعتبارها حسنت الأفعال وقبحت، وعلى تلك الخصوصية ورد المدح والذم، فما توجه به التكليف غير مقدور للعبد عندهم، والذي قدر عليه لم يتوجه به التكليف، بخلاف ما ذهب إليه القاضي، فكان ما صار إليه مطابقاً للقضايا العقلية والشرعية معاً. قال شرف الدين بن التلمساني: وما ذكروه وإن كان فيه خروج ألا عن تشنيعات المعتزلة، وعن إلزام التكليف بالمحال؛ بتقدير يكون لقدرة العبد تأثير ألبتة، كما صار إليه الأشعري ومن وافقه؛ حيث قالوا للأشعرية: إن حاصل التكليف يكون على هذا التقدير افعل يا من لا فعل له، وافعل ما أنا فاعله.. إلا أنه ضعيف؛ فإن معتمد القاضي وأصحابه في نسبة سائر الممكنات إلى الله تعالى؛ فليس تخصيص بعضها بأولى من بعض، وذلك يطرد إمكانها
The accident/case affects the most specific description of the act, not its existence. But al-Qadi says: The most specific description of the act is a state (hāl), and the Master (al-Ustādh) negates states and says: The most specific description of the act is an aspect (wajh) and a consideration (i‘tibār). Al-Shahrastānī chose the doctrine of al-Qadi, and he differentiated between the two aspects of invention (ikhtirā‘) and acquisition (kasb): that motion, inasmuch as it is motion, is attributed to the act of God, may He be exalted, as existence (ījād) and invention, and from that follows His knowledge of it from all its aspects, and that He does not act in His essence nor is He described by it with a description of inherence (qiyām), so it is not attributed to Him from the servant in respect of its particularity (khuṣūṣiyya); so it is said: He brought it into existence and originated it, and it is not said: He is moving by it. And it is attributed to the servant in respect of its particularity, which is that attribute being prayer or usurpation or theft. The power of the servant has no effect except in that aspect, and his knowledge of the act from every aspect is not required, and his essence is the locus of his act and his acquisition, and it becomes an attribute for him; so it is said: he is moving and still, praying and usurping. And if an command attaches to it and it occurs in accordance with it, it is called obedience and worship; and if a prohibition attaches to it and it occurs contrary to it, it is called disobedience and crime. That aspect is what one is obligated (mukallaf) with, and it is what the address (khiṭāb) is directed to; so it is said: Pray, and do not steal, and do not usurp. And it is what is met with reward and punishment, praise and blame, not inasmuch as it is existent; for that aspect does not differ among acts. And he (al-Shahrastānī) said: This is more just than the saying of the Mu‘tazila; for they affirmed things in their realities in non-existence (al-‘adam), and the agent does not do anything in them except existence according to them, and it is a state whose intelligible content does not differ with the difference of realities, and the obligation (taklīf) is not directed to seeking that state, but rather the particularities (khuṣūṣiyyāt). By consideration of these, acts become good or evil, and upon that particularity come praise and blame. So what the obligation is directed to is not within the power of the servant according to them, and what he is capable of is not what the obligation is directed to, unlike what al-Qadi held. So what he arrived at is in accordance with both rational and legal principles together. Sharaf al-Dīn ibn al-Tilmisānī said: What they mentioned, even if it contains an escape [except?] from the reproaches of the Mu‘tazila and from the imposition of impossible obligation, on the assumption that the servant's power has any effect at all, as al-Ash‘arī and those who agreed with him held—for they said to the Ash‘arites: The outcome of the obligation on this assumption is: 'Do, O you who have no act!' and 'Do what I am doing!'—but it is weak; for the reliance of al-Qadi and his companions in the attribution of all possible things to God, the Exalted, is that the specification of some of them over others is not more appropriate, and that necessitates their possibility.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage is an exposition and defense of a specific theory of kasb (acquisition) attributed to al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, as adopted by al-Shahrastani. The argument proceeds as follows:

1. The passage begins by clarifying the nature of the 'most specific description' (akhaṣṣ waṣf) of an act. It states that the accidental event/al-ready state (al-ḥāditha) affects this description, not the act's existence. Al-Qadi holds that this most specific description is a 'state' (ḥāl), while the 'Master' (al-Ustādh, i.e., Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini) denies states and says it is an 'aspect' (wajh) and a 'consideration' (i‘tibār).
2. Al-Shahrastani chose al-Qadi's view and differentiated between the two aspects of invention (ikhtirā‘) and acquisition (kasb). The key idea: motion qua motion is the act of God, involving existence and invention. From this follows God's complete knowledge of it and that God is not described by it as an inherent attribute—so we do not say He is moving by it. The same motion, in its particularity (e.g., being prayer, theft), is attributed to the servant. The servant's power (qudra) only affects this aspect, not the existence. The servant's essence becomes the locus of this act and its acquisition, so he is described as moving, still, praying, etc.
3. Moral and legal evaluation depends on this specific aspect. If the act conforms to a command, it is obedience; if it violates a prohibition, it is disobedience. This aspect is what the obligation (taklīf) addresses, what reward and punishment are for, not the existence per se.
4. al-Shahrastani then compares this view favorably to the Mu‘tazila position. The Mu‘tazila hold that things have realities in non-existence, and God only brings them into existence, not affecting their specific natures. The obligation is not about existence but about these specific natures. However, they claim that the servant's power is limited to the act of existence? The passage says: the Mu‘tazila say that what the obligation is directed to is not within the servant's power, and what is within his power is not what the obligation is directed to. This is a criticism: they separate the object of obligation from what the servant can do. Al-Qadi's view avoids this by making the specific quality both the object of obligation and what the servant's power acts upon.
5. Finally, Ibn al-Tilmisani adds a note: although al-Shahrastani's account escapes the standard Ash‘ari problem (that obligation becomes impossible: 'Do, O you who have no act!') because it gives the servant's power some effect, it is still weak. The weakness lies in al-Qadi's principle that all possible things are equally attributable to God; specification of some to God is arbitrary, which undermines the distinction. But the passage ends by stating that this leads to the possibility (imkān) of the acts.

Key Terms
أخص وصف الفعل
الكسب
الاختراع
الأحوال
الخصوصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: In kasb, the servant's power is completely ineffective; everything is from God.
Correction: The passage states that the servant's power affects the specific quality of the act (the 'aspect'), not its existence. So the servant contributes to the moral character of the act.

Checkpoint
According to al-Shahrastani's account, what does the servant's power (qudra) affect?
A) The existence of the act
B) The most specific description of the act
C) Both existence and description
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Al-Shahrastani's kasb theory allows the servant's power to affect the moral quality of the act, preserving accountability without compromising God's sole creative power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every motion you make is created by God, does that make you feel less responsible for your sins? Yet this passage insists your power affects the 'particularity'—the moral quality. Is there a sin you have excused by thinking it was 'God's will'? What would it mean to truly own that 'particularity' as yours and repent accordingly?
?
When you perform an act of worship like prayer, do you ever reduce it to mere physical motion, forgetting that you are responsible for its 'particularity'—your intention and mindfulness? How will you ensure that your next prayer is truly 'acquisition' (kasb) and not just motion?
?
The passage says commands target the 'particularity', not existence. You are not commanded to 'exist motion' but to 'pray' or 'not steal'. Is your obedience merely outward, or are you cultivating the inner quality that makes your acts worship? What is one 'particularity' in your life today that you must change from sin to obedience?

Al-Shahrastani's Refutation of Mu'tazila on Human Agency
Al-Shahrastani contrasts his kasb theory with the Mu'tazila and gets a critique from Ibn al-Tilmisani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Shahrastani's argument that his kasb theory is more just than the Mu'tazila position, and the criticism by Ibn al-Tilmisani.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila separate the object of obligation from what the servant can do; al-Shahrastani reunites them.

After outlining his preferred kasb theory, al-Shahrastani contrasts it with the Mu'tazila view. He argues that the Mu'tazila, by claiming that things have realities in non-existence and that God only brings existence, make the object of obligation (specific qualities) beyond the servant's power. His own view aligns obligation and capability. Ibn al-Tilmisani then offers a critique of al-Qadi's principle.

Guided Path
1
Learn the Mu'tazila position
The passage summarizes Mu'tazila view: things have fixed realities in non-existence; God only adds existence. The servant's power, for them, affects existence? Actually, the passage says they separate obligation from capability.
Read carefully: 'فما توجه به التكليف غير مقدور للعبد عندهم، والذي قدر عليه لم يتوجه به التكليف'.
2
See al-Shahrastani's critique
He says the Mu'tazila make the object of obligation (specific qualities) beyond the servant's power, while what the servant can do (existence) is not the object of obligation. This is unjust.
Al-Shahrastani calls his view 'أعدل' (more just).
3
Understand Ibn al-Tilmisani's objection
Ibn al-Tilmisani notes that al-Shahrastani's account escapes some problems of standard Ash'arism, but it is weak because al-Qadi's principle of equal possibility makes attribution to God arbitrary.
The passage ends abruptly, but the point is that the distinction between God and servant collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحادثة تؤثر في أخص وصف الفعل لا في وجوده، إلا أن القاضي يقول: إن أخص وصف الفعل حال، والأستاذ ينفي الأحوال، ويقول: إن أخص وصف الفعل وجه واعتبار. واختار الشهرستاني مذهب القاضي، وفرق بين وجهي الاختراع والكسب: بأن الحركة من حيث هي حركة تنسَب إلى فعل الله تعالى إيجاداً واختراعاً، ويلزم من ذلك علمه بها من جميع وجوهها وأنه لا يفعل في ذاته ولا يتصف بها اتصاف قيام، فلا تضاف إليه من العبد من حيث خصوصها، فيقال: أوجدها وأحدثها، ولا يقال: إنه متحرك بها، وتنسَب إلى العبد من حيث خصوصها؛ وهو كون تلك الصفة صلاة أو غصباً أو سرقةً، ولا تأثير لقدرة العبد إلا في ذلك الوجه، ولا يشترط علمه بالفعل من كل وجه، وذاته محل فعله وكسبه، وتكون صفة له، فيقال: إنه متحرك وساكن ومصل وغاصب، وإن اتصل به أمر فوقع على موافقته.. سمي طاعة وعبادة، وإن اتصل به نهي فوقع على خلافه.. سمي معصية وجريمة، وذلك الوجه هو المكلف به، وهو الذي توجه الخطاب به، فقيل: صل، ولا تسرق، ولا تغصب، وهو المقابل بالثواب والعقاب، والمدح والذم، لا من حيث إنه موجود؛ فإن ذلك الوجه لا تختلف فيه الأفعال. وقال: وهذا أعدل من قول المعتزلة؛ فإنهم أثبتوا الأشياء على حقائقها في العدم، والفاعل لا يفعل فيها غير الوجود عندهم، وهي حال لا يختلف معقولها باختلاف الحقائق، والتكليف لم يتوجه بطلب تلك الحال، بل الخصوصيات، وباعتبارها حسنت الأفعال وقبحت، وعلى تلك الخصوصية ورد المدح والذم، فما توجه به التكليف غير مقدور للعبد عندهم، والذي قدر عليه لم يتوجه به التكليف، بخلاف ما ذهب إليه القاضي، فكان ما صار إليه مطابقاً للقضايا العقلية والشرعية معاً. قال شرف الدين بن التلمساني: وما ذكروه وإن كان فيه خروج ألا عن تشنيعات المعتزلة، وعن إلزام التكليف بالمحال؛ بتقدير يكون لقدرة العبد تأثير ألبتة، كما صار إليه الأشعري ومن وافقه؛ حيث قالوا للأشعرية: إن حاصل التكليف يكون على هذا التقدير افعل يا من لا فعل له، وافعل ما أنا فاعله.. إلا أنه ضعيف؛ فإن معتمد القاضي وأصحابه في نسبة سائر الممكنات إلى الله تعالى؛ فليس تخصيص بعضها بأولى من بعض، وذلك يطرد إمكانها
The accident/case affects the most specific description of the act, not its existence. But al-Qadi says: The most specific description of the act is a state (hāl), and the Master (al-Ustādh) negates states and says: The most specific description of the act is an aspect (wajh) and a consideration (i‘tibār). Al-Shahrastānī chose the doctrine of al-Qadi, and he differentiated between the two aspects of invention (ikhtirā‘) and acquisition (kasb): that motion, inasmuch as it is motion, is attributed to the act of God, may He be exalted, as existence (ījād) and invention, and from that follows His knowledge of it from all its aspects, and that He does not act in His essence nor is He described by it with a description of inherence (qiyām), so it is not attributed to Him from the servant in respect of its particularity (khuṣūṣiyya); so it is said: He brought it into existence and originated it, and it is not said: He is moving by it. And it is attributed to the servant in respect of its particularity, which is that attribute being prayer or usurpation or theft. The power of the servant has no effect except in that aspect, and his knowledge of the act from every aspect is not required, and his essence is the locus of his act and his acquisition, and it becomes an attribute for him; so it is said: he is moving and still, praying and usurping. And if an command attaches to it and it occurs in accordance with it, it is called obedience and worship; and if a prohibition attaches to it and it occurs contrary to it, it is called disobedience and crime. That aspect is what one is obligated (mukallaf) with, and it is what the address (khiṭāb) is directed to; so it is said: Pray, and do not steal, and do not usurp. And it is what is met with reward and punishment, praise and blame, not inasmuch as it is existent; for that aspect does not differ among acts. And he (al-Shahrastānī) said: This is more just than the saying of the Mu‘tazila; for they affirmed things in their realities in non-existence (al-‘adam), and the agent does not do anything in them except existence according to them, and it is a state whose intelligible content does not differ with the difference of realities, and the obligation (taklīf) is not directed to seeking that state, but rather the particularities (khuṣūṣiyyāt). By consideration of these, acts become good or evil, and upon that particularity come praise and blame. So what the obligation is directed to is not within the power of the servant according to them, and what he is capable of is not what the obligation is directed to, unlike what al-Qadi held. So what he arrived at is in accordance with both rational and legal principles together. Sharaf al-Dīn ibn al-Tilmisānī said: What they mentioned, even if it contains an escape [except?] from the reproaches of the Mu‘tazila and from the imposition of impossible obligation, on the assumption that the servant's power has any effect at all, as al-Ash‘arī and those who agreed with him held—for they said to the Ash‘arites: The outcome of the obligation on this assumption is: 'Do, O you who have no act!' and 'Do what I am doing!'—but it is weak; for the reliance of al-Qadi and his companions in the attribution of all possible things to God, the Exalted, is that the specification of some of them over others is not more appropriate, and that necessitates their possibility.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage is an exposition and defense of a specific theory of kasb (acquisition) attributed to al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, as adopted by al-Shahrastani. The argument proceeds as follows:

1. The passage begins by clarifying the nature of the 'most specific description' (akhaṣṣ waṣf) of an act. It states that the accidental event/al-ready state (al-ḥāditha) affects this description, not the act's existence. Al-Qadi holds that this most specific description is a 'state' (ḥāl), while the 'Master' (al-Ustādh, i.e., Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini) denies states and says it is an 'aspect' (wajh) and a 'consideration' (i‘tibār).
2. Al-Shahrastani chose al-Qadi's view and differentiated between the two aspects of invention (ikhtirā‘) and acquisition (kasb). The key idea: motion qua motion is the act of God, involving existence and invention. From this follows God's complete knowledge of it and that God is not described by it as an inherent attribute—so we do not say He is moving by it. The same motion, in its particularity (e.g., being prayer, theft), is attributed to the servant. The servant's power (qudra) only affects this aspect, not the existence. The servant's essence becomes the locus of this act and its acquisition, so he is described as moving, still, praying, etc.
3. Moral and legal evaluation depends on this specific aspect. If the act conforms to a command, it is obedience; if it violates a prohibition, it is disobedience. This aspect is what the obligation (taklīf) addresses, what reward and punishment are for, not the existence per se.
4. al-Shahrastani then compares this view favorably to the Mu‘tazila position. The Mu‘tazila hold that things have realities in non-existence, and God only brings them into existence, not affecting their specific natures. The obligation is not about existence but about these specific natures. However, they claim that the servant's power is limited to the act of existence? The passage says: the Mu‘tazila say that what the obligation is directed to is not within the servant's power, and what is within his power is not what the obligation is directed to. This is a criticism: they separate the object of obligation from what the servant can do. Al-Qadi's view avoids this by making the specific quality both the object of obligation and what the servant's power acts upon.
5. Finally, Ibn al-Tilmisani adds a note: although al-Shahrastani's account escapes the standard Ash‘ari problem (that obligation becomes impossible: 'Do, O you who have no act!') because it gives the servant's power some effect, it is still weak. The weakness lies in al-Qadi's principle that all possible things are equally attributable to God; specification of some to God is arbitrary, which undermines the distinction. But the passage ends by stating that this leads to the possibility (imkān) of the acts.

Key Terms
التكليف
الوجود
الخصوصيات
المعتزلة
القاضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila view is perfectly consistent and provides full human agency.
Correction: Al-Shahrastani argues that the Mu'tazila separate obligation from capability, making the servant responsible for what he cannot do. His own view aligns them.

Checkpoint
According to al-Shahrastani, what is the main problem with the Mu'tazila position on human acts?
A) They deny God's creation of acts
B) They make the object of obligation beyond the servant's power
C) They affirm states (aḥwāl)
D) They deny human responsibility

Key Takeaway
Al-Shahrastani's kasb theory is presented as superior to Mu'tazila because it makes the object of obligation coincide with what the servant can affect, though Ibn al-Tilmisani finds it weak.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Shahrastani says the Mu'tazila separate what Allah commands from what you can do. In your life, is there a command of Allah you feel is beyond your ability? Be honest: are you using that feeling to avoid responsibility, or do you truly mean you cannot do it? What would it take for you to stop using that excuse?
?
Ibn al-Tilmisani's critique shows that even the most careful human reasoning cannot fully resolve the mysteries of divine power and human accountability. Does this humbling truth make you rely more on Allah's wisdom or less? When you encounter a theological tension that your mind cannot solve, how do you react—do you become anxious or do you trust that Allah knows better?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that you must have real power to be fairly obligated, but al-Shahrastani counters that this leads to obligation without power. Think of a time you failed a 'test' from Allah. Did you blame your lack of ability, or did you see it as a failure of your will? How does this lesson change how you view that moment?
