Kasb as the Object of Legal Obligation
Sanusi defines kasb as the act concomitant with acquired power and explains why it is the object of obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kasb—the act performed with acquired power—is the proper object of divine obligation, and how all acts are ultimately Allah's creation with conventional indications.

Core Idea
Kasb is the act concomitant with acquired power, and obligation is by divine convention, not by our power.

In this passage, Sanusi unpacks the phrase 'and it is the object of legal obligation' from his earlier definition of kasb. He clarifies that kasb—the existence of the predetermined act together with the acquired power—is what the Law obligates. He then explains that acts devoid of acquired power, like involuntary tremors, are graciously exempted, and that the connection between acts and reward is purely conventional, not rational.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the definition of kasb
Find where Sanusi defines kasb as 'the existence of the predetermined act with the acquired power' (وجود المقدور مع القدرة الحادثة). Understand that kasb is a specific type of act.
3
Understand why involuntary acts are not obligated
Notice the example of an involuntary tremor (حركة الارتعاش). Sanusi says Allah graciously exempted such acts from obligation. Ask yourself why this matters for the concept of responsibility.
4
Grasp the concept of conventional indication
The passage concludes that the indication between acts and reward/punishment is conventional (جعلي) not rational. This is key to understanding that obligation is a divine choice, not a necessary consequence of our power.
Contrast conventional indication with rational necessity.
5
Reflect on implications
Think about how this doctrine preserves Allah's sovereignty while explaining why some acts are obligated and others are not. The servant's power does not necessitate obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولي: (وهو مُتعلَّقُ التكليف الشرعي) أي: الكسب — وهو وجود المقدور معَ القدرةِ الحادثة - هو الذي كلَّفَ به الشرع فيما كلَّفَ؛ لأنَّ وقوع ذلك المقدور عارياً عن القدرة الحادثة - كحركة الارتعاش مثلاً - قد تفضَّلَ سبحانه بإسقاط التكليف به نفياً وإثباتاً ولو عكس سبحانَهُ التكليف، أو كلَّفَ بالجميع.. لكان حسناً؛ إذْ لا تأثير لقدرةِ المُكلَّف في الجميعِ، وإنَّما تلك الأفعال المخلوقةُ اللهِ تعالى، نصبَها الشرع عند اقترانها بأعراض حادثة كالقُدَرِ والإرادات.. أمارات على الثواب والعقاب؛ فبالوجه الذي صح جعلُ بعض أفعالِهِ سبحانه عند اقترانِهِ بفعل لهُ آخرَ أمارةً على ما شاءَ مِنْ ثواب أو عقاب أو غيرهما.. صح جعله مجرَّداً عن غيره، أو جعل غيره في مكانه أمارة على ذلكَ؛ لأنَّ الدلالة في ذلك جعليَّةٌ، لا عقليَّةٌ
And my saying: “And it is the object of legal obligation” means: kasb—which is the existence of the predetermined act with the acquired power—is that with which the Law has obligated in what it obligated; because the occurrence of that predetermined act devoid of the acquired power—like the movement of tremor for example—Allah Most High has graciously removed the obligation concerning it, both in affirmation and negation. And if He, glorified, had reversed the obligation, or obligated all acts, it would have been good; since there is no effect of the power of the obligated in all [such acts]. Rather, those acts are created by Allah Most High, the Law has set them, when they are accompanied by accidental occurrences like powers and wills, as indicators for reward and punishment. So by the same consideration that it is valid to make some of His actions—glorified be He—when accompanied by another action of His, an indicator for what He wills of reward or punishment or other things, it is valid to make it devoid of that other, or to make another in its place an indicator for that; because the indication in that is conventional (ja‘liyya), not rational.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage explains the phrase 'And it is the object of legal obligation' (وهو مُتعلَّقُ التكليف الشرعي). The author clarifies that 'it' refers to kasb, which is defined as the existence of the predetermined act (maqdūr) together with the acquired power (qudra ḥāditha). This is what the Law obligates. The reason is that if the act occurs without acquired power—like an involuntary tremor (حركة الارتعاش)—Allah has graciously exempted such acts from obligation, both in terms of commanding and forbidding (negation and affirmation). However, if Allah had reversed the obligation (i.e., obligated involuntary acts and exempted voluntary ones), or obligated all acts, it would have been equally good, since there is no effect of the servant's power in any of them. Rather, all acts are created by Allah. The Law has set them, when accompanied by accidents (a‘rāḍ) like powers and wills, as indicators (amārāt) for reward and punishment. The author then draws an analogy: just as it is valid for Allah to make one of His acts, when accompanied by another act of His, an indicator for what He wills of reward or punishment, it is also valid for Him to make it devoid of that other act, or to make another act in its place an indicator, because the indication in such matters is conventional (ja‘liyya), not rational. This underscores that the connection between acts and reward/punishment is by divine convention, not by any inherent effect of the servant's power.

Key Terms
الكسب
المقدور
القدرة الحادثة
التكليف الشرعي
حركة الارتعاش
الأعراض الحادثة
الأمارات
الدلالة الجعلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that obligation is based on the servant's independent power to produce acts.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that all acts are created by Allah, and the servant's power has no effect in producing them. Obligation is solely by divine convention—Allah chooses to link certain acts (those accompanied by acquired power) with reward or punishment.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is an involuntary tremor not an object of obligation?
A) Because it occurs without acquired power.
B) Because it is impossible for Allah to obligate such acts.
C) Because the servant has no power at all.
D) Because it is not created by Allah.

Key Takeaway
Kasb is the act performed with acquired power, and the Law obligates it, while acts without acquired power are exempt; the connection between acts and reward is conventional, not rational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that Allah could have obligated all acts, including your involuntary movements, but He graciously exempted them. How often do you take for granted the ease of being accountable only for what you consciously do? In light of this, how will you express gratitude for this divine leniency today?
?
If your worship—its acceptance and reward—depends solely on a conventional divine appointment rather than any inherent worth in your action, does that humble your pride? What specific change in your attitude towards your good deeds will you make after realizing this?

Refutation of Ibn Zakari's Claim on Generality of Negation
Sanusi refutes Ibn Zakari's misrepresentation of scholars' views on negated plurals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi refutes Ibn Zakari's misrepresentation of scholarly views on the generality of negated indefinite and definite nouns, and what the actual scholarly consensus is.

Core Idea
Negated indefinites are general in scope, not narrow.

In this passage, Sanusi directly takes on an opponent's claim about how Arabic negation works. Ibn Zakari had asserted that scholars of rhetoric say a negated plural—whether definite or indefinite—does not indicate generality of negation. Sanusi calls this a fabrication. As you read, notice how he marshals both scholarly consensus and Quranic usage to correct the record.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the opponent's claim
Look for the phrase 'حكايتُهُ عن علماء المعاني أنَّهم نصُّوا على أنَّ الجمعَ المنفي مُعرَّفاً أو مُنكَراً لا يفيد عموم النفي'. This is the claim Sanusi rejects.
2
See how Sanusi refutes it for the indefinite case
Notice his strong denial: 'لم ينص عليه أحد'. Then he states the actual position: indefinite in negative context is general, implicitly or explicitly.
Pay attention to the distinction between generic lā and other negatives.
3
Understand the internal dispute about scope
There is a minor disagreement among scholars whether the singular indefinite is more comprehensive than dual/plural. Sanusi mentions al-Qazwīnī, al-Sakkākī, and al-Taftāzānī.
This does not affect the core point of generality.
4
Analyze the case of definite nouns
Sanusi argues that scholars did not rule out generality for definite nouns; their apparent view is that they are like absolute (mujarrad) and gives Quranic examples.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمنها : حكايتُهُ عن علماء المعاني أنَّهم نصُّوا على أنَّ الجمعَ المنفي مُعرَّفاً أو مُنكَراً لا يفيد عموم النفي ، وإنَّما يفيد نفي العموم ، وهذا شيء لم ينص عليه أحدٌ منهم ولا مِنْ غيرِهم ؛ أعني : مِنْ كُلِّ مَنْ يقولُ بالعموم ، بل نضُوا على ضدّه في النكرة ، وأنَّها إذا كانَتْ في سياق النفْيِ تعمُّ ظاهراً معَ غيرِ ( لا ) الجنسيَّةِ ، ونصّاً معَ ( لا ) الجنسية (١) ، ولا فرق في ذلك بين أن تكون النكرة مفردةً ؛ نحو : لا رجل ، أو مثناةً ؛ نحو: لا رجلين ، أو مجموعة ؛ نحو: لا رجال ، وهذا ممَّا لا يختلفونَ فيهِ ، وإِنَّما نزاعُهم في استغراقِ المفرد : هل هو أشملُ مِن استغراق المثنى والمجموع ؛ وهو الذي نص عليه القزويني تبعاً للسكاكي (٢) ، أم هو في الجميع على حد السواء ؛ وإليهِ ميْلُ التفتازاني ، وبحثه وحججه في ذلك مشهورةٌ في مُطوَّلِهِ على التلخيص (۳) وأمَّا المعرَّفُ الذي تعلَّقَ بهِ النفْيُ فلم ينصُّوا على أنَّهُ ليسَ بعام ، بل ظاهر كلامهم أنَّهُ معَ النفْيِ كالمجرَّدِ ، وأكثر الاستعمال على ذلك ؛ نحو : ﴿ لَا يُحِبُّ الظَّالِمِينَ ﴾ [آل عمران : ٥٧] ، لَا يُحِبُّ الْمُعْتَدِينَ [البقرة : ١٩٠ ] ، ( وما للظالمين من ولي ولا نصير ) (٤) ، ومَا عَلَى
Among them: his report from scholars of meanings (al-maʿānī) that they stated that the negated plural, whether definite or indefinite, does not convey the generality of negation, but rather conveys the negation of generality. This is something that none of them stated, nor anyone else—I mean, of all those who affirm generality. Rather, they affirmed the opposite regarding the indefinite, and that when it occurs in the context of negation it is general, implicitly with other than the generic lā, and explicitly with the generic lā. There is no difference in that between the indefinite being singular, like 'lā rajul' (no man), or dual, like 'lā rajulayn' (no two men), or plural, like 'lā rijāl' (no men). This is something on which they do not differ. Their dispute is only concerning the scope of the singular: is it more comprehensive than the scope of the dual and plural—which is what al-Qazwīnī stated following al-Sakkākī—or is it equal in all? The inclination of al-Taftāzānī is towards the latter, and his investigation and proofs on that are well-known in his commentary (Muṭawwal) on al-Talkhīṣ. As for the definite that is attached to negation, they did not state that it is not general; rather, the apparent meaning of their statements is that with negation it is like the absolute, and most usage is upon that, such as: 'He does not love the wrongdoers' (Āl ʿImrān 3:57), 'He does not love the aggressors' (al-Baqara 2:190), and 'And the wrongdoers have no protector and no helper' (al-Shūrā 42:8), and 'and upon...'
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Ibn Zakari's misrepresentation of the views of scholars of rhetoric (maʿānī) concerning the generality of negated plurals. The author lists this as one of the 'types of disorder' in the opponent's argument. The opponent claimed that scholars hold that a negated plural, whether definite or indefinite, does not convey generality (umūm al-nafī) but rather conveys negation of generality (nafī al-ʿumūm). The author strongly denies this: no scholar of generality ever stated such a thing. Actually, they held the opposite for the indefinite: when an indefinite noun occurs in a negative context, it is general—implicitly with non-generic 'lā', and explicitly with generic 'lā'. This applies regardless of number (singular, dual, plural). The only point of dispute among scholars is whether the scope of the singular indefinite is broader than that of dual and plural (as al-Qazwīnī followed al-Sakkākī) or equal (as al-Taftāzānī inclined). As for the definite noun attached to negation, scholars did not explicitly say it is not general; rather, their statements imply it is like the absolute (mujarrad), and most usage supports that, citing Quranic examples like 'He does not love the wrongdoers' (3:57), 'He does not love the aggressors' (2:190), and 'And the wrongdoers have no protector and no helper' (42:8). The passage ends abruptly mid-sentence, likely continuing with more examples.

لا الجنسية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a negated indefinite noun (e.g., 'lā rajul') is not general and only negates the opposite.
Correction: Scholars of rhetoric affirm that an indefinite in a negative context is general, implicitly with non-generic lā and explicitly with generic lā.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what do scholars of meanings (al-maʿānī) say about an indefinite noun in a negative context?
A) It conveys the negation of generality.
B) It is general, implicitly with non-generic 'lā' and explicitly with generic 'lā'.
C) It is general only in the singular form.
D) It is not general unless it is definite.

Key Takeaway
Negated indefinite nouns in Arabic are generally understood to negate all individuals of that type, and this is the consensus of scholars of rhetoric.

Or do you subconsciously make exceptions for yourself or those you know? What would change in your life if you took that generality to heart?
?

Why 'Lan' Does Not Mean Eternal Negation
Sanusi refutes the claim that 'lan' indicates eternal negation using Quran and logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'lan' does not always indicate eternal negation, and how logical principles of temporal restriction apply to the interpretation of Quranic verses.

Core Idea
Context and logic reveal that 'lan' in 'lan tarani' is temporary, not eternal.

In this passage, Sanusi provides a second answer to the objection that 'lan' always means 'never'. He uses a Quranic example and logical reasoning to show that the negation in 'lan tarani' is restricted to the earthly life, not an absolute eternal denial. This lesson will explore his argument and its logical foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument come through.
Notice how Sanusi starts with a strong rejection of the opposing view.
2
Identify the Quranic counterexample
Find the verse about the Jews and how Sanusi uses it to show that 'lan' can be temporally restricted. The Jews will wish for death in the Fire, so 'lan' here is not eternal.
This is a direct refutation of the claim that 'lan' always means eternal negation.
3
Understand the logical principle
Sanusi explains that the negation of a temporally restricted request is itself temporally restricted. He gives an example from logic about Zaid moving his fingers at the time of writing.
This principle is key to understanding why 'lan tarani' applies only to this world.
4
Apply to the verse about Moses
Moses asked for a vision in this world, so the response negates it only in this world. Thus, 'lan tarani' does not preclude seeing Allah in the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن قولهم : إنَّ ( لن ( للتأبيد.. ممنوع (۱) ؛ لقوله في اليهودِ : وَلَن يَتَمَنَّوْهُ أَبَداً ﴾ [البقرة : ٩٥]، وهم يتمنونه في النار (٢) . ثم إنَّ الآيةَ جواب لسؤالِ موسى عليه السلام ، وهو إِنَّما سألَ رؤيةً ناجزة في الدنيا ، فالجواب يعود إلى سلب رؤيتِهِ في الدنيا ؛ إذ الأصل في الجواب المطابقة ، ولأنَّ الجواب وقع هنا بنقيض السؤال ، وقد قيّد المسؤول بوقتِ معيَّن ، فالأصلُ أنَّ نقيضه يتقيَّدُ بِهِ ، ولهذا قالَ أهل المنطقِ : إنَّ نقيض الوقتيّةِ كقولك : زيدٌ مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع بالضرورة وقتَ الكتابة .. يُؤخَذُ فِيهِ ذلك الوقتُ بعينِهِ ، فيُقالُ في نقيض هذه القضية : زيد ليسَ مُتحرِّكَ الأصابع بالإمكان العام وقتَ الكتابة ، وإلى هذا المعنى أشرتُ بقولي : ( وقد يُتأَنَّسُ . . . ) إلى آخره
The answer to their statement that 'lan' is for eternity is rejected; because of His statement regarding the Jews: 'And they will never wish for it, ever' [al-Baqarah 2:95], and they will wish for it in the Fire. Then, the verse is a response to the request of Moses, peace be upon him, and he only asked for an immediate vision in this world, so the response returns to negating his vision in this world, because the original rule in responding is correspondence, and because the response here occurred as the opposite of the question, and the question was restricted to a specific time, so the original rule is that its opposite is also restricted by that time. For this reason the logicians said: the opposite of a temporal proposition, such as 'Zaid is necessarily moving his fingers at the time of writing' – that specific time is taken into account, so in the opposite of this proposition it is said: 'Zaid is not moving his fingers with general possibility at the time of writing.' And to this meaning I alluded with my saying: 'And it may be used as support...' etc.
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage gives the second answer. First, it refutes the claim that 'lan' always indicates eternal negation by citing the verse about the Jews who will never wish for death, but they will wish for it in the afterlife, so 'lan' here is not eternal. Second, it provides a logical analysis: the verse is a response to Moses' request for a vision in this world. Therefore, the negation applies only to that specific time and context. The author supports this with a logical principle about the negation of a temporally restricted proposition: the negation must be restricted to the same time. Thus, 'lan tarani' means 'you will not see Me in this world', not 'never ever'.

Misconception: The word 'lan' in Arabic always indicates eternal negation, so 'lan tarani' means Allah will never be seen in any circumstance.
Correction: Sanusi shows that 'lan' can be temporally restricted, as in the verse about the Jews, and that the response to Moses is restricted to this world, not all eternity.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi proves that 'lan' does not always indicate eternity by citing a Quranic example and applying logical principles of temporal restriction.

Sanusi uses logic to understand the Quran. Do you ever reject tools of knowledge (history, science, language) because they seem 'foreign'? Are you willing to use your intellect to deepen your faith, even if it challenges comfortable assumptions? Commit to studying one verse this week with a deeper linguistic or logical analysis.

Third Objection: Causal Explanation and al-Ash'ari's Position
Third objection, answer, and detailed rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection to the proof of vision, which denies causal explanation (ta'lil), and the rebuttal showing why al-Ash'ari's position on states does not undermine the argument.

Core Idea
Causal explanation in kalam requires affirming 'states' – a concept al-Ash'ari rejected while still allowing rational considerations.

The third objection attacks the very basis of the proof by denying the validity of causal explanation (ta'lil). The objector argues that causal explanation depends on the concept of 'hal' (state), which al-Ash'ari himself rejected. Shahrastani offers a defense, but the rebuttal shows that al-Ash'ari's acceptance of rational considerations does not entail accepting causation. This passage clarifies the theological stakes and the precise boundaries of al-Ash'ari's methodology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection itself
Read the opening sentences where the objector concedes a corrector is needed but denies causal explanation entirely. Note the link between ta'lil and the concept of 'state' (hal).
2
Examine Shahrastani's answer
Shahrastani argues that al-Ash'ari, though rejecting states, affirms rational considerations (wujuh wa i'tibarat 'aqliyya) which allow for universality and particularity.
This seems to bridge the gap.
3
Analyze the refutation of Shahrastani
The rebuttal points out that affirming rational considerations is not the same as affirming causation. The opponent's reliance on rational causes is inconsistent with al-Ash'ari's methodology.
Key distinction.
4
Review the three detailed points
The passage lists three arguments about temporality, substance, and accident that the opponent uses. See how each one assumes rational causation.
These are the 'wa qultum' statements.
5
Connect to the larger proof
Reflect on how this objection and rebuttal fit into the overall argument for the beatific vision. The proof stands because it does not depend on a flawed notion of causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : سلَّمنا توقفَهُ على مُصحح ، لكن لا تُسلّمُ صحة التعليل أصلاً ; فإنَّه عند المتكلمين مبني على ثبوتِ الحالِ والواسطة بينَ الوجود والعدم , ولا نُسلّمُ ثبوت الواسطة , كيف والشيخ الأشعري إمام المذهب لا يقولُ بها , وينفي التعليل العقلي ?! وهذا السؤال لازم للشيخ ولمَنِ التزم مقالته في نفي الحال , ومَنْ قال بها كالقاضي أمكنه الاستدلال بها · وأجابَ الشَّهْرَسْتانيُّ عنه : بأنَّ الشيخ وإنْ لم يقل بالأحوالِ ; فإنَّهُ قائل بالوجوه والاعتبارات العقلية , فقد تُصُوِّرَ العموم والخصوص . ويُرَدُّ عليهِ : بأنَّهُ وإِنْ قالَ بالاعتبارات العقلية فإنَّه لم يقل بالتعليل , ومعتمدُكم فيما تطلبونَ مِنْ أقسام المشترك بين الجوهر والعرض المرئيين .. مبني على التزام أحكام العلل العقليَّةِ , وقلتُم : إنَّ الحدوث لا يكونُ علَّةً ; لأنَّهُ لا يُعقَلُ إِلا بِشِرْكَةٍ مِنَ العدمِ والعدمُ السابق لا يجامعُ الوجودَ , والعلة يجب مقارنتها للمعلول , وصحة الرؤية أمر ثبوتي , والأمرُ العدمي لا يكون علَّةً للأمر الثبوتي ولا جزءاً منها : وقلتُم : إِنَّ الجوهر لا يصحُ أنْ يُرى لجوهريَّتِهِ ولا العرض لعرضيَّتِهِ ; لما يلزم منهُ مِنْ تعليل الحكم المتحدِ النوع بعلتين مختلفتين . وقلتُم : إنَّ الجوهر لا يصحُ أنْ يُقالَ : رُنِي ; لأنَّهُ على صفةٍ خاصة من كون أو لون ; لما يلزمُ في ذلكَ مِنَ التركيب في العلَّة العقلية
Third: We concede its dependence on a corrector, but we do not concede the validity of causal explanation at all; for according to the theologians, it is based on the affirmation of the state and the intermediate between existence and non-existence, and we do not affirm the intermediate. How so, when Shaykh al-Ash'ari, the imam of the school, does not affirm it, and he negates rational causation?! And this question is binding on the Shaykh and on whoever adheres to his statement negating the state, while whoever affirms it like al-Qadi can use it as a proof. And al-Shahrastani answered it: that the Shaykh, although he does not affirm states, he does affirm the rational aspects and considerations, so universality and particularity are conceived. And it is refuted against him: that although he affirms rational considerations, he does not affirm causation, and your reliance in what you seek from the divisions of the common between the seen substance and accident is based on affirming the judgments of rational causes. And you said: that temporality cannot be a cause because it is only conceivable with a share of non-existence, and the prior non-existence does not coexist with existence, and the cause must be concomitant with the effect; and the soundness of vision is a positive matter, and the non-existent matter cannot be a cause for the positive matter nor a part of it. And you said: that the substance cannot be seen for its substantially nor the accident for its accidentally, because that would necessitate explaining a single type of judgment by two different causes. And you said: that the substance cannot be said to be seen because it is upon a specific attribute of being or color, because that would necessitate composition in the rational cause.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection to the proof. The objector concedes that soundness needs a corrector but denies that causal explanation (ta'lil) is valid. Causal explanation in kalam is based on the concept of 'hal' (state) as an intermediate between existence and non-existence, which many theologians reject, including al-Ash'ari. So the objector uses al-Ash'ari's own position against him. Then al-Shahrastani attempts to answer by saying that al-Ash'ari affirms rational considerations (wujuh wa i'tibarat 'aqliyya) which allow for universality and particularity, thus permitting causal explanation. But the rebuttal points out that al-Ash'ari does not affirm causation, and the opponent's arguments about substance and accident relying on rational causes are inconsistent with al-Ash'ari's methodology. The rebuttal then lists several points the opponent has made about temporality not being a cause, and about substance and accident not being seen due to their essence, etc., all of which rely on rational causation.

Misconception: One might think that al-Ash'ari's acceptance of rational considerations implies he accepts causal explanation.
Correction: The rebuttal clarifies that al-Ash'ari does not affirm causation; rational considerations are not the same as causal relations.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The third objection fails because causal explanation in the proof does not require al-Ash'ari's acceptance of states; rather, it relies on rational considerations that al-Ash'ari himself affirms.

The objector tries to force the argument into a rigid causal framework that al-Ash'ari does not accept. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes try to force God into your own logical boxes? What would it look like for you to embrace rational considerations without demanding full causation?

Invalidating Facing: The Mirror and Ray Reflection
Sanusi presents the mirror argument and refutes the ray reflection theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of seeing oneself in a mirror is used to invalidate the condition of facing (muqabala) in vision, and how the ray reflection theory is refuted.

Core Idea
The mirror example proves vision without facing; the ray theory fails because it would hide the mirror itself.

Sanusi begins his refutation of the condition of facing by pointing to an obvious counterexample: a person seeing themselves in a mirror. The opponents try to explain this through ray reflection, but their own theory leads to a contradiction. In this lesson, we will follow the first exchange of this theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: claim, objection, refutation, second objection, second refutation.
The passage is a debate between Sanusi and the opponents.
2
Identify the opponents' theory
The opponents explain mirror vision through ray reflection. Write down their key claim.
They rely on the concept of 'base of rays' and 'roughness'.
3
Understand the refutation
Sanusi shows the theory leads to a contradiction: the mirror itself should be invisible. Explain why in your own words.
This is a classic reductio.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
1 إبطال اشتراط المقابلة ]
ثم لزوم المقابلة يَبطُلُ برؤية الإنسان نفسَه في المرآة والماء. قالوا : لم تتشبَّتِ الأشعة فيهما لعدم التضريس ، فانعكست إلى الرائي يعني : قلنا : فيلزم ألا يرى المرآة والماء لعدم قاعدة الأشعة . قالوا : إنَّما رأى صورةً منطبعةً ، لا نفسَهُ . قلنا : فيلزمُ ألا تبعد ببعدِهِ ، ولا تتحرَّكَ بحركتِهِ .
أنَّهُ ممَّا يُبْطِلُ اشتراط المقابلة في الرؤية : رؤية الإنسانِ نفسَهُ في المرآة والماء ، ومحال أن يكون مقابلاً لنفسه . أجابوا : بأنَّا نشترط أن يكون المرئي مقابلاً أو في حكم المقابل ، والرائي في هذه الصورة في حكم المقابل . قالوا : لأنَّ الشعاعَ لمَّا لاقى جسماً صقيلاً لم يتشبَّتْ بهِ ، فانعكس إلى الناظرِ ، فرأى نفسَهُ . رُدَّ عليهم : بأنه يلزم على ما ذكروه ألا يرى الماء ولا المرآة ; إذْ قاعدة الشعاع التي باعتبارها صح إدراكُ المرئي لم تتحقق ; إذْ لا تشبُّثَ لها فيهما ; لعدم التضريس كما زعموا ، فيلزم على قولهم : أنْ يرى نفسه ولا يرى المرآة ولا الماء ، وهو خلافُ الحس
وأجابَ الحكماء عنِ الإلزام باعتبارِ اشتراط المقابلة بأن قالوا : لا تُسلَّمُ أنَّ المرئي في المرآةِ والمَاءِ لم يقابل الرائي ، وتوهمكم ذلك إنَّما جاءَ مِن اعتقادِكم أنَّ المرئي فيهما نفس الرائي ، ونحن نقولُ : إِنَّ المرئيَّ إِنَّما هو صورة منطبعةٌ فيهما موافقة لصورة الرائي ، لا نفسُ الرائي ، ولا شكَ حينَئِذٍ أَنَّ تلك الصورة مقابلة للرائي . أجابَ أهل الحقِّ : بأنَّهُ لو كان المرئي صورة منطبعةً في جسمي المرآة والماء .. لَلزِمَ ألا تبعد تلك الصورة ببعدِ الرائي منهما ولا تقرُبَ بقربه ; ضرورة قيام تلك الصورة بسطحي المرآة والماء ، فوجب أن تثبت بثباتهما ، فدل ذلكَ على أنَّ المرئي نفَس الرائي ، لا شيء ينطبع في المرآة والماء ، وكذلكَ يلزمُ ألا تتحرك بحركتِهِ ، وهو ظاهرُ . ومما يلزم على اشتراطِ المقابلة : ألا يرى الرائي إلا قدْرَ ذاتِهِ; إذْ لا يقابلُ أكبر منها . قالوا : الشعاع أعانَ على ذلك . قلنا : قد تقدَّمَ جوابه (۱) (1) أي : جوابُ كون الشيء المضيء هو الذي أعان على رؤية الأكبر ، والذي =
1. Invalidation of the condition of facing. Then the necessity of facing is invalidated by a person seeing himself in a mirror and water. They said: The rays did not adhere in them due to lack of roughness, so they reflected back to the seer. Meaning: We said: Then it follows that he does not see the mirror and water due to the absence of the base of rays. They said: He only saw an impressed image, not himself. We said: Then it follows that it does not become distant with his distance, nor move with his movement. That which invalidates the condition of facing in vision: a person seeing himself in a mirror and water, and it is impossible that he is facing himself. They answered: We require that the seen be facing or in the ruling of the facing, and the seer in this form is in the ruling of the facing. They said: Because when the ray met a polished body, it did not adhere to it, so it reflected back to the viewer, and he saw himself. It was refuted upon them: That according to what they mentioned, it follows that he does not see the water nor the mirror, since the base of the ray by which perception of the seen is valid is not realized; since there is no adhesion for it in them, due to lack of roughness as they claimed. So according to their statement, it follows that he sees himself but does not see the mirror nor the water, and that is contrary to sense. The philosophers answered the charge concerning the condition of facing by saying: It is not conceded that the seen in the mirror and water did not face the seer, and your illusion of that only came from your belief that the seen in them is the seer himself. But we say: The seen is only an image impressed in them corresponding to the image of the seer, not the seer himself. There is no doubt then that that image is facing the seer. The people of truth answered: That if the seen were an image impressed in the bodies of the mirror and water, it would necessarily follow that that image does not become distant by the distance of the seer from them nor approach by his approach, necessarily because that image subsists on the surfaces of the mirror and water, so it must be fixed by their fixity. That indicates that the seen is the seer himself, not something impressed in the mirror and water. Similarly, it follows that it does not move with his movement, and that is apparent. And among what follows from the condition of facing: that the seer sees only the amount of himself, since nothing larger than him faces him. They said: The ray helped with that. We said: Its answer has preceded. (1) That is: the answer of the fact that the luminous thing is what helped to see the larger, and which =
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage is a multi-step refutation of the condition of facing (مقابلة) in vision, using the example of a person seeing themselves in a mirror or water. The argument proceeds in several stages of objection and answer.

First, the author states that the necessity of facing is invalidated by a person seeing himself in a mirror or water, because it is impossible for one to face oneself. The opponents (the philosophers or physicists) respond with an explanation: rays do not adhere to polished surfaces (due to lack of roughness), so they reflect back to the seer, allowing him to see himself. The first reply from 'Ahl al-Haqq' (the Ash'ari theologians) is a counter-argument: if the rays do not adhere to the mirror/water, then according to the opponents' own theory, the basis for seeing them (i.e., the rays' adhesion) is absent, so the seer should not see the mirror or water at all. But he does, so their explanation fails.

The opponents then modify their claim: the seer only sees an impressed image (صورة منطبعة) of himself in the mirror/water, not his actual self. The reply: if that were the case, the image would not move or change distance with the seer's movement, because it is fixed on the surfaces of the mirror/water. But in experience, the image does follow the seer's movements, proving that what is seen is the seer himself, not an impressed image.

The philosophers offer a different defense: they deny that the seen object is the seer himself; they say it is an impressed image facing the seer. The people of truth (أهل الحق) respond with a logical proof: if it were an impressed image, it would be fixed to the surfaces and thus would not follow the seer's motion or distance, contrary to observation. Hence, the seen is indeed the seer himself.

Finally, another consequence of requiring facing is that one could only see objects equal to or smaller than one's own size, since larger objects would not face entirely. The opponents claim the ray helps to see larger objects. The author notes that the answer to this has already been given, referencing earlier discussion.

Throughout, the passage demonstrates the Ash'ari method of refuting physical conditions for vision to ultimately argue for the possibility of seeing God without direction or facing.

شعاع
تضريس
قاعدة الشعاع

The mirror example disproves the necessity of facing for vision.

?

The Impressed Image Theory and Its Refutation
Sanusi presents and refutes the impressed image theory, including the philosophers' defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the impressed image theory of vision and how the Ash'ari theologians refute it using the argument of movement and distance.

Core Idea
If vision were an impressed image, it would not follow the seer's movements, but it does.

In the second stage of the debate, the opponents propose that what is seen in the mirror is an impressed image, not the seer himself. The Ash'ari response is both logical and empirical: if the image were fixed on the mirror's surface, it could not move with the seer or change distance. This lesson follows that argument and also addresses the philosophical defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice it shifts to the impressed image theory.
The passage includes multiple voices: opponents, philosophers, and people of truth.
2
Analyze the movement argument
The key refutation is that an impressed image would be fixed. See how the text uses distance and movement to argue.
The argument is empirical: the image follows the seer, so it's not fixed.
3
Examine the philosophers' defense
The philosophers deny the premise. Then see how the people of truth reaffirm the refutation.
4
Consider the size consequence
The passage ends with another implication of facing: size limitation. Note how it's dismissed.
Sanusi refers to a previous answer.

Commentary
This passage is a multi-step refutation of the condition of facing (مقابلة) in vision, using the example of a person seeing themselves in a mirror or water. The argument proceeds in several stages of objection and answer.

First, the author states that the necessity of facing is invalidated by a person seeing himself in a mirror or water, because it is impossible for one to face oneself. The opponents (the philosophers or physicists) respond with an explanation: rays do not adhere to polished surfaces (due to lack of roughness), so they reflect back to the seer, allowing him to see himself. The first reply from 'Ahl al-Haqq' (the Ash'ari theologians) is a counter-argument: if the rays do not adhere to the mirror/water, then according to the opponents' own theory, the basis for seeing them (i.e., the rays' adhesion) is absent, so the seer should not see the mirror or water at all. But he does, so their explanation fails.

The opponents then modify their claim: the seer only sees an impressed image (صورة منطبعة) of himself in the mirror/water, not his actual self. The reply: if that were the case, the image would not move or change distance with the seer's movement, because it is fixed on the surfaces of the mirror/water. But in experience, the image does follow the seer's movements, proving that what is seen is the seer himself, not an impressed image.

The philosophers offer a different defense: they deny that the seen object is the seer himself; they say it is an impressed image facing the seer. The people of truth (أهل الحق) respond with a logical proof: if it were an impressed image, it would be fixed to the surfaces and thus would not follow the seer's motion or distance, contrary to observation. Hence, the seen is indeed the seer himself.

Finally, another consequence of requiring facing is that one could only see objects equal to or smaller than one's own size, since larger objects would not face entirely. The opponents claim the ray helps to see larger objects. The author notes that the answer to this has already been given, referencing earlier discussion.

Throughout, the passage demonstrates the Ash'ari method of refuting physical conditions for vision to ultimately argue for the possibility of seeing God without direction or facing.

Refutation of the Condition of Facing (Muqabala) for Vision
Sanusi refutes the condition of facing (muqabala) for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the condition of facing (muqabala) for vision leads to an absurd consequence and how Sanusi refutes it using the example of seeing a larger body.

Core Idea
The condition of facing (muqabala) is rationally impossible for seeing larger objects.

In this passage, Sanusi takes on a common objection to his doctrine of the beatific vision. The opponents claim that seeing requires facing (muqabala), but Sanusi shows that this condition leads to an impossible scenario. He uses the example of seeing a body larger than oneself to expose the flaw in their reasoning. This argument strengthens the Ash'ari position that Allah can be seen without the condition of facing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The Arabic is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the absurd consequence
Sanusi first states what necessarily follows from the opponent's condition: a seer cannot see any body larger than himself. Understand why that is absurd.
3
Understand the opponent's counter-argument
The opponent tries to salvage their position by invoking ‘illuminated air’ as an intermediary. Note how they claim the air faces the larger body and assists vision.
The air is said to face the larger body.
4
Follow Sanusi's refutation
Sanusi points out that if the air faces the larger body, it must also face the seer to assist, which is impossible. This completes the refutation.
The argument parallels a previous one about the pupil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني : أنَّهُ ممَّا يَرِدُ عليهم في اشتراطهم المقابلة : أنَّه يلزمهم ألا يرى الرائي مِنَ الأجسام ما هو أكبرُ مِنْ جسمِهِ ؛ لأنَّه لا يقابل أكبر منه فإن قالوا : الهواء المضيء الذي بينَهُ وبينَ ذلكَ الجسمِ الأكبرِ مقابل لذلك الجسمِ الأكبرِ ، فأعانَ على رؤيتِهِ . قلنا : فيلزمُ أنَّ ما قابلَ مِنَ الهواء ذلك الجسم العظيم يكونُ مقابلاً للرائي ليعين بعد رؤيته على رؤية ما قابل ، وهو محال (۱) ، وقد تقدَّمَ مثلُ هذا عند ذكرِ جوابهم عمَّا أُلْزِموهُ مِنْ عدمِ رؤيةِ الإنسانِ أكثرَ مِنْ حدقتِهِ (۳).
Meaning: Among the objections against them in their condition of facing (muqabala) is that it necessarily follows that the seer does not see from bodies what is larger than his body, because he does not face what is larger. If they say: The illuminated air between him and that larger body faces that larger body, so it assists in seeing it. We say: Then it necessarily follows that what faces of the air that great body would be facing the seer so that it could assist after its [the air's] vision in seeing what it faces, and that is impossible (1). And similar has preceded in the mention of their answer to what they were forced to admit regarding the impossibility of a person seeing more than his pupil (3).
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the condition of facing (muqabala) for vision. Sanusi argues that if facing were necessary, then one could not see any object larger than one's own body, because the entire larger body cannot be faced by the smaller seer. The opponent might object that the illuminated air between the seer and the larger object faces the larger object and thus assists in seeing it. Sanusi responds that this would require that the portion of air facing the large object also faces the seer, which is impossible because it would imply that the small seer's facing is equal to the large air's facing. He notes that a similar argument was previously mentioned in their response to the objection about not seeing more than the pupil's size.

Key Terms
المقابلة (muqabala)
الهواء المضيء (illuminated air)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Facing (muqabala) is a necessary condition for vision, so Allah cannot be seen because He cannot be faced.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the condition of facing leads to absurdity (e.g., not seeing larger bodies), so it cannot be a necessary condition. Hence, Allah can be seen without facing.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of requiring facing (muqabala) for seeing a large body, according to Sanusi?
A) That the seer can see the large body through the air
B) That the seer cannot see any body larger than himself
C) That the air between them becomes visible
D) That the large body must be broken into parts

Key Takeaway
The condition of facing (muqabala) for vision is rationally invalid because it leads to the impossibility of seeing larger bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever place conditions on your understanding of God that are not based on revelation or sound reason? What assumptions might you be holding that, upon reflection, are logically impossible?
?
This lesson shows how a seemingly reasonable condition (facing) becomes absurd when applied to larger realities. What 'conditions' have you imposed on God's attributes or actions that might actually limit His transcendence? Will you surrender those assumptions today?
?
Sanusi refutes the opponent by pushing their argument to its logical end. When you hold a belief about God, do you follow it to its necessary conclusions, or do you stop halfway out of comfort? What belief about God are you afraid to fully examine?

Definition of Vision (Basar) According to Ahl al-Haqq
Sanusi defines true vision (basar) as a meaning (ma'na) residing in a subject, not requiring physical conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of vision (basar) according to Ahl al-Haqq as a non-physical meaning that subsists in a subject, and the scholarly hesitation about the nature of obstacles in blindness.

Core Idea
True vision is a meaning, not a physical process.

Sanusi has already refuted false conditions for vision. Now he presents the positive definition of basar according to the people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq). This definition clarifies that vision is a meaning (ma'na) residing in a subject, related to visible things, and not dependent on physical eye structures. The passage ends with a notable hesitation about whether blindness is due to a single obstacle or multiple obstacles, showing the nuance in kalam discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Sanusi defines vision as 'a meaning that subsists in a subject' — not as a physical process.
The term 'ma'na' (meaning) is a key technical term in kalam.
2
Understand multiplicity and obstacles
Observe that vision becomes multiple when visible things are multiple, and that what is unseen is due to obstacles in the subject.
This explains how we see many things at once.
3
Reflect on the hesitation about blindness
Consider the question Sanusi poses: is blindness caused by one obstacle or many? He says there is hesitation (taraddud). Think about why scholars might differ.
This shows that some details in kalam are left open.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا تقرر هذا فالبصرُ عندَ أهل الحق : عبارةٌ عن معنى يقوم بمحل ما يتعلَّق بالمرئيَّاتِ ، ويتعدَّدُ في حقنا بتعددِها ، وما لم ير في الموجوداتِ فلموانع قامَتْ بالمحل على حَسَبها ، وهل قام في العمى مانع واحدٌ يُضادُّ جميع الإدراكات ، أو موانع تعدَّدَتْ بعددِ ما فاتَتْ رؤيتُهُ مِنَ الموجوداتِ ؟ فيهِ تردُّد
And when this is established, then vision (al-basar) according to the people of truth (ahl al-haqq) is an expression for a meaning (ma'na) that subsists in a subject (mahall), which relates to visible things, and it becomes multiple in our case by their multiplicity. And what is not seen among existents is due to obstacles (mawani') that have arisen in the subject accordingly. And whether there arises in blindness a single obstacle that opposes all perceptions, or multiple obstacles equal to the number of what is missed of the existents? There is hesitation (taraddud) concerning it.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage provides the definition of vision (basar) according to Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth). After having refuted the false conditions, Sanusi states that true vision is a meaning (ma'na) that subsists in a subject (mahall) and relates to visible things (mari'iyyat). In us (humans), this meaning becomes multiple as the visible things are multiple—meaning we see many things at once. When something is not seen, it is due to obstacles (mawani') that have arisen in the subject. Then he raises a question: in the case of blindness, is there a single obstacle that prevents all perceptions, or are there multiple obstacles equal to the number of things not seen? He notes that there is hesitation (taraddud) on this point, meaning it is not definitively settled among scholars.

Key Terms
البصر (basar/vision)
المعنى (ma'na/meaning)
المحل (mahall/subject)
المرئيات (mari'iyyat/visible things)
الموانع (mawani'/obstacles)
العمى ('ama/blindness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision is a physical process involving the emission or reception of rays from the eye.
Correction: According to Ahl al-Haqq, vision is a non-physical meaning (ma'na) that subsists in a subject, not a material process.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Haqq, what is vision (basar)?
A) The emission of rays from the eye
B) A meaning that subsists in a subject and relates to visible things
C) The physical structure of the eye
D) The act of facing the object

Key Takeaway
Vision, according to Ahl al-Haqq, is a non-physical meaning that relates to visible things, and its absence is due to obstacles, about which there is scholarly hesitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Sanusi mentions hesitation about whether blindness is due to one obstacle or many. Does your own spiritual blindness come from a single deep-seated issue or many small ones? Ask Allah to show you the true barrier, and commit to addressing it starting now.
?
Your sight is not merely physical; it is a meaning from Allah. How often do you thank Him for the ability to see truth beyond the surface? Today, before you sleep, thank Him specifically for the vision of faith, and beg Him to remove any obstacles that dim it.

The Obstacle in Vision and Its Finite Nature
Sanusi explains the obstacle in vision and its finite nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of the obstacle (mane‘) in vision, its multiplication according to unseen objects, the finitude of obstacles, and the Mu'tazila's position.

Core Idea
Obstacles to vision are finite because visible things are finite.

Sanusi builds on a general kalam principle to explain how vision fails. He argues that obstacles multiply with the number of unseen objects, but this does not lead to infinity because the world is finite. He then contrasts the Mu'tazila's denial of an internal obstacle with the exception of Abu al-Hudhayl.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the principle about the receiver being unable to be empty of a thing, its opposite, or its like.
2
Understand the principle
Focus on the opening phrase: the receiver of something cannot be devoid of it, its opposite, or something similar. This is a key kalam axiom applied to the eye.
3
Follow the argument about multiplicity and finitude
The obstacles multiply with the number of existent things not seen. But this does not imply an infinite number of obstacles because existents are finite. The non-sequitur is addressed here.
The text explicitly refutes the worry of infinity.
4
Note the Mu'tazila position and Abu al-Hudhayl's exception
Most Mu'tazila denied the obstacle entirely, attributing blindness to physical defect. Only Abu al-Hudhayl accepted the obstacle but wrongly allowed the eye to be empty of both obstacle and perception.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو مأخوذُ مِنَ القاعدة التي سبق بيانُها ؛ وهي أنَّ القابل للشيء لا يخلو عنه أو عن ضده أو مثلِهِ (۱) ، وتتعدد تلك الموانع بحسب تعدُّدِ تلك الموجوداتِ التي لم تُرَ (۲) ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ تعددِ الإدراكات وتعدُّدِ موانعها قيام ما لا يتناهى عدده بالعين ؛ لأنَّ إدراك البصرِ إِنَّما يتعلَّقُ بالموجود ، والموجودات متناهية ، فإدراكاتها وموانعها متناهية . وأنكرت المعتزلة أنْ يقوم بالعين هذا المعنى الذي سميناه مانعاً ، وحملوا العمى على انتقاض البنية (۳) ، إلا أبا الهذيل العلاف ؛ فإنَّهُ اعترف بالمانع على الوجه الذي نقولهُ ، غيرَ أَنَّهُ يُجوِّزُ عُرُو المحلَّ عنهُ وعن الإدراك ، وذلك باطل
·
قوله : ( وهل قامَ في العمى مانع واحد . . . ( إلى آخره؛ ) يعني أنَّهُ ممَّا اضطربَ فيهِ أئمتُنا : أنَّ العمى هل هو معنى واحدٌ يُضادُّ جميعَ آحادِ الأبصار كما يُضادُّ الموتُ جميعَ آحاد العلوم والإرادات ، أو هو اجتماع موانع كثيرة بعددِ ما فاتَ مِنْ أَحادِ الأبصارِ ؟ والأَوَّلُ رأي القاضي والأستاذ ، والثاني هو التحقيق
And it is taken from the principle that has been explained earlier: that the receiver of a thing is not devoid of it or its opposite or its like (1), and those obstacles multiply according to the multiplicity of those existent things that are not seen (2). And it does not follow from the multiplicity of perceptions and the multiplicity of their obstacles that an infinite number subsists in the eye, because visual perception only relates to the existent, and existents are finite, so their perceptions and obstacles are finite. The Mu'tazila denied that this meaning, which we call an obstacle, subsists in the eye, and they interpreted blindness as a disruption of the structure (3), except Abu al-Hudhayl al-‘Allaf, for he acknowledged the obstacle in the manner we state, except that he allows that the locus be empty of it and of perception, and that is false.
·
His statement: 'And whether in blindness there is a single obstacle… to the end' means that our imams disagreed: Is blindness a single meaning that opposes all individual sights, as death opposes all individual knowledges and wills, or is it a collection of many obstacles equal to the number of individual sights that have been missed? The first is the view of al-Qadi and al-Ustadh, and the second is the verified view.
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of the obstacle (mane‘) that prevents vision. It begins by invoking a general principle: a thing capable of receiving something cannot be empty of that thing, its opposite, or something like it. When applying this to the eye, it argues that obstacles to seeing multiply according to the number of unseen objects. However, this does not imply an infinite number of obstacles in the eye, because visible objects are finite. The Mu'tazila denied that any such obstacle exists in the eye, explaining blindness as a physical defect (disruption of the structure). An exception was Abu al-Hudhayl, who accepted the obstacle but wrongly allowed that the eye could be empty of both obstacle and perception. Then the passage quotes a question from the matn: 'Is blindness a single obstacle…?' The commentator explains that there is disagreement among authoritative imams: some (al-Qadi and al-Ustadh) hold that blindness is a single meaning that opposes all individual acts of seeing (like death opposes all knowledge and will), while the verified view is that it is a collection of multiple obstacles, one for each individual sight that is lost.

مانع (mane‘)
عمى ('ama)
إدراك (idrak)
بصر (basar)
موجود (mawjud)
أبو الهذيل العلاف (Abu al-Hudhayl al-‘Allaf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the multiplicity of obstacles would require an infinite number of things to exist in the eye, making the concept of obstacles impossible.
Correction: The text argues that since visible things are finite, the obstacles are finite as well. The worry of infinity is unfounded because the perceptions and obstacles correspond to finite existents.

Checkpoint
Why do obstacles to vision not multiply to infinity?
A) Because obstacles are immaterial.
B) Because visible existents are finite.
C) Because the eye can only hold so many obstacles.
D) Because obstacles are not real.

Key Takeaway
Obstacles to vision exist as opposites or likes of perception, but their number is finite because the world is finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your eye has obstacles that prevent it from seeing certain things, but these obstacles are finite. What about your heart? What spiritual obstacles—like arrogance, heedlessness, or attachment to sin—have you allowed to accumulate, knowing they block your perception of Allah's signs? Will you remove one tonight by seeking forgiveness and reflecting on a verse?
?
The Mu'tazila blamed structural defects for blindness, avoiding internal accountability. When you fail to see the truth in a situation, do you blame external circumstances (like lack of evidence or other people) rather than examining the internal obstacle of your own ego or desires? What would it cost you to honestly confront that obstacle tonight?
?
Abu al-Hudhayl acknowledged the obstacle but claimed the eye could be empty of both obstacle and perception—a false comfort. Are you living in a similar illusion, thinking you can be neutral or empty of faith and its opposite? What is the one belief or habit you are avoiding committing to, and what will you decide about it before you sleep tonight?

The Dispute on Blindness: Single or Multiple Obstacles?
Sanusi presents the internal Ash'ari dispute on the nature of blindness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement among Ash'ari imams about whether blindness is a single obstacle or a collection of many obstacles, and which view is considered verified.

Core Idea
Blindness is the sum of obstacles for each lost sight, not one single obstacle.

After establishing the nature of obstacles, Sanusi turns to a specific internal Ash'ari dispute: Is blindness one single meaning that opposes all individual acts of seeing, or is it a collection of multiple obstacles, one for each sight that is missing? He attributes the first view to al-Qadi and al-Ustadh and declares the second as the verified position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. This is a short but dense dispute between two positions.

Identify the two views

The first view is attributed to al-Qadi and al-Ustadh.

Think of how death is a single meaning that removes all knowledge and will. Similarly, the first view sees blindness as a single obstacle removing all vision. The second view sees it as many obstacles stacking.

The First Aspect of Looseness in the Term 'Miracle'
Sanusi explains the first aspect of looseness in the term 'mu'jizah' via two cases and an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'mu'jizah' (miracle) is used metaphorically to mean 'absence of power' rather than literal incapacity, and how this parallels the loose usage of 'ignorance'.

Core Idea
A 'miracle' does not literally incapacitate opponents; it signals the absence of power to imitate.

In this passage, Sanusi examines the first of two reasons why the term 'miracle' is not used literally. He argues that the word 'incapacity' cannot be truly applied to opponents because either the miracle is beyond human power (so no prior capacity exists) or it is within human power but opposition is absent. The term is thus a concession, meaning 'absence of power', just as 'ignorance' is used loosely for 'absence of knowledge'. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: a statement, then two conditional cases, then the conclusion.
2
Understand the two cases
Identify the two scenarios Sanusi presents: miracle beyond human power, and miracle within human power. How does each case invalidate literal 'incapacity'?
3
Grasp the parallel with 'ignorance'
See how Sanusi draws an analogy with 'ignorance' used loosely for 'absence of knowledge'. Why does this analogy strengthen his point?
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this subtle point matters for Islamic theology: miracles are signs of prophethood, not proofs of opponent's incapacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما : أن اللفظ يشعر بحقيقة العجز، ولا يصح ثبوت العجز؛ لأنه إن كانت الآية ليست من جنس مقدور البشر فلا يصح لفظ العجز حقيقة عما ليس بمقدور، وإن كانت من جنس مقدور البشر فالعجز عندنا يقارن المعجوز عنه، والمعارضة منتفية، فلا يصح ثبوت عجز متعلق بها، فقد تسومح وأطلق العجز على انتفاء القدرة، كما يتسامح في الجهل ويطلق على انتفاء العلم
The first: that the word suggests the reality of incapacity, but the establishment of incapacity is not valid; because if the sign is not of the kind within human capability, then the word 'incapacity' is not truly applicable to what is not within capability, and if it is of the kind within human capability, then incapacity with us accompanies the thing incapacitated, and opposition is absent, so the establishment of an incapacity related to it is not valid. Thus tolerance was exercised and 'incapacity' was applied to the absence of power, just as tolerance is exercised in ignorance and it is applied to the absence of knowledge.
Source pages p. 545

Commentary
This passage details the first of the two respects in which the term 'muʿjizah' is applied loosely. The argument is: the word 'incapacity' ('ajz) suggests true incapacity. But if the miraculous sign is not within human power at all (like bringing a dead person back to life), then it is not literally true to say opponents are 'incapacitated' from matching it, because they never had the capacity to begin with. If the sign is within human power in principle (like the Prophet's recitation of the Qur'an), then 'incapacity' would mean they were prevented from doing what they could otherwise do. But in fact, no one even attempts opposition because they know it is impossible. So the term 'muʿjizah' is used loosely, meaning 'absence of power' rather than literal 'incapacity', just as 'ignorance' (jahl) is used loosely to mean 'absence of knowledge'.

Key Terms
العجز
انتفاء القدرة
التسامح
الجهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a miracle is called so because it literally incapacitates the opponents from imitating it.
Correction: Sanusi shows that 'incapacity' is used metaphorically: either the miracle is beyond human power (so no prior capacity) or it is within human power but opposition is absent. The term actually means 'absence of power', not literal incapacitation.

Checkpoint
According to the first aspect, why is 'incapacity' not literally applicable to a miracle that is beyond human power?
A) Because the opponents have the capacity to imitate it
B) Because incapacity can only exist where there is prior capacity
C) Because the miracle is an act of Allah
D) Because the opponents do not attempt opposition

Key Takeaway
The term 'miracle' (muʿjizah) is a loose term meaning absence of power to imitate, not literal incapacitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that the term 'miracle' is used loosely and does not mean literal incapacitation. Think of a time you used the word 'miracle' casually—perhaps for a recovery or a coincidence. How does Sanusi's analysis expose the lack of precision in your own speech? What would it cost you to be more careful with such a weighty term?
?
Sanusi points out that 'incapacity' can be a loose term, just as 'ignorance' is often used to mean simply 'absence of knowledge'. In your life, where do you assume you 'know' something about God or religion when in fact you have only a loose, metaphorical grasp? Which doctrine do you hold without recognizing its terminological nuance, and how might that lead to error?
?
The passage reveals that a key term in your faith (miracle) does not mean what its root suggests. Are you content with surface-level understanding of Islamic terms, or do you feel the weight of responsibility to dig deeper? If you neglect this, how might you deceive yourself about your own knowledge?

Using 'Miracle' Metaphorically
Sanusi explains the metaphorical usage of 'mu'jizah'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'mu'jizah' is considered metaphorical in Islamic theology, specifically because it attributes the effect to the created event while the true agent is Allah.

Core Idea
A miracle is called 'mu'jizah' metaphorically; only Allah truly incapacitates.

This lesson unpacks the second way the term 'miracle' is used loosely. Sanusi shows that the Arabic word itself implies an agent performing the action of incapacitation, but that agent can only be Allah. Therefore, the term is metaphorical when applied to the miraculous event.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source text once, then the English translation. Notice the key terms: 'فاعل العجز' (agent of incapacity) and 'مجاز' (metaphor).
The passage is very concise; take your time to let the words settle.
2
Understand the linguistic form
The word 'mu'jizah' is on the pattern of 'ism fāʿil' (active participle), which normally indicates the doer of an action. Here, the action is 'incapacitation' (iʿjāz).
This linguistic point is crucial for the argument.
3
Identify the true agent
Sanusi states that Allah is the true agent of incapacity (فاعل العجز). The miracle event itself does not perform incapacitation; it is merely the occasion where Allah causes incapacity in opponents.
This is the theological foundation.
4
Recognize the metaphor
Because the miracle is named after the effect (incapacitation) while the true cause is Allah, the term 'mu'jizah' is used metaphorically (majāz). Compare to calling a sword a 'killer' when the real killer is the person.
This completes the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في التوسع : أن لفظ ( المعجزة ) يشعر بفاعل العجز ، والله تعالى هو فاعل العجز ، فسمي ما فعل العجز عنده معجزاً مجازاً
The second aspect of looseness: that the word 'al-muʿjizah' suggests the agent of incapacity, while Allah—exalted—is the agent of incapacity, so that thing at which incapacity occurs is called muʿjizah metaphorically.
Source pages p. 546

Commentary
This passage gives the second respect in which the term 'muʿjizah' is used loosely. The word 'muʿjizah' is a noun of agent (ism fāʿil), which normally indicates the doer of the action. The action is 'incapacitation' (iʿjāz). But the true agent of incapacitation is Allah alone, not the miracle itself. So calling the miracle 'muʿjizah' is metaphorical (majāz): it is named after the effect (the incapacitation) because it is the occasion for Allah's act. This is similar to calling a sword 'killer' metaphorically, when the actual killer is the person wielding it.

Key Terms
فاعل العجز
المجاز
المعجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the miracle itself actively incapacitates opponents.
Correction: The agent of incapacitation is Allah alone; the miracle is merely the occasion for Allah's act.

Checkpoint
Why is calling the miracle 'muʿjizah' considered metaphorical according to the second aspect?
A) Because the miracle does not actually cause incapacity
B) Because the word suggests an agent, but Allah is the true agent
C) Because the miracle is not a sign of prophethood
D) Because the miracle is a human action

Key Takeaway
The term 'mu'jizah' is used metaphorically because the true agent of incapacitation is Allah, not the miracle itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the word 'miracle', do you instinctively think of the event itself as the cause of people's disbelief, or do you immediately remember that Allah alone incapacitates? What practical shift would it take to make the latter your default thought?
?
The term 'mu'jizah' is a metaphor — it points to Allah's action, not the action of the miracle. Are there other common Islamic terms you use that might similarly misdirect your heart's focus away from Allah? What is one such term, and how can you correct that tendency starting today?
?
If Allah alone is the agent of incapacity, then even your inability to obey Allah perfectly is created by Him. Does that make you feel despair or hope? How should it affect the way you seek His help in your daily struggle against sin?

Explaining 'Al-Aqwiya' al-'Arida' and 'Makhraqa'
Sanusi explains the terms 'al-aqwiya' al-'arida' and 'makhraqa' used by critics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of the terms 'al-aqwiya' al-'arida' and 'makhraqa' as used by critics of Islamic theology, and see an example of how these terms were applied in a historical critique.

Core Idea
The critics' terms 'al-aqwiya al-arida' and 'makhraqa' reveal their mockery disguised as argument.

In this passage, Sanusi explains two terms that were used by critics to dismiss theological arguments: 'al-aqwiya' al-'arida' (superficial eloquence) and 'makhraqa' (foolish mockery). He illustrates the latter with a famous example from the Quran's Surah al-Fil, where a critic ridiculed the miracle of the elephant army. Understanding these terms helps us see the rhetorical strategies of early opponents of Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow of ideas.
2
Identify the two key terms
Locate 'al-aqwiya' al-'arida' and 'makhraqa' in the text and note their definitions as given by Sanusi.
Notice that each term is introduced with 'قوله' (his saying).
3
Analyze the illustrative example
Read the example of the critic's parody of Surah al-Fil. Ask yourself: how does this statement demonstrate 'makhraqa' (foolish mockery)?
The critic tries to trivialize the divine miracle by focusing on absurd details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (الأقوياء العارضة) هي القوة والقدرة على الكلام. قوله: (وأتى بمخرقة) أي: مضحكة وحمق؛ لدلالتها على خرق، وهذا كقوله عندما سمع بسورة الفيل: الفيل ما الفيل، وما أدراك ما الفيل، له ذنب وثيل، وخرطوم طويل، وإن ذلك في خلق ربنا لقليل، والواو في قوله: (وثيل) للعطف
His saying: 'al-aqwiya' al-'arida' means strength and ability in speech. His saying: 'and he came with a makhraqa' meaning a joke and foolishness, because it indicates his foolishness. And this is like his saying when he heard Surah al-Fil: 'The elephant, what is the elephant? And what will make you know what the elephant is? It has a tail and a trunk, and a long proboscis, and that in the creation of our Lord is little.' And the waw in his saying 'wathil' is for conjunction.
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage provides lexical explanations. 'Al-aqwiya' al-'arida' refers to strength and ability in speech, likely describing someone claiming eloquence. 'Makhraqa' means a joke or foolish act, indicating the person's foolishness. The author gives an example of someone mocking the Quran by parodying Surah al-Fil, saying ridiculous things about the elephant, and then stating that such things are insignificant in God's creation. The 'waw' in 'wathil' is a conjunction.

Key Terms
أقوياء عارضة
مخرقة
خرق
سورة الفيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Makhraqa merely refers to clever wordplay or rhetorical trick.
Correction: It actually implies foolishness and mockery, as Sanusi explicitly says it means 'joke and foolishness' and is derived from 'kharaq' (foolishness).

Checkpoint
What does 'makhraqa' mean in this context?
A) A miracle.
B) A joke or foolishness.
C) A poetic verse.
D) A type of Quranic recitation.

Key Takeaway
The terms 'al-aqwiya al-arida' and 'makhraqa' were used by critics to label reasoned arguments as mere eloquence or foolish mockery.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The critics' 'makhraqa' was a joke masking arrogance. Have you ever laughed at something from Allah's revelation to feel superior? How will you guard your heart against such pride today?

Ibn al-Tilmisānī’s Definitions of Four Key Rhetorical Terms
Ibn al-Tilmisānī defines four rhetorical terms and their conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinct definitions of faṣāḥa, jazāla, naẓm, and balāgha as given by Ibn al-Tilmisānī in the context of Quranic rhetoric.

Core Idea
Balāgha is the sum of fluent word choice and eloquent arrangement.

Ibn al-Tilmisānī provides concise definitions for four central terms of Arabic rhetoric. These definitions form the foundation for understanding how the Quran achieves its unmatched eloquence. In this lesson, we examine his classification and the relationships between the terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Start by reading the passage that contains all four definitions. Notice how each term builds on the previous one.
The passage is from Ibn al-Tilmisānī's commentary.
2
Identify the condition for each term
For faṣāḥa, the condition is clarifying the purpose; for jazāla, it is few letters and harmonious articulation; for naẓm, it is the arrangement; for balāgha, it is the combination of jazāla and naẓm.
3
Understand the relationship between terms
Note that balāgha is not a separate quality but the combination of jazāla and naẓm. This shows a layered approach to rhetoric.
This is the key insight.
4
Ask yourself: How do these apply to the Quran?
Think about how each term might be used to describe the unique qualities of Quranic language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[على بيان معنى الفصاحة، والجزالة، والنظم، والبلاغة] قالَ ابنُ التِّلِمْساني: ( الفصاحةُ: عبارة عن دلالة اللفظ على المعنى بشرطِ إيضاح الغرض منه، والجزالة: عبارة عن دلالته على معناه بشرطِ قلَّةِ حروفِهِ وتناسب مخارجها، والنظم عبارة عن الأسلوب الخاص في ترتيب الأقوالِ بعضها مع بعض، ثم الحسْنُ فيهِ بحسَبِ تناسب الكلماتِ في مواردِها، وذلك أنواع وأصناف، ومجموع الجزالة والنظم هو البلاغة ) قلتُ: المشهور بين البيانيين: أنَّ الفصاحةَ تُوصَفُ بها الكلمة والكلام والمتكلم: فمعناها في الكلمةِ: أنْ تكونَ خالصةً مِنْ تنافرِ الحروفِ؛ احترازاً من نحو قوله: [من الطويل] غَدَائِرُهُ مُسْتَشْزَرَاتِ إِلَى الْعُلَا
[On explaining the meaning of eloquence (al-faṣāḥa), fluency (al-jazāla), composition (al-naẓm), and rhetoric (al-balāgha)] Ibn al-Tilmisānī said: 'Eloquence (al-faṣāḥa) is the expression of the word indicating the meaning with the condition of clarifying the intended purpose from it; fluency (al-jazāla) is its indication of its meaning with the condition of fewness of its letters and the harmony of their points of articulation; composition (al-naẓm) is the specific style in arranging statements with each other, and then the beauty in it is according to the proportion of words in their contexts; and there are types and kinds of that; and the sum of fluency and composition is rhetoric (al-balāgha).' I said: The well-known among the rhetoricians is that eloquence is used to describe the word, the speech, and the speaker: its meaning in the word is that it be free from discordance of letters; as a precaution against such as his saying [al-ṭawīl]: 'Ghada'iruhu mustashzarāti ilā al-'ulā' (His locks are raised high...)
Source pages p. 588

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of four key rhetorical terms used in discussing the Quran's inimitability. Ibn al-Tilmisānī first gives his definitions: faṣāḥa is the word's clear indication of meaning that also clarifies the speaker's intent; jazāla is the word's indication with few letters and harmonious articulation; naẓm is the specific arrangement of words in a discourse, with beauty arising from the proportional placement of words; and balāgha is the combination of jazāla and naẓm. After citing this, the author (al-Sanūsī or the commentator) adds his own clarification: the standard view among rhetoricians (bayāniyyūn) is that faṣāḥa applies to the word, the sentence (kalām), and the speaker. For the word specifically, faṣāḥa means it is free from discordant letters (tanāfur al-ḥurūf), and he gives an example from the famous line of Imru' al-Qais where the word 'mustashzarāt' has discordant letters. This comment refines the definition by grounding it in the established technical usage of the rhetorical tradition.

Key Terms
فصاحة
جزالة
نظم
بلاغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That faṣāḥa and balāgha mean the same thing.
Correction: Ibn al-Tilmisānī defines faṣāḥa as clarity of word, while balāgha is the combination of jazāla and naẓm.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Tilmisānī, what is the definition of balāgha?
A) It is the word indicating meaning with few letters.
B) It is the specific style of arranging statements.
C) It is the combination of jazāla and naẓm.
D) It is freedom from discordance of letters.

Key Takeaway
Balāgha is the combination of fluent vocabulary (jazāla) and effective arrangement (naẓm).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you explain Islamic concepts, do you ensure your words sincerely convey the intended meaning, or do you assume understanding? What step will you take today to make your speech more purposeful?
?
The passage shows that true eloquence is both good vocabulary and good arrangement. In your conversations about faith, which do you neglect more often? What will you do to balance them?
?
Ibn al-Tilmisānī defines balāgha as a combination. Think of a recent discussion about deen: was your speech eloquent by this standard? If not, what specific change will you make next time?

The author clarifies the definition of faṣāḥa at the word level.
