Conditions for Eloquence: Avoidances in Fasāḥa
The author lists three avoidances in fasāḥa and defines istishzār.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three conditions that must be avoided for speech to be considered eloquent (fasīḥ), and how sound taste judges eloquence.

Core Idea
Eloquent speech avoids strangeness, weak analogy, and unpleasant sound.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary on the Umm al-Barahin outlines the practical criteria for eloquence. After stating that sound taste is the final arbiter, the author lists three specific defects to avoid. We'll examine each defect with examples from classical Arabic usage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation to get the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the examples given.
2
Identify the three avoidances
Locate the three conditions (strangeness, weak analogy, unpleasant sound) and note the examples provided.
3
Understand the role of sound taste
Reflect on why the author begins by stating that sound taste is the arbiter.
This underscores that eloquence is not purely mechanical.
4
Examine the explanation of istishzār
Notice how the author clarifies the reading of 'mustashzirāt' based on the meaning of istishzār.
This shows attention to variant readings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمحكّم في ذلك الذوق السليم . ومِنَ الغرابة ؛ احترازاً مِنْ قولِهِ : ما لكم تكأكَأْتُم عليَّ تكأْكُوكُم على ذي جِنَّةٍ ؟! افرنقعوا عنِّي . ومِنْ ضَعفِ القياس ؛ احترازاً مِنْ قولِهِ : الْحَمْدُ اللهِ الْعَلِيُّ الْأَجْلَل . إذْ قياسُهُ : الأجل ( بالإدغام ) . وبعضُهم يزيدُ : وأن تكون غير مكروهة في السمع ؛ احترازاً مِنْ نحو قوله : كَرِيمُ الْجِرِشَّى شَرِيفُ النَّسَبْ . والاستشزار : الارتفاع والرفع ؛ فعلى الأولى يكون قوله : ( مستشزرات ) بكسر الزاي ، وعلى الثاني بفتحها .
The arbiter in that is sound taste. And [the avoidance] of strangeness, guarding against his saying: 'Why have you crowded upon me, crowding like someone possessed? Clear away from me.' And [the avoidance] of weak analogy, guarding against his saying: 'Praise be to God, the Most High, the Most Glorious.' For its analogy is 'al-ajall' (with assimilation). And some add: that it be not disliked in hearing, guarding against something like his saying: 'Generous of soul, noble of lineage.' And istishzār: raising and lifting; so on the first [interpretation] his word 'mustashzirāt' is with kasr of the zāy, and on the second with fatḥa.
Source pages p. 589

Commentary
This passage defines the avoidances required for eloquent speech (fasāḥa). It begins by stating that sound taste is the arbiter in judging eloquence. Then it lists three avoidances: first, avoidance of strangeness (gharāba), exemplified by an odd utterance that people would find incomprehensible or bizarre. Second, avoidance of weak analogy (ḍaʿf al-qiyās), illustrated by a misconstructed phrase that results from applying a grammatical analogy incorrectly. Third, some authorities add avoidance of unpleasantness to the ear (karāha fī al-samʿ), giving an example of a word that sounds harsh. Finally, it explains the word 'istishzār' (raising), clarifying that it can be read with kasr or fatḥa depending on interpretation.

Key Terms
فصاحة
غرابة
قياس
كراهة السمع
استشزار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think eloquence only requires rare vocabulary.
Correction: This passage shows that avoiding strangeness is a key condition, so rare words can be a defect.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
To be eloquent, speech must avoid strangeness, weak analogy, and unpleasant sound, as judged by sound taste.

The Three Defects that Spoil Eloquence in Speech
Sanusi defines fasāḥa in speech by listing three defects to avoid, each with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three defects that speech must avoid to be considered eloquent (fasīḥ) according to Sanusi.

Core Idea
Eloquence in speech requires more than just eloquent words—composition and clarity matter.

Sanusi has already defined eloquence (faṣāḥa) in individual words. Now he turns to speech as a whole, explaining that even if every word is eloquent, the overall utterance can be flawed if it suffers from certain defects. This passage identifies three such defects, each illustrated with a vivid example to help you recognize and avoid them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal English translation. Let the structure—each condition introduced by a phrase—sink in before analyzing.
Notice that each defect is introduced by a term (tanāfur, ḍaʿf al-taʾlīf, taʿqīd maʿnawī) followed by an example.
2
Identify the three defects
List the three defects in order. For each, find the example given and try to understand why it illustrates the defect. The commentary will help explain the details.
You may need to think about what makes the example 'discordant' or 'weak' in composition.
3
Reflect on the overall definition
Combine the three conditions into a single definition of fasāḥa in speech. Consider why all three are necessary and what happens if one is missing.
This connects the lesson to the broader aim of using language with precision and beauty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما معناها في الكلام : فأن تكون كلماته فصيحةً على ما سبق ، لا تنافر بينها ؛ احترازاً مِنْ نحو قوله : وَقَبْرُ حَرْبِ بِمَكَانِ قَفْرٍ وَلَيْسَ قُرْبَ قَبْرِ حَرْبٍ قَبْرُ . سالماً مِنْ ضَعفِ التأليفِ ؛ احترازاً مِنْ نحو : ضربَ غلامُهُ زيداً . ومِنَ التعقيد المعنوي ؛ احترازاً مِنْ نحو قوله : وَمَا مِثْلُهُ فِي النَّاسِ إِلا مُمَلَكاً أَبُو أُمِّهِ حَيٌّ أَبُوهُ يُقَارِبُهُ .
And as for its meaning in speech: it is that its words be eloquent according to what preceded, with no discord between them, guarding against something like his saying: 'And the grave of Harb is in a desolate place, and there is not near the grave of Harb a grave.' Free from weak composition, guarding against something like: 'His slave struck Zayd.' And from semantic complexity, guarding against something like his saying: 'And there is no one like him among people except a king whose maternal grandfather is alive, his father resembles him.'
Source pages p. 590

Commentary
This passage defines fasāḥa in speech (fasāḥat al-kalām). It states that for speech to be eloquent, its individual words must be eloquent (as defined earlier) and there must be no discord (tanāfur) among them; an example of discord is given in a line of verse where the repeated sounds are harsh. Second, the speech must be free from weak composition (ḍaʿf al-taʾlīf), illustrated by a sentence where the pronoun reference is confusing. Third, it must be free from semantic complexity (taʿqīd maʿnawī), where the meaning is hard to grasp due to ambiguous references, as shown in a poetic line. Each condition is illustrated with an example of what to avoid.

Key Terms
فصاحة
تنافر
ضعف التأليف
تعقيد معنوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fasāḥa only concerns individual words being eloquent.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that even if each word is eloquent, the speech overall must also avoid discord, weak composition, and semantic complexity to be considered fasīḥ.

Checkpoint

D) Repetition, alliteration, and assonance

Key Takeaway
The eloquence of speech lies not only in eloquent words but also in the harmony of composition and clarity of meaning, avoiding discord, weak structure, and semantic complexity.

Definition of Fasāḥa in the Speaker: A Faculty
The author defines fasāḥa in the speaker as a faculty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that fasāḥa (eloquence) in the speaker is defined as a faculty (malaka) that enables eloquent expression of intended meaning.

Core Idea
Fasāḥa in the speaker is a skill, not just a feature of speech.

This lesson presents the author's definition of fasāḥa as it pertains to the speaker. While we often think of eloquence as a quality of words, the author emphasizes that it is first a faculty in the person—an acquired ability to produce eloquent speech. Understanding this distinction is key to the study of balāgha.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation to grasp the core statement.
The definition is concise, so pay attention to each word.
2
Identify the key term 'malaka'
Note that the author uses 'malaka' (faculty) to describe the speaker's ability. This indicates eloquence is an acquired skill.
Malaka implies a stable trait that enables action.
3
Understand the scope
Realize that this faculty applies to both single words (kalima) and connected speech (kalam).
This shows fasāḥa covers all levels of expression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما معناها في المُتكلّم : فهو أن تكونَ لَهُ مَلَكَةٌ يَقتدِرُ بها على التعبير بالفصيح عما يقصدهُ مِنْ كلمة أو كلام .
And as for its meaning in the speaker: it is that he have a faculty by which he is able to express with eloquence what he intends, whether a word or speech.
Source pages p. 590

Commentary
This passage defines fasāḥa in the speaker (fasāḥat al-mutakallim). It states that it is a faculty (malaka) that enables the speaker to express his intended meaning eloquently, whether in a single word or in connected speech. This emphasizes that eloquence is an acquired skill, not a mere accident or innate trait.

Key Terms
فصاحة
ملكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think fasāḥa is only a quality of speech itself, not of the speaker.
Correction: The definition clarifies that fasāḥa in the speaker is a faculty—an ability to produce eloquent speech, distinct from the speech's own eloquence.

Checkpoint
How is fasāḥa in the speaker defined?
A) As the speech being eloquent
B) As a faculty that enables eloquent expression
C) As conformity to the situation
D) As avoidance of weak analogy

Key Takeaway
Fasāḥa in the speaker is defined as a faculty (malaka) that enables eloquent expression of intended meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about your faith, do you sense that eloquence flows from a deep inner faculty you have cultivated, or do you rely on mere words learned by rote?
?
What specific steps have you taken this week to strengthen your malaka of eloquent expression, especially when conveying meanings of the Quran and Sunnah?
?
If your speech were to be assessed tomorrow by a master of fasāḥa, what would your faculty reveal about the state of your heart and mind?

Understanding Balāgha: Eloquence in Context
Sanusi defines balāgha and illustrates it with three situational examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that balāgha (rhetorical eloquence) requires both fasāḥa (linguistic fluency) and conformity to the situation (muqtaḍā al-ḥāl).

Core Idea
Balāgha is eloquence that fits the context, not just fancy words.

This passage from Sanusi's Sharḥ al-Sanūsiyya introduces the concept of balāgha, a key term in Arabic rhetoric. While fasāḥa ensures the speech is clear and flowing, balāgha adds the crucial requirement that the speech must suit the situation and the audience. The author illustrates this with three communication scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Understand that balāgha applies to speech and speaker, and its meaning in speech is being eloquent and conforming to the situation.
Notice the phrase 'jarīyan 'alā mā yaqtaḍīhi al-ḥāl'
2
Examine the three examples
Each example describes a different audience: a denier, an uninformed person, and a doubtful person. Note the corresponding level of emphasis.
Emphasis increases with the strength of denial or doubt.
3
Reflect on the key insight
Balāgha is not just about beautiful language but about effective communication tailored to context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما البلاغة : فلا يُوصَفُ بها إلا الكلام والمتكلم . أما معناها في الكلام : فهو أن يكون فصيحاً، جارياً على ما يقتضيه الحالُ ؛ أي : السبب الذي ورد الكلام لأجله ؛ كالكلام الوارد لدفع إنكار مُنكِرٍ ؛ فإنَّهُ يناسبُهُ أنْ يُؤكَّد بحسب مراتب الإنكار . والوارد لإفادة خالي الذهنِ مِنَ الحُكْم يناسبُهُ أَنْ يُلقى إليه الكلام غير مُؤكَّد . والوارد لإفادة مَنْ هو مُشعر بالحكمِ شَاكٌ فِيهِ يُستحسَنُ أَنْ يُؤكَّدَ .
And as for balāgha: it is not described except for speech and the speaker. As for its meaning in speech: it is that it be eloquent, running according to what the situation requires; that is, the reason for which the speech was uttered. For example, speech that comes to repel the denial of a denier: it is appropriate that it be emphasized according to the degrees of denial. And speech that comes to inform one whose mind is free of the judgment: it is appropriate that the speech be delivered to him unemphasized. And speech that comes to inform one who has a sense of the judgment but is doubtful about it: it is recommended that it be emphasized.
Source pages p. 590

Commentary
This passage introduces balāgha (rhetoric) and restricts its description to speech and the speaker. It then defines balāgha in speech: it must be eloquent (fasīḥ) and also conform to the situation (muqtaḍā al-ḥāl), meaning the reason for which the speech is produced. Three examples illustrate degrees of emphasis: for repelling a denier, emphasis is needed according to the strength of the denial; for informing someone with no prior knowledge, no emphasis is appropriate; for someone who is aware but doubtful, moderate emphasis is recommended. Thus balāgha adds contextual appropriateness to eloquence.

Key Terms
بلاغة
مقتضى الحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think balāgha is simply eloquent or ornate speech, regardless of context.
Correction: Balāgha specifically requires that speech conform to the situation (muqtaḍā al-ḥāl) and the needs of the audience, as shown by the three examples.

Checkpoint
What additional requirement does balāgha have beyond fasāḥa?
A) It requires rare vocabulary
B) It requires the speech to be in Arabic
C) It requires conformity to the situation
D) It requires the speaker to be a poet

Key Takeaway
Balāgha is eloquence that conforms to the situation, requiring different degrees of emphasis for different audiences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you share Islamic knowledge, do you first assess the other person's level of belief or doubt, or do you speak the same way to everyone? How might this lesson change your approach starting today?
?
Think of a recent conversation where you tried to correct someone's misunderstanding. Did you match the force of your words to the strength of their denial? If not, what will you do differently next time?
?
Are you more focused on sounding eloquent or on truly reaching the heart of your listener? Which one does this lesson demand, and what is one concrete step you will take this week to align with it?

Balagha: The Faculty of Eloquence and Its Distinction from Fasaha
Sanusi defines balagha as a faculty and distinguishes it from fasaha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's definition of balagha as a faculty, its relationship to fasaha, and the two extremes of eloquence.

Core Idea
Eloquence is a higher, more specific quality than fluency.

Sanusi begins by defining balagha (eloquence) as a faculty that enables a speaker to produce eloquent speech. He then distinguishes it from fasaha (fluency), showing that while every eloquent speaker is fluent, the reverse is not true. This passage also introduces the two extremes of eloquence, from the miraculous to the animal-like.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Let the definitions sink in.
2
Identify the definition
Notice that balagha is defined as a faculty (malaka) enabling eloquent expression. This is the core.
3
Understand the relationship
Sanusi says balagha is more specific than fasaha. Every eloquent person is fluent, but not vice versa. Grasp this hierarchy.
4
Explore the two extremes
Find the highest (i'jaz) and lowest (near animal sounds) extremes. Note that between them are countless degrees.
The highest is judged by taste (dhawq).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما معناها في المُتكلّم : فهي مَلَكَةٌ يَقتدِرُ بها على التعبير بكلام بليغ . فعُلِمَ مِنْ هذا : أنَّ البلاغة أخصُّ مِنَ الفصاحة ، فكلُّ بليغ فصيح ، وليس كل فصيح بليغاً ، وقد تُطلَقُ إحداهما على الأخرى توسعاً . وللبلاغة طرفان : أعلى ؛ وهو الإعجاز ، والمحكّم فيه الذوق ، وأدنى ؛ وهو ما إذا بُدِّلَ عن حالِهِ التحق عندَ البلغاء بأصواتِ الحيوانات ، وبينهما مراتب لا تكاد تنحصر
As for its meaning in relation to the speaker: it is a faculty by which one is able to express oneself with eloquent speech. So it is known from this that balagha is more specific than fasaha: every eloquent (baligh) speaker is fluent (fasih), but not every fluent speaker is eloquent. Sometimes one is used in place of the other by extension. Balagha has two extremes: the highest, which is i‘jāz (inimitability), and its arbiter is taste; and the lowest, which is that if it were changed from its state, it would, in the view of the eloquent, join the sounds of animals. Between them are ranks that are almost endless.
Source pages p. 591

Commentary
This passage defines balagha (eloquence) in terms of a speaker's faculty and clarifies its relationship to fasaha (fluency). It begins by stating that balagha is a faculty (malaka) enabling one to produce eloquent speech. From this definition, it deduces that balagha is more specific (akhaṣṣ) than fasaha: every eloquent person is fluent, but not every fluent person is eloquent. This distinction is important because the two terms are sometimes used interchangeably out of broadening. Then it describes the two extremes (ṭarafān) of balagha: the highest degree is i‘jāz, which is the miraculous inimitability of the Qur’an, judged by taste (dhawq); the lowest degree is speech that, if altered, would resemble animal sounds. Between them lie countless intermediate levels.

Key Terms
بلاغة
فصاحة
ملكة
إعجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fasaha and balagha are synonyms.
Correction: Sanusi shows that balagha is more specific: every baligh is fasih, but not every fasih is baligh.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, how is balagha related to fasaha?
A) Balagha is broader than fasaha.
B) Balagha is more specific than fasaha.
C) They are completely identical.
D) Balagha is a type of fasaha.

Key Takeaway
Balagha is a specific faculty of eloquence that goes beyond mere fluency, with a scale ranging from miraculous to degenerate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own speech were placed on the balagha scale, where would it fall—closer to animal sounds or to i'jāz? What would need to change for it to rise even one rank?
?
You now know that balagha is a faculty you can develop. Are you actively training your tongue to be eloquent for Allah's sake, or do you let it speak carelessly? What concrete step will you take this week to elevate your speech?
?
The highest eloquence is judged by taste (dhawq). Have you refined your spiritual taste to recognize truth and beauty in speech, or do you still prefer hollow fluency? How can you train your dhawq?
